# 冲虚至德真经鬳斋口义

*The Oral Exegesis of the Perfect Scripture of Supreme Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue from the Yanzhai Studio*

**Scripture Title:** *Oral Exegesis of the Perfect Scripture of Supreme Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue from the Yanzhai Studio*
**Author:** Lin Xiyi of the Southern Song Dynasty
**Volume:** Eight fascicles

**Base Text Source:** *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, *Dongshen* Section, *Jade Instructions* Category
**Collated Edition:** Ming Wanli-era block-print edition by He Rucheng (hereafter "Ming edition")

---

**Liezi**
Liezi’s surname was Lie, given name Yukou, a native of Zheng. He dwelt in the Zheng gardens for forty years, yet none recognized him. Initially, he served Huqiuzi, later became a disciple of Lao Shangshi, and befriended Bogaozi, advancing in the Way of these two masters. After nine years, he could ride the wind and travel.

His disciple Yan Hui asked: "Is the purpose of seeking the Way to attain wealth?"
Liezi replied: "Jie and Zhou prized profit and scorned the Way, thus they perished."

His book comprises eight chapters. Liezi was a man of the Way, and Zhuangzi frequently praised him. The present Liezi Temple in Putian, Bianliang (modern Zhengzhou), marks his former retreat. During the Tang Kaiyuan era, he was enfeoffed as the Perfected Lord of Supreme Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue, and his text was titled *The Perfect Scripture of Supreme Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue*.

---

The *New Book* was definitively arranged into eight chapters. The Left Supervisor of the Capital Waterworks and Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, Minister Xiang, stated: "The newly collated *Liezi* from the Central Secretariat comprises five fascicles. Your servant Xiang, along with Minister Can, Prefect of Changshe, collated three fascicles from the Chamberlain for Ceremonials, four from the Grand Scribe, six from your servant Xiang’s collection, and two from Minister Can’s. The combined internal and external texts totaled twenty fascicles. After removing twelve duplicate fascicles, eight were finalized. The Central Secretariat’s version was more extensive, while external texts were fewer. Chapters were disordered and scattered across various fascicles, with frequent character errors—such as *jin* (exhaust) miswritten as *jin* (advance), or *guan* (thread) as *xing* (form). Some passages in the *New Book* were fragmentary, and collation relied on the Central Secretariat’s version for finalization. All were transcribed onto bamboo slips, ready for copying."

Liezi was a man of Zheng, a contemporary of Duke Mu of Zheng, and a master of the Way. His teachings originated from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, and he was styled a Daoist. The Daoists grasp the essential and hold to the root, embracing clarity, emptiness, and non-action. In cultivating the self and engaging with others, they esteem non-contention, aligning with the Six Classics. However, the chapters *King Mu* and *Tang’s Questions* are fantastical and extravagant, unworthy of a noble person’s discourse. As for the *Power and Fate* chapter, it expounds on predetermined destiny akin to Yangzi’s teachings, prizing only unrestrained abandon—two doctrines at odds, unlike a single school’s work. Yet each has its insights, offering much of value.

During Emperor Jing of Han’s reign, the arts of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi were esteemed, and this text circulated widely. Later, it fell into obscurity, scattered among the people, with no transmitters remaining.

Moreover, it abounds in allegories akin to those of Zhuang Zhou, which is why Sima Qian the Grand Historian did not include it in his *Records* but merely appended it as a catalogue entry.

Your servant, Xiang, humbly submits this *Catalogue of the* Liezi *as Collated by the Left Director of the Capital Waterworks, the Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, Your Servant Xiang*. Presented on the renyin day of the eighth month in the third year of the Yongshi era.

*The True Scripture of Profound Virtue and Perfect Dao: Oral Exegesis from the Yan Studio, Volume One*
*Yan Studio, Lin Xiyi*

**Heavenly Auspices, Chapter One**
This chapter focuses on the principles of Heaven, and because they are precious, it is called "Auspices."

Master Liezi dwelled in the gardens of Zheng for forty years, yet no one recognized him. The lord of the state and his ministers and grandees regarded him as one of the common folk. When the state’s resources were insufficient, he prepared to "marry" into Wei. His disciples said: "Master, if you depart without a set time for return, dare we make a request? What teaching will you leave us?" The Master replied: "Have you not heard the words of Master Huqiu Zilin?" That Zheng has its Yuan gardens is like Qin having its Ju parks—see the *Zuozhuan*. Liezi dwelled beside the gardens of Zheng, hence his "marriage" to Wei. "Marriage" and "mourning" here are regional expressions. Master Huqiu Zilin was one whom Liezi served, so his disciples inquired about his teacher’s words.

Master Liezi smiled and said: "What words did Master Hu have?" Though he added: "Yet our Master once spoke to Bohun Maoren, and I overheard it from the side. Let me tell you." "What words?" implies that this cannot be transmitted through words. "Our Master" refers to Huqiu. "Maoren" was Huqiu’s friend. "Overheard from the side" means he stood beside his teacher and heard it. First saying "what words?" before recounting them suggests that the wordless teaching is more wondrous than the spoken.

His words were: "That which has life does not live; that which transforms does not change. The non-living can give life; the unchanging can effect change. The living cannot but live; the changing cannot but change—thus there is constant life and constant change. In constant life and constant change, there is no time without life, no time without change: such is yin and yang, such are the four seasons. The non-living is solitary and unique; the unchanging is cyclical recurrence. Their boundary cannot be exhausted. 'Solitary and unique'—its Dao cannot be fathomed."

That which has life arises from the non-living; that which transforms originates from the unchanging. Only the non-living and unchanging can give rise to life and effect change—this is the work of creation. The living cannot but live; the changing cannot but change—such are the myriad things. Creation is without life or change, hence it is constant life and constant change. "No time" is synonymous with "constant." The phrasing here merely elaborates. Yin and yang, the four seasons, refer to creation. The two "such" below substantiate the preceding "non-living" and "unchanging."

"Solitary and unique" resembles Laozi’s "seeming the ancestor of the myriad things" and "prior to the Lord on High." "Unique" conveys the sense of utmost loftiness and wonder, without peer. "Solitary" suggests something seemingly present yet elusive, beyond description. "Cyclical recurrence" refers to the alternation of yin and yang and the four seasons, which knows no end—hence "their boundary cannot be exhausted." The "solitary and unique" is creation: vague and indistinct, seemingly existent yet nonexistent—thus "its Dao cannot be fathomed."

The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: "The valley spirit does not die; it is called the mysterious female. The gateway of the mysterious female is called the root of Heaven and Earth. Subtle and continuous, it seems to endure; its function is inexhaustible." This is the complete text of a chapter in the *Laozi*, yet it is attributed to the *Book of the Yellow Emperor*, showing that Laozi’s teachings had a lineage, though his complete writings are no longer extant.

In the *Laozi*, Chapter Six: "Essence is full; spirit is empty." "Valley" signifies emptiness. "Valley spirit" is the spirit within emptiness. It speaks of the human spirit emerging from emptiness, hence it endures and does not die. "Mysterious" denotes the boundless and infinite; "female" denotes emptiness and insubstantiality—these two characters merely describe emptiness. Heaven and Earth emerge from this, hence it is called the root. "Subtle and continuous" conveys unceasing persistence. "Seems to endure" means seeming both present and absent. Functioning within emptiness, it is effortless and ever-present—this aligns with Laozi’s "empty yet inexhaustible, active yet ever more productive."

Master Huiweng said: "Within the most wondrous principle lies the intent of ceaseless generation." This is a fine observation, though it leans toward the discourse on nourishing life. While this chapter can serve that purpose, Laozi’s original intent was not solely focused on it. Thus, Liezi cites it to substantiate his discourse on the non-living and unchanging.

Therefore, that which gives life does not live; that which transforms does not change. Self-generating, self-transforming, self-forming, self-coloring, self-knowing, self-empowering, self-diminishing, self-ceasing—it is not that these are called generation and transformation, form and color, knowledge and power, diminution and cessation. That which gives life does not live, for it does not impose intention on life. That which transforms does not change, for it does not exert force on transformation. Throughout Heaven and Earth, all is naturally so.

"Form" and "color" refer to humans and things. "Knowledge" and "power" distinguish among humans. "Diminution" and "cessation" encompass decline and success, life and death, gain and loss. That which is naturally so is life yet not life, change yet not change, form yet not form, color yet not color, diminution yet not diminution, cessation yet not cessation. Initially without fixed names, without tangible traces—if one seeks it through fixed names and tangible traces, one errs. It does not say "without fixed names or tangible traces" but merely uses the word "called," which is strikingly apt.

Master Liezi said: "Of old, the sages followed yin and yang to unify Heaven and Earth. If the formed arises from the formless, then whence came Heaven and Earth?" This chapter first poses the question "What words?" from Master Hu, and having expounded one passage, now returns to the phrase "What words?"—these three characters carry profound meaning.

The *Zhuangzi* states: "One may speak all day yet never have spoken." This aligns with the same meaning. Thus it is said: There is the Great Simplicity (Taiyi), the Great Beginning (Taichu), the Great Origin (Taishi), and the Great Purity (Taisu). The Great Simplicity is that in which qi has not yet appeared; the Great Beginning is the inception of qi; the Great Origin is the inception of form; the Great Purity is the inception of substance.

The *Zhuangzi* further says: "There is a beginning; there is that which precedes the beginning; there is that which precedes even that which precedes the beginning." Its words are wondrously subtle. This text divides it into four names, yet it remains but the intent of *Zhuangzi*. "Form" is the general term; "substance" follows the nature of things; "qi" generates form. That in which qi has not yet appeared is the boundless giving rise to the bounded.

The *Zhuangzi* states: "Qi intermingles in the realm of the indistinct and nebulous, and there arises form." Here, another layer is added to qi. This "yi" (易) must not be taken as the *Yi* of the Confucian classics—*yi* means transformation, and transformation means change. The Great Simplicity is the great process of creation.

"Form" is the beginning, "substance" is the purity. What artisans today call "embryonic purity" (taisu) is this same "su." When qi, form, and substance are all present yet not yet separated, it is called "primordial chaos" (hunlun). "Primordial chaos" means that the myriad things are intermingled and not yet distinct. It cannot be seen, cannot be heard, cannot be grasped—hence it is called *yi*.

*Yi* has no fixed boundaries. It transforms into the One; the One transforms into the Seven; the Seven transforms into the Nine. The Nine is the culmination, after which it transforms back into the One. The One is the beginning of the transformation of form.

Having spoken of the four "Great" terms above, here qi, form, and substance are summarized together. This not only expresses the subtlety of principle but also serves as the pivot of composition. Without such a pivot, the artful turning of prose would not be revealed.

When qi, form, and substance are all present yet not yet separated, it is merely the inception of that in which qi has not yet appeared. At the inception of that in which qi has not yet appeared, one sees only its vast, undifferentiated expanse. Yet when the myriad things are said to be intermingled, it is spoken of in terms of the Three Powers (Heaven, Earth, and Humanity)—not as the "myriad things" are spoken of elsewhere.

"To follow" (xun) means "to seek." If qi has not yet appeared, then what is there to see? What is there to hear? What is there to seek?

Thus, the *Yi* (Change) is precisely the Great Change (*Taiyi*). Observing this single statement, one sees that all below—where form, qi, and substance are present—merely elucidates the two characters *Taiyi* ("Great Change"). "Without form or boundary" speaks of the absence of traces. "Transforming into the One" means that only after qi transforms does the Supreme Ultimate (*Taiji*) arise. With the Supreme Ultimate comes yin-yang and the Five Phases, hence it is said: "One transforms into seven"—yin and yang (two) together with the Five Phases make seven. The numbers of young yin and old yin are eight and six; the numbers of young yang and old yang are seven and nine. Here, the "nine" refers to the ultimate number of *Qian*. Some take seven as young yang and nine as old yang, but this is not the intent of this text. Nine transforms again into one, for it is said: when things reach their extreme, they change; what exists must return to non-existence. Non-existence gives rise to existence, hence it is said: "The One is the inception of form’s transformation." This is the ultimate principle.

The pure and light ascends to become Heaven; the turbid and heavy descends to become Earth; the harmonious qi of the mean becomes Humanity. Thus, Heaven and Earth contain essence, and the myriad things are transformed and born. Yang qi, light and pure, ascends to form Heaven; yin qi, turbid and heavy, descends to form Earth; the harmonious union of yin and yang qi becomes Humanity. "Harmonious" (*chong*) also means "balanced." The generation of things by Heaven and Earth likewise arises from the union of yin and yang’s essence, whence transformation and life proceed. Hence it is said: "Sole yin does not generate; sole yang does not complete."

Master Liezi said: "Heaven has no perfect function, the sage has no perfect ability, and the myriad things have no perfect use." Thus, Heaven’s duty is to generate and cover; Earth’s duty is to shape and bear; the sage’s duty is to teach and transform; and things fulfill their proper roles. Yet Heaven has its shortcomings, Earth has its strengths, the sage has his limitations, and things have their capacities. Why? The one who generates and covers cannot shape and bear; the one who shapes and bears cannot teach and transform; the one who teaches and transforms cannot violate what is proper; and what is proper is fixed and does not transgress its position. Thus, the Way of Heaven and Earth is either yin or yang; the teaching of the sage is either benevolence or righteousness; the proper role of the myriad things is either softness or hardness. All these follow what is proper and cannot transgress their positions.

This passage is entirely sound in its reasoning. Its main idea is that even Heaven and Earth cannot exhaust the uses of creation—how much less can humanity and things? Heaven can generate and cover things; Earth can form and bear things. Each has its capacities, and thus none possesses perfect function. The sage, dwelling between Heaven and Earth, is charged with the duty of teaching and transformation, following the proper roles of the myriad things so that each fulfills its office. "The sage has his limitations, and things have their capacities" means that the sage may have what he cannot do while things can do it; teaching and transformation cannot violate what is proper. For example, loyalty, substance, and refinement follow the times; the Nine Virtues follow their natures—all are cases where one cannot violate what is proper. The proper roles of things are each fixed: the crooked cannot be made straight, the small cannot be made large, the salty cannot be made sour, the cool cannot be made hot—these do not transgress their positions.

Yin-yang, hardness-softness, benevolence-righteousness: the *Great Commentary on the Yi* divides these among Heaven, Earth, and Humanity, while here they are divided among Heaven, Earth, the sage, and the myriad things—this too has its reason.

Thus, there are those that generate, and those that generate generation; there are those that form, and those that form formation; there are those that sound, and those that sound sound; there are those that color, and those that color color; there are those that flavor, and those that flavor flavor. What is generated by the generative perishes, yet the generative never ends; what is formed by the formative is substantial, yet the formative never exists; what is sounded by the sonorous is heard, yet the sonorous never emits; what is colored by the chromatic is manifest, yet the chromatic never appears; what is flavored by the sapid is tasted, yet the sapid never presents itself. All are the offices of non-action.

"Those that generate, form, sound, color, and flavor" refer to the myriad things in Heaven and Earth. "Those that generate generation, form formation, sound sound, color color, and flavor flavor" are creation, the office of the ruler—non-active creation. The non-generative generates what is generated; the formless forms what is formed; extending to coloring what is colored, sounding what is sounded, and flavoring what is flavored—all are the offices of creation.

The passage that follows is again singularly striking: It can be yin, it can be yang; it can be soft, it can be hard; it can be short, it can be long; it can be round, it can be square; it can give life, it can bring death; it can be hot, it can be cool; it can float, it can sink; it can be *gong*, it can be *shang*; it can emerge, it can submerge; it can be dark, it can be yellow; it can be sweet, it can be bitter; it can be rank, it can be fragrant. It has no knowledge, no ability—yet there is nothing it does not know, nothing it cannot do.

These twenty-four "can"s are merely the two words "creation’s work." The marvel of creation is that, though it seems to have no knowledge or ability, there is nothing it does not know, nothing it cannot do.

This passage is also excellent.

Master Liezi was traveling to Wei and ate by the roadside. His followers saw a century-old skull. Plucking a clump of mugwort, he pointed and said to his disciple Baifeng: "Only you and I know that it has never been born and never died. Has this exceeded nourishment? Exceeded joy?"

There are manifold transformations of beings: when a frog becomes a quail, or when it meets water and becomes duckweed, or when it encounters the scum that forms upon water and earth, it becomes the green cloak of moss. If born upon a mound, it becomes plantain. When plantain meets rich soil, it becomes crow’s-foot. The root of crow’s-foot becomes a grub, its leaves become butterflies. Butterflies, in turn, transform into insects, born beneath the stove, their form like a cast-off husk, and they are called *niao-gou-duo*. After a thousand days, *niao-gou-duo* transforms into a bird called *gan-yu-gu*. The froth from *gan-yu-gu* becomes *si-mi*. *Si-mi* becomes *shi-xi*, which in turn gives rise to *yi-lu*. *Yi-lu* generates *shi-xi-huang-xu*, and *shi-xi-huang-xu* generates *jiu-you*. *Jiu-you* generates *mao-rui*, and *mao-rui* generates *fu-quan*. This passage parallels *Zhuangzi*, though with several additional phrases.

As Liezi ate by the roadside, he saw within a clump of mugwort this withered skull, and pointing to it, he spoke to his disciple. Baifeng was his name. "Never born, never dead"—this is the state of neither birth nor death. "He" refers to the skull, while "I" refers to Liezi himself. The two phrases "exceeded nourishment" and "exceeded joy" in *Zhuangzi* read: "Is this true nourishment? Is this true joy?" The meaning runs deep. This text omits the words "if" and "I," replacing "true" with "exceeded," likely a corruption of the sound. If interpreted thus, it might suggest that this dead one, in life, indulged excessively in self-nurture or in joy. Though the logic holds, it lacks depth. Following *Zhuangzi*’s intent, it should say: "Do you truly know how one nourishes oneself in life? Do I truly know the joy of extinction after death?" Pointing to the skull, he refers to himself. To live is to eat and drink—this is nourishment; to die is to find joy in stillness. Yet here, the phrasing is inverted, playing with the brush’s artistry.

"Manifold transformations of beings" speaks of the ceaseless generation of things in heaven and earth, each kind distinct, yet all traced to the most minute origins—not to illustrate the great through the small, but to show that the great differs not from the small. This conveys the idea of no smallness, no greatness, no nobility, no baseness. The meaning is precisely this, yet the brilliance of the writing transcends the ages. Within order, there is disorder; within disorder, order—like watching drifting clouds or a skein of wild geese, like gazing upon solitary peaks or broken slopes, the more one reads, the finer it becomes. The additions in this text, however, make it inferior to *Zhuangzi*. "When a frog becomes a quail" refers to the frog’s transformation into a quail, though *Zhuangzi* omits these four characters, which also disrupt the flow of the following line. "Scum" refers to the film of dust and grime upon water, the first growth of algae not yet fully formed, with threads of silk entwined—though the thing itself is most minute. "The green cloak of moss" is simply green algae. "The meeting of water and earth" refers to the water’s edge where it clings to the shore, where such growth is typically thick and abundant, hence called a "cloak." These two lines describe algae, then shift to speak of mounds. "Mounds" are the high places in fields and wilds. "Plantain" is the coin-leaf herb. "Rich soil" is manure and earth. When coin-leaf grows in rich soil, it transforms into crow’s-foot grass. The root of crow’s-foot becomes a grub, while its leaves become butterflies. The grub is the scarab beetle. *Xu* is another name for butterflies. The addition of this line beneath "butterflies" is particularly striking. What follows describes the transformations of insects born beneath the stove, which then become creatures called *niao-gou-duo*. Soft and without skin or shell, they resemble a shed husk, much like the silkworm. The *niao-gou-duo* insect then transforms into a bird. *Gan-yu-gu* is the name of this bird. The froth from its beak becomes *si-mi*. *Si-mi* is an insect. *Shi-xi* are midges. *Yi-lu* and *huang-xu* are also insect names. Here, three instances of "midge" appear, likely errors in transmission. *Jiu-you*, *mao-rui*, and *fu-quan* are also insect names. *Zhuangzi* omits several characters here, intending to convey that the transformations of the myriad things are endless and inexhaustible.

Sheep’s liver transforms into earthworms; horse blood into will-o’-the-wisps; human blood into marsh fires. The sparrowhawk becomes the goshawk, the goshawk becomes the cuckoo. The cuckoo, in time, reverts to the sparrowhawk. Swallows become clams; field mice become quails; rotting gourds become fish; old leeks become amaranth; aged rams become gibbons; fish roe becomes *gu* insects. The beast *dan-yuan* conceives of itself and is called *lei*. The marsh bird *yi* is born from gazing and is called the osprey. The purely female is named *da-yao*; the purely male is named *zhi-feng*. These lines do not appear in *Zhuangzi*, and the repeated use of "also" disrupts the flow, though they too describe the transformations of things. As in the *Monthly Ordinances*, sparrows become clams, and hawks become turtledoves—such are the natural principles of heaven and earth, inevitable occurrences. An aged ram becoming a gibbon is like an old mouse becoming a bat. *Dan-yuan* is a beast recorded in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*, resembling a leopard but with fur. "Conceiving of itself" means it is female without males. Some say the same of the women’s kingdom in the sea. *Lei* is its name. The osprey is the bird *Zhuangzi* describes: the male cries facing the wind, the female cries with the wind, and they conceive by gazing. *Da-yao* refers to creatures like turtles and softshells, purely female without males, while bees are purely male without females. *Zhi* means small. Bees in the hive transform by incantation, their tails bearing stings—only the queen lacks them.

Some say: this creature takes the multitude of yang to honor yin—yin is its sovereign. The yearning scholar remains unwed yet stirs response; the longing maiden knows no husband yet conceives. Hou Ji was born from a giant’s footprint; Yi Yin was born from the hollow mulberry—these four lines speak of transformations among humans. Jue Zhao was born from dampness; vinegar gnats arise from wine—these two lines speak of common transformations in foodstuffs. The *yangxi* grass pairs with the unyielding shoot; aged bamboo gives rise to the *qingning*. The *qingning* begets the *cheng*; the *cheng* begets the horse; the horse begets man; man in time enters the mechanism. All things emerge from the mechanism, all things return to the mechanism. From this point onward, the passage aligns with *Zhuangzi*. Viewed through *Zhuangzi*, having expounded the preceding section, it then ties off with the most bizarre conclusion—this is where it aims to shock the vulgar, not a statement of truth. Now, with the addition of the yearning scholar and maiden, the text becomes muddled.

*Yangxi* is the name of a grass. Grasses resembling bamboo that do not produce edible shoots are called *bugou*; aged bamboo that does produce edible shoots is edible, but this is not. *Qingning* is an insect; *cheng* is also an insect. *Ma* (horse) is likewise a grass, such as what is now called *machicai* (horse’s-teeth vegetable) or *malancao* (indigo plant). *Ren* (man) is also a grass, such as what is now called *renshen* (ginseng) or *renmianzi* (dracontomelon). Clearly employing many plant names, it then speaks of the characters for "horse" and "man," deliberately crafting deceptive and strange terms. Those who have sought to explain this before have all admitted uncertainty—countless generations have been duped, unable to see through it. The transformations of the myriad things, endlessly arising and ceaselessly born—what is there that does not exist?

Entering the mechanism speaks of returning to annihilation. To exit the mechanism and enter the mechanism is to exit and enter death and life—this is the transmission of fire, yet one knows not its end.

In the *"Utmost Joy"* chapter, I have long suspected that the *Liezi* is not a complete work, and this passage makes it all the clearer. Since the Qin dynasty, many books have been scattered and lost; those recovered later emerged only after being sought, obtained at different times, preserved in varying quantities, and only settled after collation. By the time of collation, errors and admixtures had already occurred. Then came the calamities of the Central Plains under the Jin, and books were lost again. When they resurfaced in the south, many forgeries became intermingled, as with the *Guan Yinzi*. What is good is entirely good; what is adulterated is entirely adulterated. The first several passages at the beginning of this book’s first chapter are exquisite, beyond doubt, but the middle sections are inevitably mixed with later interpolations. Yet the refinement or crudeness of its text is still easily discerned.

The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* states: *"When form moves, it does not generate form but generates shadow; when sound moves, it does not generate sound but generates echo. Where non-being does not generate non-being, it generates being."* Form must come to an end. Do heaven and earth end? They end together with me. Do they advance toward an end? I do not know. The Dao ends where there was originally no beginning; it advances where there was originally no duration. That which is born returns to non-birth; that which has form returns to formlessness. Yet the non-born is not originally non-born; the formless is not originally formless. *"When form moves, it generates shadow; when sound moves, it generates echo"*—these two lines are well put, for their meaning is to illustrate how non-being generates being. All that is generated from being arises from non-being, and thus all being must return to non-being. That which has form must have an end. Heaven and earth are also form—how could they not end together with me? Yet if one believes that heaven and earth end in annihilation, this is something I cannot know. Thus it says: *"Do they advance toward an end? I do not know."* "Advance" means "end"; to take "end" as "advance" is a corruption of the pronunciation. If there was originally no beginning, then there is no end; if there was originally no duration, then there is no annihilation. "No duration" means ceaseless transformation. That which is born must return to non-birth, for non-birth is the source of birth. That which has form must return to formlessness, for formlessness is the source of form. If something was originally non-born, then it has no name of non-birth; if something was originally formless, then it has no name of formlessness. To call it non-birth or formlessness is already to depart from its truth. Thus it says: *"The non-born is not originally non-born; the formless is not originally formless."*

That which is born is by principle that which must end. What must end cannot but end, just as what is born cannot but be born. Yet to desire eternal life and to halt the end is to be deluded by calculations. Spirit is the portion allotted by heaven; bones and flesh are the portion allotted by earth. That which belongs to heaven is clear and disperses; that which belongs to earth is turbid and congeals. When spirit departs from form, each returns to its true nature—this is called *gui* (ghost). *Gui* means "to return," to return to one’s true dwelling.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"When spirit enters its gate and bones return to their root, where then do I remain?"* This passage directly expounds the principle of life and death, and its explanation is indeed clear. Death and life are the constant principle, yet those who covet life constantly seek to preserve it. *"To halt"* means "to stop"; *"to halt the end"* is to desire to stop it from ending. *"Deluded by calculations"* means being misled by the reckonings of longevity and brevity. Spirit belongs to heaven; bones and flesh belong to earth. *"Perfect enlightenment"* refers to the doctrine of the four great elements. *"Portion"* means "that which is allotted." *"Enters its gate"* means returning to the place from which it originally emerged. *"Returns to its root"* means returning to the place where it originally began. Once spirit and bones have each reverted to their origins, where then does the present "I" remain? This is what the *Perfect Enlightenment Sutra* means when it asks: *"Where now does my dharma-body abide?"*

Master Zhu Xi’s claim that the Buddhists plagiarized this idea is, I fear, unfounded. Since ancient times, there has naturally existed such discourse in the world—Yuan Rang was a figure of this kind. The Buddha emerged from the West; how could he have plagiarized this? To denounce it so severely is unjust.

From birth to death, the great transformations of human life unfold in four stages: infancy, youth and prime, old age, and death.

In infancy, qi is concentrated and the will is unified—harmony reaches its utmost. Nothing harms it, and no virtue surpasses it. In youth and prime, blood and qi surge and overflow, desires and thoughts arise in abundance. External things assail it, and virtue thus declines. In old age, desires and thoughts soften, the body prepares to rest, and nothing takes precedence. Though it does not yet match the wholeness of infancy, it surpasses the state of youth and prime. In death, one comes to rest, returning to the ultimate.

Before blood and qi settle, and after their vigor has waned, the sage divides life into three segments, while here it is divided into four. *Zhuangzi* says: *"The great clod burdens me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death."* This, too, divides life into four segments.

Laozi’s depiction of the harmony of infancy is most apt. The surging of blood and qi is what the sage calls "vigorous prime." The rise of desires and thoughts is the labor of life. To desire wealth and honor is desire; to scheme ahead and calculate behind is thought. When these fill and arise uncontrollably, external things assail the heart, and the harmony and virtue of infancy wane. In old age, though desires and thoughts remain, one can no longer exert oneself. *Zhuangzi* calls this "eased by old age," while here it is termed "the body preparing to rest"—the meaning is the same, though the words differ. *"Nothing takes precedence"* means one cannot compete with external things. Naturally receding, though not yet equal to infancy’s state of harming nothing, it is already far removed from the days of youth and prime, when things assailed it. When form and qi are exhausted, one returns to their source—this is what *Zhuangzi* means by *"rests me with death."* *"Ultimate"* refers to the extremity of the Great Ultimate, the beginning of all transformations of form, as previously mentioned.

Confucius once traveled on Mount Tai and saw Rong Qigi walking in the wilds of Cheng, clad in a deerskin robe with a rope belt, playing the zither and singing. Confucius asked him: *"What is the source of your joy, sir?"* He replied: *"My joys are many. Heaven gives birth to the myriad things, and man is the most noble—this is my first joy. Among men, the male is honored and the female is lowly, and I have been born a man—this is my second joy. Some do not live to see the sun and moon, never leaving swaddling clothes, but I have already reached ninety years—this is my third joy. Poverty is the constant lot of the scholar, and death is the end of all men. Abiding in constancy and meeting one’s end—what is there to grieve?"* Confucius said: *"Excellent! He knows how to console himself."* Rong was his surname, Qigi his given name. He wore a deerskin robe and a rope belt. Of heaven and earth’s nature, man is most noble among things. Among humankind, the male is more honored than the female. The three joys are a discourse close to human sentiment. This passage instructs on the principles of poverty, wealth, life, and death, and thus presents such a parable. One who can console himself does so not by insight into the Dao but by reasoning through the patterns of things to find ease. Du Fu’s poem—*"On the riverbank, a small hall where kingfishers nest; by the fields, a high tomb where qilin stand. Carefully pondering the patterns of things, one must seek joy—why let floating fame bind this body?"*—captures the meaning of this passage.

Lin Lei was nearly a hundred years old. In late spring, he donned a fur robe, gleaned fallen grain from old fields, and walked while singing. Confucius, passing through Wei, saw him in the wilds and said to his disciples: *"That old man may be worth speaking to. Go and inquire."* Zigong volunteered and met him at the edge of the ridge. Facing him, he sighed and said: *"Sir, do you have no regrets, walking and singing while gleaning grain?"* Lin Lei continued without pause, neither stopping his song nor his steps. Zigong pressed him repeatedly, until at last he looked up and replied: *"What should I regret?"* Zigong said: *"In your youth, you did not cultivate conduct; in your prime, you did not strive for success; in old age, you have no wife or children, and death approaches—what joy is there in gleaning grain and singing as you walk?"* Lin Lei laughed and said: *"The source of my joy is something all men possess, yet they turn it into sorrow. In youth, I did not cultivate conduct; in my prime, I did not strive for success—thus I have lived so long. In old age, I have no wife or children, and death approaches—thus I can be so joyful."* Zigong said: *"Longevity is what men desire; death is what they detest. Why do you find joy in death?"* Lin Lei said: *"Death and life are but a going and a returning. Thus, one who dies here—how do we know they are not born there? So I know they are not the same, and how do I know that busily striving for life is not delusion? How do I know that my death now is not better than my past life?"* Zigong did not grasp his meaning and returned to report to the Master. The Master said: *"I knew he was worth speaking to—indeed, so! Yet what he has attained is not yet complete."*

*"Late spring"* refers to the season. *"Walking while singing"* means singing as he went. *"In youth, he did not cultivate conduct"* means he did not study and left no reputation among men. *"In his prime, he did not strive for success"* means he did not contend for fame or profit in the world. Zigong mocked him for this, but Lin Lei took it as the reason for his longevity: had he labored body and mind to vie for external fame and profit, he would have died young. Zigong and Lin Lei are parabolic figures. *"Death and life are but a going and a returning"* means that from life to death, it is like a journey that must return. *"One who dies here—how do we know they are not born there?"* This is akin to the Buddhist teaching of present and future lives, past and future selves. *"I know they are not the same"* means how do we know this life is not better than the next, or the future self not better than the past?

The death of today surpasses not the life of yesterday—this aligns with *Zhuangzi*’s teaching of the feeble mourner who knows not the return. *"One who attains it yet does not exhaust it"* means he grasps the principle of life and death but has not plumbed its subtleties.

The *Liezi* reveres Confucius throughout its text, thus often borrowing his name in its parables—though whether this truly originates from Liezi remains unknown. Zilu, weary of study, said to Zhongni: *"I wish to rest."* Zhongni replied: *"In life, there is no rest."* Zilu asked: *"Then shall I, Si, have no place to rest?"* Zhongni said: *"You shall. Behold the grave mound—lofty as a tower, smooth as a stewpot, swelling as a burial mound, bulging as a tripod. There you will know your rest."* Zilu exclaimed: *"Death is vast indeed! The noble man finds repose there; the petty man is subdued there."*

Zhongni said: *"Si, you have understood. Men all know the joy of life but not its suffering; they know the weariness of age but not its ease; they know the dread of death but not its rest."* One weary of study has studied without grasping its essence. Having wearied the mind for so long, weariness naturally arises. *"Rest"* means cessation. *"I see his advance, but never his halt"*—this is the case. Zilu, weary of study, sought a place to cease, and the Master thus told him that *"in life, there is no rest."*

Here, *Liezi* borrows the names of sages and the discourse on advance and cessation to clarify the principle of life and death. *"In life, there is no rest"* means that as long as form exists, life must labor. When can one rest? Only in death is rest possible. Zilu, not yet enlightened, pressed further with his question of whether rest had no place, and the Master answered with the matter of graves and mounds. *"Lofty as a tower, smooth as a stewpot, swelling as a burial mound, bulging as a tripod"*—all describe their rising forms. The noble man finds his own rest there; though the petty man’s heart clings without cease, even he cannot but submit when he reaches this.

Though this passage was composed for those who cling to life and dread death, the Chan school later spoke of "dying to the heart" and of "the dead man who yet lives"—here lies a profound meaning, not merely concerning life and death.

Master Yan said: *"How excellent, the death of the ancients!"* The virtuous find rest therein; the unvirtuous submit. Death is the culmination of power. The ancients called the dead *"those who return."* If the dead are called *"those who return,"* then the living are *"those who journey."* To journey without knowing return is to lose one’s home. One man loses his home, and the world condemns him; when the whole world loses its home, none know to condemn it.

There are those who leave their native land, abandon their six relations, forsake their family estate, and wander the four directions without returning—what manner of men are these? The world must call them reckless and dissolute. And there are those who prize worldly eminence, boast of their skills, cultivate fame, and vaunt themselves before the world without self-knowledge—what manner of men are these? The world must deem them wise and cunning. Both are lost, yet the world approves one and rejects the other. Only the sage knows whom to approve and whom to reject.

The virtuous and the unvirtuous are but the noble man and the petty man. *"Culmination"* means return—virtue is only settled in death, which is the meaning of *"returning to the root and reverting to the real."* Thus, the great matter of life and death is used to illustrate this.

*"Losing one’s home"* is the discourse on weakness and loss. *"Prize"* means to value; *"eminence"* refers to form; *"worldly"* refers to life. These three characters are all corrupted by phonetic transmission—it should read *"valuing form and life."* To value form and life is to hold the body as precious. The world deems recklessness wrong, so it rejects such men and instead embraces the wise and cunning, not realizing that wisdom and cunning are also wrong. The sage’s approval and rejection are guided by the Dao, hence it is said: *"know whom to approve and whom to reject."*

Someone asked Liezi: *"Why do you value emptiness?"* Liezi replied: *"Emptiness has no value."* To value emptiness is to esteem it; *"has no value"* means that even the esteem for emptiness is absent—what then is there to esteem?

Liezi said: *"It is not the name. Nothing knows stillness like emptiness."* Stillness and emptiness find their proper place; grasping and giving lose theirs.

When affairs shatter the uncarved block and give rise to the performance of benevolence and righteousness, they cannot be restored. What is not its true name—this means that once a name exists, it is no longer the true name. As the *Laozi* says: *"That which can be named is not the constant name."* To speak of emptiness and stillness is to leave no trace, and thus no name. Without name or trace, one finds their proper place; but the moment there is grasping and giving, distinction and division, one loses it.

The great Dao is broken into fragments, and only then do the names of benevolence and righteousness arise. *"Shattering the uncarved block"* means fragmentation—this describes how the scholars of today, having shattered the great Dao, take the seven virtues as their playthings, so that the wind of true purity and simplicity can never return. *"Performing benevolence and righteousness"* is like what people today call *"manipulating texts and toying with laws."*

Zhou Xiong said: *"The turning never ceases; heaven and earth shift imperceptibly—who notices?"* Thus, what is diminished in one place is filled in another; what is completed here is lost there. Loss and gain, completion and depletion follow the world and follow death. Coming and going connect without pause, with no gap to perceive—who notices? For qi does not advance all at once, nor does form deplete in an instant; one does not sense its completion, nor its depletion. It is like a person from youth to old age—appearance, complexion, wisdom, and bearing change day by day, yet skin, nails, and hair fall away with the passing world, no different from infancy when there was no pause in change. The interval is imperceptible; only afterward does one know.

Zhou Xiong is the borrowed name of an ancient worthy. Between heaven and earth, the turning never ceases. Heaven completes one revolution each day; earth moves in fourfold ascent and descent, never pausing for a single breath. Yet humans dwell within it unaware, like one sitting in a boat—though the boat moves, the passenger does not know. Who can perceive the shifting of heaven and earth? *"Imperceptible"* means it cannot be seen. Things diminish and fill, complete and deplete—all proceed imperceptibly between heaven and earth, and none notice. Life and death come and go in endless cycles, connected without interruption, with no gap to perceive.

Rivers form through the passage of water; their currents flow ceaselessly away. Generations form through the passage of people; their lives drift slowly toward dusk. What person does not renew with the age? What age can remain unchanged by people? This is precisely the meaning.

*"Following the world"* means following life. Cold and heat come and go gradually, so it is said: *"qi does not advance all at once."* High banks become valleys, deep valleys become hills—even the smallest thing or vessel depletes gradually, so it is said: *"form does not deplete in an instant."* Only because it is not sudden does one fail to notice. *"Not sudden"* means not abrupt. The same is true of a person from youth to old age—there is no visible gap, and one only knows when the time arrives. Thus it is said: *"The interval is imperceptible; only afterward does one know."*

*"Bearing"* refers to physical form; *"wisdom"* refers to one’s views. In the state of Qi, there was a man who worried that heaven and earth would collapse, leaving him nowhere to dwell, and so he abandoned sleep and food. Another, concerned for his worry, went to enlighten him, saying: *"Heaven is but accumulated qi—there is no place without qi. You bend and stretch, breathe and move, spending all day within it. Why fear collapse?"* The man replied: *"If heaven is but accumulated qi, should not the sun, moon, and stars fall?"* The enlightener said: *"The sun, moon, and stars are merely luminous accumulations within qi. Even if they fell, they could do no harm."* The man asked: *"What of the earth’s ruin?"* The enlightener replied: *"Earth is but accumulated clods, filling the four directions. There is no place without clods. You tread and step, spending all day upon it. Why fear its ruin?"* The man was suddenly at ease and overjoyed. The enlightener, too, was suddenly at ease and overjoyed.

Changluzi heard this and laughed, saying: *"The rainbow and the secondary bow, clouds and mist, wind and rain, the four seasons—these are the forms taken by accumulated qi in the heavens. Mountains and peaks, rivers and seas, metals and stones, water and fire—these are the forms taken by accumulated matter on the earth. Knowing that they are but accumulated qi and accumulated clods, how can one say they will not perish? The heavens and earth are but a tiny thing within the void, the greatest of all things within the bounded. That they are hard to exhaust, hard to fathom—this is certainly so. That they are hard to measure, hard to comprehend—this is certainly so. To worry over their ruin is indeed too distant a concern; to say they will not ruin is also incorrect. The heavens and earth cannot but perish, and when they do, they will return to ruin. When their time of ruin comes, how can one not worry?*

Liezi heard this and laughed, saying: *"To say the heavens and earth will perish is also mistaken; to say they will not perish is also mistaken. Whether they perish or not, I cannot know. Nevertheless, that is one state, this is another. Thus, the living do not know death, the dead do not know life; the coming do not know the going, the going do not know the coming. Whether they perish or not, why should I trouble my heart over it? Bending and stretching, breathing and moving, one harmonizes with the qi in the heavens—thus, man is also a natural part of accumulated qi. The light of the sun, moon, and stars also arises from this qi. 'Only if' means 'but if' or 'even if.' 'The four voids' refers to the space beyond the four directions. 'Hesitant steps' means to linger. 'Treading and trampling' means to walk upon. This says that aside from the great void, all within is accumulated clods. How can one say they will not perish? For what accumulates must also disperse—how can it not perish? The meaning of this passage is that the heavens are originally accumulated qi, the earth originally accumulated clods, and thus they must have a time of ruin. Therefore, these words are set forth to describe it. The *Book of Changes* says: 'If Qian and Kun perish, the Dao cannot be seen.' The sages also had this idea, though they did not speak of it. Within the great void, formless and boundless, the heavens and earth are but a tiny thing. Yet from man’s perspective, seeing things as substantial, they appear the greatest within the bounded. These two lines are also well put. 'Hard to exhaust, hard to fathom; hard to measure, hard to comprehend'—this means they cannot be known by man. The final turn, however, says: 'The coming do not know the going, the going do not know the coming.' This is to say that those who study the Dao should not trouble their hearts over being and non-being, going and coming. Today’s Chan school emerges from this. The later turn: Shun asked Zheng, 'Can the Dao be possessed?' Zheng said, 'Your body is not your own—how can you possess the Dao?' Shun said, 'If my body is not my own, then whose is it?' Zheng said, 'It is the form entrusted by heaven and earth. Life is not your own—it is the harmony entrusted by heaven and earth. Nature and destiny are not your own—they are the compliance entrusted by heaven and earth. Children and grandchildren are not your own—they are the shedding entrusted by heaven and earth. Thus, when you walk, you do not know where you go; when you dwell, you do not know what you hold; when you eat, you do not know why. Heaven and earth are the forceful yang—qi. How can they be possessed?' 'Entrusted' means gathered. The four great elements temporarily combine to form this body, thus it is called 'entrusted form.' Yin and yang harmonize to give birth to the myriad things, thus life is called 'entrusted harmony.' Compliance with principle—nature and destiny are within me, yet they are the principle of creation, thus called 'entrusted compliance.' Generations succeed one another like the molting of cicadas, thus children and grandchildren are called 'entrusted shedding.' 'Do not know what you hold' means having no attachment. 'Forceful yang qi' is the qi of life. The active is yang, and man’s walking, dwelling, and eating are all movements of this qi—none of it is one’s own. The *Perfect Enlightenment Sutra* says, 'Now this illusory body—where should it be?' This is the same idea. This passage is identical to the *Zhuangzi*’s 'Knowledge Wanders North' chapter."*

The character *zhēng* in the *Zhuangzi* is written as *chéng*—this must be a scribal error. Yet to call it *chéng* is also a figurative expression.

In Qi, the Guo clan was immensely wealthy; in Song, the Xiang clan was desperately poor. The Xiang traveled from Song to Qi to seek their method. The Guo said to them: "I am skilled in thievery. When I first began to steal, I had enough after one year, abundance after two, and great plenty after three. From then on, my bounty extended to the towns and villages."

The Xiang rejoiced greatly, understanding the words of thievery but not its Way. He then scaled walls and bored through houses, seizing whatever his hands and eyes could reach. Before long, he was caught with stolen goods and punished, his ancestral property confiscated.

The Xiang, believing the Guo had deceived him, went to reproach him. The Guo said: "How did you go about stealing?" The Xiang described what he had done. The Guo exclaimed: "Alas! Have you lost the Way of thievery to such an extent? Now I will tell you.

I have heard that Heaven has its seasons, Earth its advantages. I steal Heaven and Earth’s seasons and advantages—the soaking nourishment of clouds and rain, the products of mountains and marshes—to grow my grain, cultivate my crops, build my walls, and construct my dwellings. On land, I steal from the birds and beasts; in water, I steal from the fish and turtles—nothing is not stolen. Yet grain, crops, timber, birds, beasts, fish, and turtles are all born of Heaven. How can they be mine? Thus, though I steal from Heaven, I incur no misfortune.

Gold, jade, treasures, grain, silk, and wealth are what men accumulate—how can they be Heaven’s gifts? If you steal these and are punished, whom can you blame?"

The Xiang was deeply perplexed, thinking the Guo was further deceiving him. He encountered Master Dongguo and asked him about it. Master Dongguo said: "Is not your very body nothing but theft? You steal the harmony of yin and yang to form your life and sustain your body—how much more so with external things? Are they not also theft?

Truly, Heaven and Earth and the myriad things do not exist apart from one another. To measure and claim them as one’s own is delusion. The Guo’s theft is the public Way, thus he incurs no misfortune; your theft is private desire, thus you are punished. Whether public or private, both are theft. Whether without public or private, both are theft. Public and private are the virtue of Heaven and Earth.

One who knows the virtue of Heaven and Earth—what is theft? What is not theft? *Wei ji shi* means not yet able to reckon the seasons. *Xian ju* refers to the property accumulated by previous generations. *Miu ji* means to deceive oneself. *Wang er yuan zhi* means to go and reproach him.

The seasons and advantages are Heaven’s timing and Earth’s benefits. *Pang run* means soaking moisture. Grain, crops, timber, birds, beasts, fish, and turtles are all external things born of Heaven—even the yin and yang of one’s body, how can they be one’s own? They too are made by Heaven and Earth.

*Cheng* means truly so. That Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things do not exist apart means that all things arise from Heaven and Earth, and not one can be separated from them. *Ren* is the same as *ren* (to recognize)—to claim them as one’s own is the delusion of folly.

The meaning of this chapter is that man exists between Heaven and Earth, all stealing from what belongs to Heaven and Earth to sustain his life. Thus, it is expressed in such terms, forming a heterodox teaching. From Heaven’s seasons and Earth’s advantages to birds, beasts, fish, and turtles—all belong to Heaven and Earth, and man steals and uses them. The sage says: "Use Heaven’s Way, divide Earth’s benefits." The *Liezi* here embellishes its words. Liu Zihou’s *Tian Shuo* analogy also originates from this.

The final turn of thought is the same as the previous section. The public Way is what all men share; the private Way is not shared by all. In human judgment, there is public and private; in the virtue of Heaven and Earth, there is no public or private. What is public is so of itself, also made by Heaven and Earth; what is private is so of itself, also made by Heaven and Earth. Viewed from the virtue of Heaven and Earth, both theft and non-theft are matters of intention.

This meaning suggests that to love good and hate evil as if arising from intention is to make even good into evil. The *Laozi* says: "When all under Heaven know beauty as beauty, ugliness is already present." This is precisely the same idea. Such passages do not seem to belong to the original *Liezi* text.

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Yanzhai’s Exegesis, Scroll One* ends here.

Variant: The Ming edition has *zai* instead of [omitted character].
Variant: The Ming edition has *shan* (mountain) instead of *shou* (beast).
Variant: *Yao* was originally *da* (great), emended according to the Ming edition.
Variant: *Changshi* in the Ming edition is *rimu* (daily passage).

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Yanzhai’s Oral Exegesis, Scroll Two*
By Yanzhai Lin Xiang

Emperor Huangdi, Scroll Two

In the fifteenth year of his reign, the Yellow Emperor rejoiced that all under Heaven bore him allegiance. He nurtured his proper destiny, delighted his ears and eyes, and gratified his nose and mouth. Yet his complexion grew sallow and dull, his flesh withered, and his five faculties dimmed in confusion.

Another fifteen years passed, and he grew anxious that all under Heaven remained untamed. He exhausted his wisdom and intelligence, exerted his strength of mind, and toiled for the common people. Again his complexion grew sallow and dull, his brows furrowed, and his five faculties dimmed in confusion.

Then the Yellow Emperor sighed deeply and exclaimed: "My excesses have gone too far! The harm of nurturing oneself alone is thus; the harm of governing the myriad things is thus."

Thereupon he relinquished all affairs of state, abandoned his palace chambers, dismissed his attendants, removed the bells and chimes, reduced his kitchen provisions, and withdrew to dwell in seclusion in the Hall of the Great Court. There he purified his heart and disciplined his form, abstaining from government matters for three months.

One day, as he slept, he dreamed of wandering in the land of Huaxu. The land of Huaxu lies west of Yan Prefecture and north of Tai Prefecture, countless thousands of miles from the state of Qi—so distant that no boat, carriage, or foot could reach it, and one could only journey there in spirit.

In that land, there were no rulers or leaders; all was spontaneous and self-so.

The people there had no cravings or desires; all was spontaneous and self-so.
They knew not the joy of life, nor the dread of death, and thus suffered no premature demise.
They knew not attachment to self, nor estrangement from others, and thus harbored no love or resentment.
They knew not defiance, nor compliance, and thus perceived no gain or loss.
All things were met without fondness or regret, without fear or taboo.
They entered water yet did not drown; entered fire yet did not burn.
Strikes and blows brought no pain; pricks and prods caused no itch or ache.
They tread upon emptiness as if walking on solid ground, and slept in the void as if resting in a forest.
Clouds and mist did not obstruct their sight—obstruction and clarity were one to their eyes.
Thunder and lightning did not disturb their hearing; beauty and ugliness did not unsettle their hearts.
Mountains and valleys did not hinder their steps; their movement was but spirit alone.

When the Yellow Emperor awoke, he felt a profound contentment. He summoned Tian Lao, Li Mu, and Tai Shan Ji, and addressed them, saying: "For three months I have dwelt in seclusion, purifying my heart and disciplining my form, seeking the Dao of nurturing the self and ordering the world, yet I found no method. Exhausted, I slept, and dreamed thus. Now I know the supreme Dao cannot be sought through human sentiment."

I have come to know it, I have attained it, yet I cannot convey it to you.

Twenty-eight years later, the realm knew great peace, nearly resembling the land of Huaxu, and the Emperor ascended in transcendence. The people mourned him for over two hundred years without cease.

This speaks of the land of Huaxu as sharing the same intent as the land of established virtue in the *Zhuangzi*’s "Mountain Trees" chapter. It describes how the Yellow Emperor’s governance of the world began with intention and ended in non-intention, began with action and ended in non-action.

*Zhengming* refers to the nature of life—taking nature as the norm, though the pronunciation is corrupted. *"Jiran"* describes his skin as parched and darkened, emaciated. *"Hunran"* speaks of his five emotions in disarray, bewildered and dazed. The five emotions are joy, anger, sorrow, happiness, and desire. *"Zan"* means to join in sighing. *"Yin yi"* suggests excess like water spreading unchecked; commentators take *yin* as meaning "profound." *"Zhi shi"* refers to attendants or servants. *"Xuan"* is a bell rack. *"Dating"* is akin to the imperial palace. *"Fu xing"* is what modern people call "absorbing qi."

The *Huainanzi* states: due west lies Yan Province, and to the northwest lies Tai Province. This speaks of lands beyond the Nine Provinces, much as the Buddha speaks of the western lands beyond Jambudvīpa.

*"Si"* means separation. Qi State is the Central Land. *"Si Qi guo"* means a distance of ten thousand *li* from the Central Land. *"Ziran"* means non-intention. *"No favor or aversion, no compliance or defiance"* describes a heart without attachment or rejection. *"Entering water without drowning, entering fire without burning"* means no circumstance fails to bring self-possession. *"Striking without pain, prodding without itching"* means that though he had form, it was as if he had none. *"Obstruction and clarity were one."* *"Shen xing"* means his movement left no trace.

Tian Lao, Li Mu, and Tai Shan Ji were three ministers of the Yellow Emperor. *"Deng jia"* means "ascending in transcendence"; *jia* should be read as *xia*. The *Zhuangzi* contains many such ideas. Some say that in comparing *Liezi* to *Zhuangzi*, the former surpasses the latter—though this may not be so.

The Mount Lieguye rises amidst the seas and rivers. Upon its summit dwells a divine being who inhales the wind and drinks the dew, partaking not of the five grains. Her heart is like a deep spring, her form like a maiden’s. She clings not, nor does she cherish; immortals and sages serve as her ministers. She knows no fear, nor does she rage; the guileless and sincere act as her envoys. She bestows no favors, yet all things are self-sufficient; she gathers nothing, yet she herself lacks nothing. Yin and yang remain ever in harmony, sun and moon ever bright. The four seasons proceed in constant order, wind and rain in perfect balance. Birth and nurture occur in due season, the harvest ever abundant. The land suffers no pestilence, the people no premature death or misfortune. Creatures bear no blemish or disease, and ghosts emit no uncanny resonance.

This passage shares much in spirit with the *Zhuangzi*, depicting only the governance of non-action. *"Her heart like a deep spring"* means it is like still water. *"She clings not"* means she does not bend or twist, akin to *"she cherishes not."* *"She knows no fear, nor does she rage"* describes her harmony. *"She bestows no favors"* means she gives nothing. *"She gathers nothing"* means she takes nothing. Since all things are self-sufficient, she herself lacks nothing. *"Lacks"* means deficiency. *"Birth and nurture"* refers to the procreation of birds and beasts. *"Pestilence"* means epidemic illness. *"Creatures bear no blemish or disease"* means they suffer no affliction. *"Ghosts emit no uncanny resonance"* means there is no strange or eerie phenomenon. All spectral anomalies and omens are the mischief of ghosts. This aligns with the *"Free and Easy Wandering"* chapter of the *Zhuangzi*.

Liezi studied under Master Lao Shang and befriended Bo Gaozi. Having mastered the Way of these two, he rode the wind home. Yin Sheng, hearing of this, followed Liezi and dwelled with him for months without returning to his lodging. Seizing an opportunity, he entreated Liezi ten times for his teachings, yet ten times Liezi refused to instruct him. Yin Sheng, aggrieved, sought to depart, but Liezi gave no command. Yin Sheng withdrew.

Months later, his resolve unbroken, he returned to follow Liezi again. Liezi asked, *"Why do you come and go so frequently?"* Yin Sheng replied, *"Before, I, Zhang Dai, made requests of you, but you would not teach me, leaving me resentful. Now, once more, I find myself thus—hence my return."* Liezi said, *"Formerly, I took you for one of understanding, but now your vulgarity has reached this point? Very well, I shall tell you what I learned from my master. Since I began serving my master and befriending such men, after three years, my heart dared not entertain right or wrong, my mouth dared not speak of profit or harm. Only then did my master deign to glance at me. After five years, my heart reversed its thoughts of right and wrong, my mouth reversed its words of profit and harm. Only then did my master begin to relax his countenance and smile. After seven years, I followed my heart’s inclinations, yet it harbored no right or wrong; I followed my mouth’s utterances, yet it spoke no profit or harm. Only then did my master draw me to sit beside him on the same mat. After nine years, whether my heart wandered or my mouth spoke, I knew not whether my own right and wrong, profit and harm existed, nor whether those of others did. I knew not whether my master was my teacher or those men my friends. Within and without, all had advanced. Thereafter, my eyes were as ears, my ears as nose, my nose as mouth—all were the same. My heart congealed, my form dissolved, flesh and bone fused as one. I no longer sensed the body’s support or the feet’s tread, drifting east and west with the wind like a withered leaf or dry husk. Did the wind ride me, or did I ride the wind? I knew not.*

*Now, you have barely spent a full cycle in your master’s presence, yet your resentment and grievance arise again and again. Your body in its parts will not be borne by qi, your limbs in their joints will not be supported by the earth. To tread the void and ride the wind—how could this be within your reach?"*

Yin Sheng, deeply ashamed, held his breath for a long while and dared not speak again. *"Did not return to his lodging"* means he did not go home. *"Aggrieved"* means resentful. Having entreated ten times without instruction, he wished to depart. *"Gave no command for his withdrawal"* meant allowing him to leave of his own accord. *"Returned to follow him"* means he left and then came back.

Chapter Dai—such was Yin Sheng’s given name. *Ji* sounds like *ju*, a phonetic corruption. *The Master* refers to Lao Shang. *That man* refers to Bo Gaozi.

For three years, his mind harbored no thoughts of right or wrong, his mouth uttered no words of right or wrong—thus did he maintain stillness and silence, fearing even self-movement. *Geng* means "to change" or "to turn toward." Having abandoned thoughts of right and wrong, gain and loss, and cut off speech of right and wrong, gain and loss, should such thoughts arise again, his mind, now settled, would issue nothing but what is correct.

*Following* means compliance: whatever he thought or spoke arose naturally, without deliberate engagement in right or wrong, gain or loss. Thus, mind and principle became one, no longer subject to choice. *Unrestrained* means "free": thoughts arose without deliberation, words flowed without constraint—whether spoken vertically or horizontally, all was permissible. Freed and unshackled, no longer aware of right or wrong, gain or loss, the mind merged with principle and forgot it.

These four stages mark the successive steps in the work of Daoist cultivation. Once the inner self has merged with principle, then in movement and repose, every gesture and turn is likewise transformed. Thus it is said: "inner and outer advance together." When even the eyes, ears, nose, and mouth become indistinguishable, this is the moment of transformation and forgetting. The Buddhists call this "the six senses arising from one source"—the same idea.

*Gan* means "body." *Gan-ke* refers to the cast-off shell of a cicada. "Withered leaf or dry husk" describes one who no longer perceives his own body. Only after forgetting the body can one ride the wind. Your resentment and grievance are such that the burden of body and mind remains unforgotten—how then could heaven and earth bear even a fragment of your body, let alone its entirety? This chapter thus declares that the art of riding the wind requires one to regard the body as nothing before it can be achieved. This is no empty claim; only those who cultivate the Dao know these words to be true.

Liezi asked Guan Yin: "The perfected person walks through emptiness without sinking, treads fire without burning, moves above the myriad things without trembling. How may one attain this?" Guan Yin replied: "It is the preservation of pure qi, not a matter of cunning, skill, or bold resolve."

*Ji* is pronounced like *yu* in *yu-nü* ("fish" and "woman"). Whatever has form, sound, or color is a thing—how can things differ so greatly from one another? Why should one strive to surpass the ancients? It is but a matter of appearance. Thus, things are created from the formless and come to rest where transformation ceases. Those who attain this and exhaust its depths—how could they be led astray?

They dwell in measureless depth, conceal themselves in traceless order, and wander through the beginning and end of the myriad things. They unify their nature, nurture their qi, embrace their virtue, and thereby commune with the source of all things. Such as these preserve the wholeness of heaven, their spirit without flaw—how could things intrude upon them?

Guan Yin, named Xi, was one who met Laozi. These words are not transmitted sayings of master and disciple but rather teachings attributed to him. Guan Yinzi has his own book; though later hands have adulterated it, its most profound passages surpass those of other masters.

"Walking through emptiness without sinking" means moving through the void as if on solid ground. "Above the myriad things" describes its loftiness. The preservation of pure qi—modern practitioners of life-nurturing arts do likewise: one guards it with a mind devoid of intention, for it is not attainable through cunning, skill, or bold resolve. The *Zhuangzi*’s "Attaining Life" chapter contains similar words. This is a transmitted teaching of one lineage, a single great thread running through it all.

Ji means "to dwell"; Yu is "I," a corruption of its sound. Appearance, voice, and color are things with form. If one remains confined within the realm of the formed, how can one transcend? Only by being without mind does one rise above the myriad things.

The "prior" refers to the beginning of creation. How can one reach the prior? To say one is bound by form is to say one cannot know the beginning of creation. The earlier passage spoke of appearance, image, voice, and color—here it mentions only color. Among four words, one is singled out: such is the method of composition.

"Not-formed" refers to what precedes the emergence of qi. "Without transformation" refers to the unmanifest beginning of creation. "Creation" is that from which things arise. "To rest" means to abide. If one does not grasp the wonder of the not-formed and the untransformed but merely exhausts what is obtained from things, how can one attain the most profound insight? "Upright" means the utmost extreme.

"Without depth of measure" means it lies right before one, so shallow and near that people fail to see it. "Without end" means without beginning. "Measure" is method; "thread" is coherence. This is the method before one’s eyes, yet its origin is unknown—concealed and unrecognized, like the Master who took me for one who hides.

The beginning and end of the myriad things, that from which things are created, all belong to creation. Unifying one’s nature, nurturing one’s qi, and containing one’s virtue—this is nothing but pure unity and stillness. In terms of principle, it is nature; in terms of life, it is qi; in terms of what is obtained within oneself, it is virtue. "Its heavenly guard is complete" means its purity is undisturbed. "Without retreat or gap" means stability. When pure and stable, external things cannot move it. Thus it is said: how could things intrude?

When a drunkard falls from a carriage, though the fall be swift, he does not die. His bones and joints are the same as others’, yet the harm he suffers differs, for his spirit is whole. He is unaware of riding, unaware of falling. Life and death, alarm and fear do not enter his breast; thus he encounters things without dread. If one preserved in wine can be thus, how much more so one preserved in Heaven? The sage hides in Heaven, and so nothing can harm him.

These several lines echo the *Zhuangzi*. "Suffering harm" refers to the fall. Both riding and falling occur in drunkenness, without awareness or fear, so the spirit remains whole. Only because the spirit is whole, though injury may occur, it does not lead to death. "Encountering things" means not being obstructed by them. "Without dread" means without fear. "Hiding in Heaven" means being without mind and forgetting oneself. Thus is the analogy made. These lines are most precise.

Lie Yukou shot for Bo Hun Wu Ren, drawing the bow to full draw and placing a cup of water on his elbow. He loosed the arrow, and as one shaft followed another, each arrow found its place upon the string. In that moment, he was like a wooden figure.

Bo Hun Wu Ren said: "This is the shooting of one who shoots, not the shooting of one who does not shoot. Suppose I take you to climb a high mountain, tread upon perilous stones, and stand above an abyss of a hundred fathoms—could you shoot then?"

Thereupon, Wu Ren ascended the high mountain, trod upon perilous stones, and stood above the abyss of a hundred fathoms. Turning his back, he retreated hesitantly, with two-thirds of his feet hanging over the edge. He beckoned Yukou to advance. Yukou prostrated himself upon the ground, sweat streaming down to his heels.

Bo Hun Wu Ren said: "The perfected person gazes upward into the azure sky, plunges downward into the Yellow Springs, and roams to the eight limits, yet his spirit and qi remain unchanged. Now you, trembling and with eyes darting in alarm—you are far from the center indeed."

This passage is entirely the same as that in the *Zhuangzi*'s "Tian Zifang" chapter. "Drawing the bow to full draw" means drawing the bow to its fullest. The front hand is straight and level, allowing a cup of water to be placed upon the elbow—this speaks of steadiness. "Loosed" means shot. "Followed" means gone. "Repeated" means again. As one arrow departs, another is already on the string. The arrows on the string follow one after another, so that as one leaves, another is already in place—this describes the swiftness of his skill. "Wooden figure" refers to a wooden puppet. "Turning his back hesitantly" means facing the high mountain while his back overlooks the deep abyss, retreating yet not stopping—hence "hesitantly." With one-third of his feet on the ledge and two-thirds hanging over the void, this was peril in the extreme, yet Bo Hun could do it—this is what is meant by "guarding the pure qi."

Shooting while standing on level ground is ordinary shooting, hence he said: "This is not the shooting of one who does not shoot." If the spirit can guard the One, then though one gazes upward into the azure sky or plunges downward into the Yellow Springs, roaming to the eight limits, the heart remains unmoved. If, in the face of danger or ease, you still tremble and your eyes dart in alarm, then you have not yet grasped the learning of the perfected person. To seek the essence of hitting the mark with such shooting is indeed difficult. Thus he said: "You are far from the center indeed."

"Startled" means fearful. "Darting" means moving. "Darting eyes" means moving eyes. "Far from the center" means difficult. The Fan clan had a son named Zihua who was skilled at cultivating a private reputation. The entire state admired him. Favored by the lord of Jin, he held no office yet ranked above the three ministers. Whatever he glanced at, the state of Jin ennobled; whatever he spoke ill of, the state of Jin demoted. Those who frequented his courtyard were promoted at court.

Zihua sent his knights to attack one another with cunning and stupidity, strength and weakness. Though they were wounded before him, he paid no heed. Day and night, he made this his sport and amusement, until it nearly became the custom of the state.

He Sheng and Zi Bo, the foremost guests of the Fan clan, went on a journey. Passing through the fields, they lodged at the house of Tian Geng, Shang Qiu Kai. In the middle of the night, He Sheng and Zi Bo spoke to each other of Zihua’s fame and power, how he could make the living perish and the dead live, the rich poor and the poor rich.

Shang Qiu Kai, who had long suffered from hunger and cold, secretly listened from behind the north window. Thereupon, he borrowed grain and carried a basket to Zihua’s gate. Zihua’s disciples were all from noble families. Clad in white silk and riding in carriages, they strolled with leisurely steps and haughty gazes. Seeing Shang Qiu Kai—old, weak, with a soot-blackened face and unkempt clothes—they all sneered. Soon they grew familiar, mocking and deceiving him, pushing and shoving, leaning and pressing, doing whatever they pleased. Shang Qiu Kai never showed anger, and the guests’ tricks were simple, wearying them of their sport.

Then they took Shang Qiu Kai to a high terrace and, before the crowd, casually said: "Whoever can throw themselves down will be rewarded with a hundred gold pieces." The crowd all vied to respond. Shang Qiu Kai, taking them at their word, threw himself down first. His form was like a flying bird, alighting upon the ground—his bones nearly unharmed, as if made of stone.

The followers of the Fan clan thought this a mere coincidence and did not yet find it strange. Then they pointed to a secluded bend in the river and said: "There is a precious pearl in that water; you can retrieve it by swimming." Shang Qiu Kai again followed and swam. When he emerged, he indeed held a pearl. The crowd began to doubt.

Zihua then ordered that Shang Qiu Kai be given meat to eat and silk to wear. Soon after, the Fan clan’s treasury caught fire. Zihua said: "Whoever can enter the fire and retrieve the brocade will be rewarded according to what they bring out." Shang Qiu Kai went without hesitation, entering and exiting the fire. No dust clung to him, and his body was not scorched.

The followers of the Fan clan, believing him to possess the Dao, together apologized to him, saying: "We did not know you possessed the Dao and deceived you; we did not know you were a spirit-person and humiliated you. You must think us fools, deaf, blind. We dare ask: what is your Dao?"

Shang Qiu Kai said: "I possess no Dao. Even my own heart does not know the reason. Nevertheless, there is one thing—let me try to speak of it to you."

When those two guests of yours previously lodged at my dwelling, I heard them praise the power of the Fan clan, claiming it could make the living perish and the dead live, the rich poor and the poor rich. I believed them wholeheartedly and came without hesitation. Upon arrival, I took your followers’ words as truth, fearing only that my belief was not sincere enough, my actions not swift enough.
