I knew not where to place my body or where profit and harm lay. My heart was unified, and the myriad things posed no obstacle—it was as simple as this.

Now that I know your followers deceived me, I harbor suspicion within and display caution without, seeking only to preserve my past good fortune in escaping unscorched and undrowned. Yet my heart burns with shame, and I tremble with fear. How could I ever again approach fire or water?

From then on, the disciples of the Fan clan, upon encountering beggars or horse-doctors on the road, dared not insult them but would alight from their carriages to bow. Zai Wo heard of this and reported it to Confucius. Confucius said: "Did you not know?"

The man of utmost sincerity can move all things—stirring heaven and earth, moving ghosts and spirits, traversing the six directions without resistance. How could this apply merely to treading perilous paths or entering fire and water? If even Shang Qiu Kai, believing in false things, met no resistance, how much more so when both self and other are sincere? Remember this well. This passage depicts the word "sincerity" with utmost precision; grasp this meaning fully, and you may learn the Dao.

*Private name*: a personal appellation, called *private self* in Zhejiang.
*Mouth biased toward fat*: speaking ill and gnawing at others—people in Jin judged others’ likes and dislikes to promote or demote them.
*Wisdom and baseness*: wisdom and foolishness.
*Injury and harm*: contention causing damage.
The people of an entire state had made this their custom.
He Sheng, Zi Bo: names of two guests.
*Beyond the fields*: the wilderness.
*Field elder*: an old rustic.
*Three Elders, Five Elders*: all terms for the aged.
*Unkempt robes and caps*: torn and disordered attire.
*眲*: the meaning of disdain.
*挡, 扌必, 挨, 抌*: all four characters denote playful pushing and striking.
*Exhausted and weary*: the force of mockery spent.
*漫*: casual speech.
*Unscathed bones without stone*: no injury sustained.
*Swirling eddies*: where water forms whirlpools.
*昉*: to begin.
Began to let his fellow guests wear silk and eat meat.
*Dust and smoke could not blind*: dust and smoke could not confuse him.
*My sincerity without duality*: trust without doubt.
*Not knowing where to place the body*: forgetting oneself.
*Not knowing where profit and harm lie*: unaware of worldly dangers.
With the heart unified, no thing opposes the self.
Now that he knows the followers’ words were deceitful, inner suspicion arises, and outer perception is confused.
Recalling his past entry into fire and water, he counts himself lucky to have escaped unscorched and undrowned.
This means that when the heart is utterly pure, profit and harm, fortune and misfortune cannot move it; but if even a trace of calculation remains, it becomes difficult.
Zen has the phrase *leakage*—three excellent characters.
Even beggars and horse-doctors, if their hearts are sincere, may learn the Dao; thus, upon seeing them, one must alight from the carriage. This reflects the *Sutra of Perfect Enlightenment*’s teaching of not slighting beginners.
*Utmost sincerity*: the same as utmost integrity.
*Believing the false*: even when one believes another’s false words as true, it is still thus—how much more so when both are truly sincere?

During King Xuan of Zhou’s reign, the director of herds had a servant named Liang Yang who could raise wild birds and beasts. He fed them in the palace gardens, and even tigers, wolves, eagles, and ospreys became docile. Males and females gathered before him, breeding in groups; different species lived together without attacking or biting one another. The king, fearing the art would die with Liang Yang, ordered Mao Qiu Yuan to learn it. Liang Yang said: "I am but a lowly servant—what art could I teach you?" Fearing the king would suspect him of concealment, he offered a single word on his method of raising tigers.

All creatures with blood and qi rejoice when followed and rage when opposed—this is their nature. Yet how could joy and anger arise without cause? They are provoked by opposition. When feeding a tiger, one does not dare offer living prey, lest it be enraged by the killing; nor whole prey, lest it be enraged by the tearing. One times its hunger and satiety, understands its anger—though tigers and humans are different species, they fawn upon those who feed them, for this is following their nature. Thus, to kill them is to oppose it. How then could I dare oppose them to provoke anger? Yet neither do I follow them to elicit joy. For joy inevitably turns to anger, and anger inevitably returns to joy—both are immoderate.

Now, my heart holds neither opposition nor compliance, so birds and beasts regard me as one of their own. Thus, those that roam my garden do not long for high forests or open marshes; those that rest in my courtyard do not yearn for deep mountains or secluded valleys—this is the principle at work.

*Director of herds*: the official in charge of livestock.
*Servant*: his subordinate.
Fearing the art would not be passed down, the king had him teach Mao Qiu Yuan.
He speaks of raising tigers rather than other beasts, taking the greatest example.
One does not dare offer living or whole prey, lest the tiger’s anger arise.
Though tigers differ from humans, none fed by a man fail to fawn upon him, for he follows their nature.
These lines echo *Zhuangzi*’s "Human World" chapter.
*How could I oppose them to provoke anger? Neither do I follow them to elicit joy.*
*Joy inevitably turns to anger, anger inevitably returns to joy.*
These lines depict human nature and the principles of things with great precision.
*Immoderate*: neither accords with principle.
*Heart without opposition or compliance*: without intent.
Without intent, one can forget the self and merge with things. This passage merely elucidates the principle of *wu xin* (no-mind)—it is an excellent discourse.

Yan Hui asked Confucius: "I once crossed the deep rapids of Shang. The ferryman handled the boat as if by divine skill. I asked him: *Can one learn to steer a boat?* He said: *Yes. Those who can swim may be taught. Skilled swimmers learn quickly. But as for divers—never have I seen one who, without ever having seen a boat, could suddenly take up the oars.* I asked him further, but he did not answer. May I ask what this means?"

Confucius said: "As for medical texts, you and I have long studied their words, yet we have not grasped their essence—do you truly take this to be the Way? Those who can swim may be taught, for they are light upon the water. Skilled swimmers learn quickly, for they have forgotten the water. But as for divers—never having seen a boat yet suddenly taking up the oars—it is because they see the abyss as level ground, and a capsized boat as merely their carriage rolling back. Though ten thousand things may overturn or retreat before them, they cannot enter the dwelling of their heart—where could they go and not be at ease?

*Shangshen* is the name of the abyss. *You* refers to those who float. Skilled swimmers are practiced in floating. *Mo ren* are those who can dive. *Su* means to rise. *Yan* (medical texts) is akin to *yi* (sighing). To *play with their words* is to engage with their surface. *Essence* refers to their inner meaning. This speaks of one who has not deeply seen the Way. *Do you truly take this to be the Way?* *Er* means 'you'—do you take this to be the Way? To see only the outer form of the Way without perceiving its inner truth—is this truly the Way?

Those who are light upon the water may be taught, for they do not fear it. Those who have forgotten the water have practiced repeatedly and thus mastered it, for they are at home in it. But divers rise to take up the oars without learning, for they see water as level ground. Though ten thousand things may overturn or retreat before them, they remain unmoved in heart—where could they go and not find themselves at ease? This further expounds the principle. The heart is the dwelling of spirit and clarity; if nothing can enter its dwelling, then nothing can disturb it.

One who grasps with tiles is skillful; one who grasps with hooks is hesitant; one who grasps with gold is dull. The skill is the same, yet when one clings to externals, they weigh heavily upon the outside. All who weigh externals neglect the inner. These lines are identical to those in *Zhuangzi’s* "Attaining Life" chapter, though Zhuangzi uses *zhu* (to pour) where this uses *kou* (to cast). The words differ, but the meaning is the same. *Kou* means to throw. Zhuangzi speaks of *lightening the inner*, while this speaks of *neglecting the inner*. *Gong* means to grasp or clasp. Hooks are heavier than tiles, and gold heavier than hooks. The archer’s skill is fundamentally one in the heart, yet when one clings to externals, what is weighed lies outside, and the inner heart becomes obstructed, hesitant, and dull—though skilled, one becomes clumsy.

Confucius observed at Lüliang, where the waterfall plunged thirty *ren* and the frothing current stretched thirty *li*, a place where turtles, alligators, fish, and soft-shelled turtles could not swim. He saw a man swimming there and thought him in distress, seeking to drown himself. He sent his disciples to follow the current and rescue him. After several hundred paces, the man emerged, loosened his hair, and walked along singing as he swam through the shallows. Confucius followed and asked him: "The waterfall at Lüliang plunges thirty *ren*, and its frothing current stretches thirty *li*—a place where turtles, alligators, fish, and soft-shelled turtles cannot swim. When I saw you entering it, I thought you were in distress and wished to die, so I sent my disciples to follow the current and rescue you. Yet you emerged, loosened your hair, and walked along singing. I took you for a ghost, but upon closer look, you are a man. May I ask if there is a Way to tread water?"

The man said: "No, I have no Way. I begin with what is familiar, grow with my nature, and complete it with fate. I enter with the swirling current and emerge with the surging flow. I follow the water’s Way without imposing my own will—this is how I tread it."

Confucius said: "What do you mean by *beginning with what is familiar, growing with nature, and completing it with fate*?"

The man said: "I was born on the hills and am at ease on the hills—that is what is familiar. I grew up in the water and am at ease in the water—that is my nature. I do not know why I am so, yet I am so—that is fate."

*Lüliang* is a place name. *Xuan shui* refers to a waterfall. The frothing current stretches thirty *li*—it is vast. *Bing liu* means to follow the current to rescue him. *Cheng* means to receive or catch. *Tang xing* (as per the annotation) refers to the shallows beneath a dike. *Ji* (in *Zhuangzi’s* "Attaining Life" chapter) is written as *qi*, referring to the swirling vortex of water—*ji* is also a miswriting. *Gu* refers to the surging flow. *Entering and emerging* means rising and falling with the water. *Following the water’s Way without imposing one’s own will* means submitting to the water’s force without resistance.

When born on the hills, one is at ease on the hills; when raised in the water, one is at ease in the water—all is in accord with one’s natural disposition, without knowing why. Thus, the three terms *familiarity, nature,* and *fate* are initially without distinction; they are merely used in this way for the sake of composition. To forcibly seek meaning in the words *born* and *raised* would be a mistake. Mencius said: "To speak of nature is merely to speak of what is familiar." This is the same *familiarity*. Later, when Confucius traveled to Chu, he emerged from a forest and saw a hunchback catching cicadas as easily as if picking them up.

Confucius said: "You are skillful. Is there a Way to this?"

The man said: "I have a Way."

In the fifth or sixth month, he stacked pellets like pellets of clay. When he could stack two without them falling, he lost only a fraction; when he could stack three without them falling, he lost but one in ten; when he could stack five without them falling, he caught cicadas as easily as picking them up.

My stance is like a wooden stake firmly planted; my arm, holding the pole, is like a withered branch. Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things are many, I know only the cicada’s wings. I do not turn back, nor do I waver—if I do not allow the myriad things to distract me from the cicada’s wings, how could I fail?

Confucius turned to his disciples and said: "When the mind is undivided, it verges on the numinous." This must refer to the hunchbacked elder.

The elder said: "You, a scholar in Confucian robes—what do you know of such matters? First cultivate what you have, then you may speak further of higher things."

This passage is identical to the *Zhuangzi*’s "Attaining Life" chapter. *Bearing the cicada* means holding a pole to catch them. Stacking pellets at the tip of the pole, from two to five without them falling, shows how his concentration becomes spirit-like. Guo Xiang’s commentary on the *Zhuangzi* uses two instances of *stillness and scrutiny*, which is also apt. *A wooden stake firmly planted* refers to what is now called a wooden post. *Stake* is the post; *zhu* is the name of a tree; *ju* means fixed. It seems these three characters existed in ancient times. *Not turning back, not wavering* simply means unwavering focus. When catching cicadas, his body was as motionless as a wooden post, his arm like a withered branch. His mind was fixed solely on the cicada’s wings, unaware of anything else—this is the height of singular focus. *When the mind is undivided* means his intent is not divided. *Verging on the numinous* means concentration that becomes spirit-like. Though this uses a metaphor to discuss the preservation of pure *qi*, such skill truly exists in the world, even today—yet people treat it as mere technique, unaware of the Dao it embodies.

The hunchback is one whose back is bent. *Confucian robes* refer to the attire of scholars. "First cultivate what you have today, then you may speak further of higher things"—this suggests the profundity of the Way does not end here. *Speak further* means to discuss what lies beyond. The rest is the same as in the *Zhuangzi*. *Wang’er* in the original is written as *pellets* in the *Zhuangzi*. The character *doubt* in the original should be *concentration*, following the *Zhuangzi*.

There was a man by the sea who loved gulls. Every morning, he went to the shore and followed the gulls, and hundreds of gulls would come without cease. His father said: "I hear the gulls all follow you—catch some so I may play with them." The next day, when he went to the shore, the gulls danced above but would not land.

Thus it is said: "The perfect word leaves words behind; the perfect act leaves acting behind." What common knowledge knows is shallow. *Gull* and *ou* are interchangeable. *Hundreds coming without cease* means their comings and goings were countless, not limited to a hundred. *Dancing but not landing* means they were wary. For if the slightest thought arises, the mechanism is already exposed—how could one forget self and other? This metaphor illustrates wordless words, actionless action, and knowledge beyond knowing—its meaning is deeply intimate. For *non-action*, *wordlessness*, and *unknowing* are merely the absence of self-will. The perfect word is wordless, hence: "The perfect word leaves words behind." The perfect act is actionless, hence: "The perfect act leaves acting behind." People do not know what cannot be known and take what can be known as knowledge—what they see is shallow, hence: "What common knowledge knows." *Common* means the same, like the word *all*.

These three phrases are also found in the *Zhuangzi*’s "Attaining Life" chapter.

When Viscount Xiang of Zhao led a hundred thousand men to hunt in Zhongshan, they piled grass and set fire to the forest, fanning flames across a hundred *li*. A man emerged from a stone cliff, rising and falling with the smoke and embers. The crowd thought him a ghost. After the fire passed, he walked out slowly, as if untouched by what he had endured.

Viscount Xiang, astonished, detained him and observed him closely: his form, complexion, and seven orifices were human; his breath and voice were human. He asked: "By what Way did you dwell in stone? By what Way did you enter fire?"

The man said: "What thing do you call stone? What thing do you call fire?"

Viscount Xiang said: "What you just emerged from was stone; what you just passed through was fire."

The man said: "I do not know."

Marquis Wen of Wei heard of this and asked Zixia: "What sort of man is he?"

Zixia said: "From what I have heard of the Master’s teachings, one who is harmonious is in great accord with things, so that nothing can harm or obstruct him. Roaming through metal and stone, treading through water and fire—all are possible."

Marquis Wen said: "Why do you not do this?"

Zixia said: "To hollow out the heart and discard knowledge—I have not yet attained this. However, I can speak of it when there is leisure."

Marquis Wen said: "Why does the Master not do this?"

Zixia said: "The Master is capable but chooses not to act."

Marquis Wen was greatly pleased.

*Jie reng* means piling grass. They trampled the grass and burned the forest for the hunt. *What thing do you call stone? What thing do you call fire?* This is also a metaphor for knowledge beyond knowing. The account of treading through fire shares the same meaning as Shang Qiu Kai’s story. *Harmonious* means being in great accord with things. This *harmony* is the work of creation.

When the heart-mind merges as one with the workings of creation, then nothing differs from the self. Initially, there is no obstruction—hollowing out the heart and discarding knowledge is precisely the knowledge beyond knowing.

*Try* means to attempt; *speak of it when there is leisure* means to have long expounded upon this. The Master being *capable but choosing not to act* is like Huangbo and the foreign monk crossing water: Huangbo thought it a display of demonic trickery, but the monk turned back and bowed, saying, "A vessel of the Great Vehicle—I am not your equal." This is the very point of the discussion.

A divine diviner from Qi arrived in Zheng and was named Ji Cheng. He knew the life and death, survival and perishing, fortune and misfortune, long life and early death of men, predicting them by year, month, ten-day period, or day with godlike accuracy. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled. When Liezi saw him, he was entranced and returned to tell Huqiuzi, saying, "At first, I thought your Way was supreme, but now there is something even greater."

Huzi said, "I have shown you only its outward form, not its true substance—how could you have truly attained the Way? A flock of hens without a rooster—how could they lay eggs? If you set your Way against the world and insist it must be believed, you will surely be exposed. That is why others can divine your fate. Bring him here tomorrow to meet me."

*Year, month, ten-day period, or day* refers to predictions near or far. A *divine diviner* is a physiognomist whose words proved as accurate as a god’s. Those who fled did so in fear of his veracity. *Entranced* means utterly convinced. *Shown you only its outward form* means revealing only its external aspect; *not its true substance* means not yet revealing its inner reality. *You* refers to Liezi. "You have not yet fully witnessed its true substance—how can you claim to have attained the Way?" The word *truly* implies mistaking partial attainment for full mastery; this must be understood through the tone of the passage. Only with both male and female can life be born—this refers to egg-laying creatures. Without a male, how could there be eggs? This means that without a heart-mind, there are no traces. This sentence illustrates that his heart-mind had not yet transformed, so it could be perceived through form.

*Set against* means to elevate. To elevate one’s Way above the world and demand belief in it is to leave traces—this is the untransformed state, which is why the diviner could read your fate.

The next day, Liezi brought him to meet Huzi. Upon leaving, the diviner said to Liezi, "Alas! Your master is dead—he will not live. He cannot last even ten days. I saw something strange in him—like damp ashes."

Liezi entered, his tears soaking his lapels, and told Huzi. Huzi said, "Just now, I showed him the earthly pattern of stillness—like the first sprouts of guilt. *Hu* is the sound of not trembling. If it does not cease, he nearly saw my *blocked virtue’s subtlety*."

The "damp ashes" describe his vital qi nearing exhaustion, like embers already sodden and about to extinguish.

*Earthly pattern* is akin to the term used in Chan meditation practice. *Guilt* coalesces as a sprout—sprouting yet not sprouting. *Not trembling* means precisely that: not trembling, not moving. *Not ceasing* coalesces as *not upright*, where "upright" cannot be fixed in speech. This "not upright" aligns with Mencius’ phrase "there must be action, yet do not fix it." Only when there is the semblance of movement yet no movement does the diviner perceive "damp ashes." Living ashes are fire; damp ashes signify the fire on the verge of extinction.

*Blocked virtue’s subtlety* is another term from meditative practice. *Virtue’s subtlety* is the impulse of life. *Blocked* means closed—sealing the mechanism so it does not move, hence the appearance of life on the brink of extinction, leading Jixian to conclude he would not live.

He tried bringing him again. The next day, he brought him once more. Upon seeing Huzi, the diviner said to Liezi, "Fortunate indeed! Your master has met me—he is recovering. The ashes now hold life! I saw his *blocked power*."

Liezi entered and told Huzi. Huzi said, "Just now, I showed him the *Heaven-and-Earth* pattern. Names and realities did not enter, yet the subtlety arose from the heels—this is *blocked power*. He nearly saw my *subtle goodness*."

*Blocked power* is movement within stillness. *Power* is akin to *mechanism*, though mechanism is subtle while power is manifest. Within the sealed closure, the mechanism stirs and becomes visible, so Jixian perceived it as fully alive.

*Heaven-and-Earth* is another meditative term, akin to "heavenly field." The field above heaven is not earthly soil but the soil of spontaneity—like modern practitioners of self-cultivation who call the tongue the "heavenly ford" or the crown of the head "mud-ball," signifying the impulse of life sprouting and ascending.

*Names and realities did not enter* means both being and non-being are transcended. *The mechanism arises from the heels* describes qi ascending from below, so subtle it cannot be seen—hence "mechanism." *Subtle goodness* refers to the movement of inherent nature.

He tried bringing him again. The next day, he brought him once more to see Huzi.

He emerged and said to Liezi: "Your master is not in a state of fasting; I cannot divine for him. Try fasting, and I shall divine for him again later." Liezi entered and reported this to Huzi. Huzi said: "Earlier, I showed him the Great Void without trace—this was nearly revealing to him my qi-mechanism in equipoise. *Great Void without trace* is another meditative term: *Great Void* means the vast emptiness; *without trace* means no beginning or end is visible. *Equipoise* means balance, or half: when the qi-mechanism moves to the state of balance or halfway, it is half-dynamic and half-quiescent. The spirit-diviner deemed me not fasting, for I was half-dynamic and half-quiescent, unsettled.

The whirlpool of the giant salamander becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of still water becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of flowing water becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of surging water becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of cascading water becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of seeping water becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of dammed water becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of welling water becomes an abyss; the whirlpool of divergent water becomes an abyss—these are the Nine Abysses. This passage speaks of the Nine Abysses as names for meditative states. Today, Buddhists use the term *observances*, but the ancients called them *abysses*. With nine names, they likely correspond to the modern Twelve Observances. Yet *Zhuangzi* mentions only three; here, all nine appear, suggesting this is not from the original *Liezi* but an interpolation by later hands. *Whirlpool* should be written as *審* (to examine), following *Zhuangzi*—*審* means *truth*. The names of the Nine Abysses are all metaphors, hence: "such-and-such an abyss is a metaphor for truth; such-and-such a metaphor is truth for such-and-such an abyss."

The *giant salamander* is a large fish; *whirling* means spiraling. *Zhuangzi* writes *桓* (to revolve) here, which is correct. Water containing a giant salamander presents the image of half-quiescence and half-dynamism—this is the so-called *qi-mechanism in equipoise*. *Still water* is quiescence, the so-called *mechanism of blocked virtue*. *Flowing water* is dynamism, the so-called *mechanism of subtlety*. *Zhuangzi* says: "The abyss has nine names; here, three are given." These three metaphors are cited precisely to confirm the earlier statement. Examining the flow of this text, it seems to lose the original meaning; from this, the right and wrong of the two books become apparent.

*Surging water* wells up from below. *Cascading water* pours down from above. *Seeping water* (also written as *汜*) should be *汍*—water emerging from a side crevice. *Dammed water* is obstructed and does not flow; it is not water that stills itself. *Welling water* seeps out from underground springs, pooling into stagnant ponds. *Divergent water*, as the *Mao Commentary on the Book of Songs* says, "shares the same source but flows to different destinations." The above water names mostly appear in the *Erya*; they were likely added by later hands using the *Erya*’s terminology. The annotators say: "Water’s rushing, surging, flowing, and stilling resemble the heart-mind of the perfected one, which responds to external things with ease or difficulty, exhibiting movement, quiescence, advance, or retreat." This interpretation is mistaken. Guo Xiang’s commentary on this passage in *Zhuangzi* is of the same kind.

He tried bringing him again.

The next day, he brought him again to see Master Hu. Before he could settle into his stance, the visitor lost himself and fled. Master Hu said, "Pursue him." Liezi chased after him but could not catch up. Returning, he reported to Master Hu, saying, "He has vanished, he is gone—I could not reach him." Master Hu said, "Just now, I showed him what had never yet departed from my origin. I met him with emptiness and compliance, leaving him unsure of who or what I was. Thus, he took me for withered reeds, for surging waves—hence his flight. 'Vanished' and 'gone' mean he could no longer perceive me. 'Never yet departed from my origin' is also a matter of observing names. 'Emptiness' is the void. 'Compliance' means yielding and following. As if nothing, as if something—unsure of what it was, hence 'unsure of who or what.' *Mao* is pronounced *tui*. *Maomao* means broken and crushed. *Bōliú* means vast and boundless. He saw nothing of me, only broken reeds and boundless waves—thus he lost himself and fled."

Thereafter, Liezi returned home, believing he had never yet begun to learn. For three years he did not venture out, cooking for his wife, eating pork as he would human food, and in all matters remaining detached. He carved and polished only to return to simplicity, standing alone like an unhewn block, sealed and complete, thus abiding to the end. "Cooking for his wife" means he took her place at the hearth and cauldron without leaving. "Eating pork as he would human food" speaks of gathering his spirit within, no longer discerning outward forms. "Detached in all matters" means that though he acted, he did so without self-awareness, as if not personally engaged. "Carving and polishing to return to simplicity" refers to breaking his limbs and dulling his senses. "Standing alone like an unhewn block" is like a wooden figurine. "Sealed" means possessing edges and corners. "Abundant" means many. If his form were like a wooden figurine, how could it have many edges and corners? "Abiding to the end" means he remained thus for the rest of his life, ever constant in this state. *Fēn* is written as *fēn*, *róng* as *zāi*. Following *Zhuangzi* here, these are all errors in transcription.

Both Zhuangzi and Liezi belong to the same school of learning. Such discussions must have been part of their shared teachings, which is why both texts contain them.

When Liezi was traveling to Qi, he turned back midway and encountered Bohun Maoren. Bohun Maoren asked, "Why have you returned?" Liezi replied, "I was startled." "Startled by what?"

Liezi had eaten at ten millet-porridge stalls, and five of them had served him first. Bohun Maoren said, "If this is so, then why were you startled?"

Liezi replied, "When inner sincerity is not dissolved, one’s outward demeanor radiates brilliance, thereby overawing others’ hearts, causing them to slight the noble and the elder and to attend solely to their own concerns. Those millet-porridge vendors merely sell gruel for a living, with no great surplus profit. Their gains are meager, their influence slight, and yet they still behave thus—how much more so a lord of ten thousand chariots, whose person is wearied by state affairs and whose wisdom is exhausted by governance? If he were to entrust me with responsibilities and demand results from me, this is why I was startled."

Bohun Maoren said, "Excellent is your observation! Remain as you are, and others will come to safeguard you."

Shortly after, Liezi went to visit Bohun Maoren, only to find the shoes outside his door filling the entrance. Bohun Maoren stood facing north, leaning heavily on his staff and pressing it against his chin. After a while, without a word, he went inside. The attendant informed Liezi, who rushed over barefoot, clutching his shoes, and upon reaching the door, asked, "Since you have come, Master, have you not neglected your medicine?"

Bohun Maoren replied, "It is done. I had already told you: others will come to safeguard you. Indeed, they have safeguarded you."

Not that you can make others safeguard you, but that you cannot prevent others from safeguarding you—why then employ such artifice to move them? Such delight in being moved only breeds strangeness. If you must provoke a response, it will unsettle your very foundation—what purpose does this serve? Those who keep your company offer you no counsel; their petty words are all poison to men. Neither awakened nor enlightened, how can they discern one another? Where lies the path to return? Where do words turn back upon themselves?

One who takes his meal at a ten-vinegar stall, yet five stalls send him offerings first—such a man commands respect without purchase, for harmony and compliance accumulate within, and radiance and brilliance bloom without. These are the words of the sagely gate. Yet if inner sincerity remains unresolved, it is but accumulated virtue untransformed. Resolution is transformation. *Die* is movement; *xingdie* is the bearing and conduct of form. One who "achieves radiance" possesses a luminous demeanor, embodying the meaning of inner accumulation manifesting outwardly—yet this is deemed a learning of traces. To "pacify the hearts of others" is to subdue them, for if I cannot transcend traces, others perceive them, and thus their hearts submit in reverence. As Master Zhaozhou said: "This old monk’s cultivation lacks power—even ghosts and spirits see through it." This is the same idea.

To "value age" is to respect the elderly, for age naturally commands reverence. Yet I am neither old nor noble, and still, others slight them while honoring me—this reverence surpasses even that due to age and rank. *Zheng* means to gather, to accumulate. Such matters, when amassed over time, inevitably breed harm: the more one’s fame and traces are exposed, the harder it becomes to escape the perils of the age. "No surplus profit" means the gain left over is scant. This line, compared to *Zhuangzi*, adds a "not"—altering the meaning entirely. *Ying* is profit. Those with power in the world can sway others; buying vinegar is a trifling matter, for one initially holds no authority to influence others—yet they revere me so. How much more so for a ruler?

When the body is at its peak and wisdom is exhausted, they will surely seek me out and employ me, demanding I present them with success—this is what is meant by *zheng*, the very peril it entails. *Xiao* means to offer. Bohun Maoren rejoiced in this, saying: "Excellent is your observation!"—meaning you possess a single eye for this. He added: "Remain as you are." This meant there was no need to wander further, for others would flock to you and safeguard you as their teacher. *Chu* means to stay; *yi* is an auxiliary particle. *Bao* means to guard. The multitude of followers filling the doorway attests to this.

Yet this single word *bao* already hints at insufficiency, for Bohun Maoren’s vision stands a level higher. The shoes outside the door were many—disciples had gathered in throngs. "Leaning heavily on his staff and pressing it against his chin" describes him standing erect, planting his staff and resting his chin upon it. *Cu* means to press. The *binzhe* was the one who received guests. "Clutching his shoes and running barefoot"—in ancient times, one sat on mats and removed shoes before entering; in his haste to welcome Bohun Maoren, he did not have time to put them on. "Neglecting medicine" refers to teaching, to opening and refining as with medicinal stones. *Fei* means to set aside. "It is done" conveys the sense of "let it rest." I had already told you others would safeguard you—you cannot prevent them from doing so, just as *Zhuangzi* says: "Forgetting oneself is easy; making others forget you is difficult."

Why then do you act thus? *Er* refers to you; *yong* means to do. What is the purpose of your conduct in moving others so? That others are moved and take delight in you stems from your inability to conceal yourself, allowing strangeness to emerge and be seen—hence the saying: "Delight in being moved breeds strangeness." If you persist in this, not only will your outward form fail to remain hidden, but you will inevitably be stirred, shaking your very foundation—all to no avail. *Wu wei* means "no benefit," with an added sense of "even worse."

Those who keep your company are your friends. Their learning is shallow, their words trivial—hence "petty words," all of which are poison to men. With no one to admonish you, you remain unawakened and unenlightened. Who then will question you or care for your well-being? *Xiang shu* means "who," an interrogative phrase. This passage aligns with *Zhuangzi*’s "Lie Yu Kou" chapter, differing by only a word or two.

When Yang Zhu journeyed south to Pei, and Lao Dan traveled west to Qin, they met at the outskirts. Arriving in Liang, Yang Zhu encountered Laozi. Midway, Laozi looked up to the sky and sighed: "At first, I thought you could be taught—now, you cannot." Yang Zhu did not respond. Upon reaching their lodging, he brought water for washing, a towel and comb, removed his shoes outside the door, and advanced on his knees, saying: "Earlier, Master looked up to the sky and sighed: 'At first, I thought you could be taught—now, you cannot.' Your disciple wished to ask for an explanation, but your journey afforded no pause, so I dared not. Now that you have paused, I beg to know my fault."

Laozi said: "Your haughty gaze, your arrogant stare—who would wish to dwell with you? The purest white appears sullied; the greatest virtue seems insufficient." Yang Zhu’s expression changed, and he said solemnly: "I humbly receive your instruction."

When he had departed before, the innkeepers had come forth to greet and escort him; the host of the lodging held the mat, his wife held the towel and comb, and the guests yielded their seats while the cooks withdrew from the hearth. Upon his return, however, the innkeepers contended with him for seating.

Yang Zhu asked about his fault, saying: "Master declared I could no longer be taught—where, then, does my error lie?"

"睢睢吁吁" describes a bearing of self-importance and constraint. "Who would wish to dwell with you?" means that he had not forgotten the distinction between self and others, ever acting as though sharing company with them. "The purest white appears sullied" conveys the intent to remain unassuming despite clarity. "The greatest virtue seems insufficient" means that though one possesses it, one does not presume upon it.

"迎将" means to greet and see off. "家公" refers to the master of the inn. "执席" and "执巾栉" denote acts of service. "炀者" means the cook. Yielding seats and withdrawing from the hearth were signs of respect. "Contending for seating" shows that they no longer regarded him as worthy of reverence. Before hearing Laozi’s words, he carried himself with self-importance, so others treated him with deference. Once enlightened, he withdrew into humility, and people came to see him as an ordinary man.

This passage is identical to the "Yu Yan" chapter of the *Zhuangzi*, except that "涫" in this text appears as "盥" in the *Zhuangzi*, though the meaning is the same.

Yang Zhu passed through Song and stayed at an inn. The innkeeper had two concubines: one beautiful, the other plain. The plain one was favored, while the beautiful one was slighted. Yang Zhu asked the reason. The innkeeper’s young attendant replied: "The beautiful one is self-aware of her beauty, so I do not recognize it as beauty. The plain one is self-aware of her plainness, so I do not recognize it as plainness." Yang Zhu said: "Take note, disciples. Conduct yourself with virtue but abandon the conduct of self-regard—wherever you go, will you not be beloved?" This passage is also found in the "Shan Mu" chapter of the *Zhuangzi*.

"Self-aware of her beauty" means she was proud and boastful. "Self-aware of her plainness" means she was modest and considered herself inadequate. "Conduct yourself with virtue but abandon the conduct of self-regard" means to possess the virtue of the worthy without the bearing of self-importance—wherever one goes, people will cherish and delight in them. This is a most intimate and practical lesson in life.

*Mencius* portrays Yang Zhu as an egoist, but judging from these passages, Yang Zhu seems to have followed Laozi’s teachings. Could it be that Yang Zhu initially studied under Laozi before establishing his own school?

There are ways that always prevail in the world and ways that do not always prevail. The way that always prevails is called softness; the way that does not always prevail is called strength. Both are known, yet people fail to recognize them. Thus, the ancients said: "Strength first contends with those unlike oneself; softness first yields to what surpasses oneself. Contending with those unlike oneself leads to peril when matched. Yielding to what surpasses oneself brings no peril. By this, one prevails over the self as if over others; by this, one bears the world as if bearing nothing. It is called prevailing without striving to prevail, bearing without striving to bear."

Softness can always prevail; strength cannot. This is Laozi’s teaching. Both principles are easily understood, yet most people remain unaware of them. Since ancient times, those who boast of their strength regard others as inferior and take pleasure in surpassing them. If this is considered strength, then one stronger than oneself will surely contend with them, and they will not prevail—thus, peril follows. Hence it is said: "Strength first contends with those unlike oneself; when matched, peril ensues." Those who esteem softness see all others as surpassing themselves and remain behind, always weak and unassuming—where, then, is peril? Hence it is said: "Softness first yields to what surpasses oneself, bringing no peril."

By this way, one guards the self and always prevails, thus: "By this, one prevails over the self as if over others." "As if over others" means "as if over equals." Those who can always prevail over themselves are such people. By this way, one bears the affairs of the world and always prevails, thus: "By this, one bears the world as if bearing nothing," meaning those who can bear the world are such people. For if one claims not to prevail, one prevails at all times—hence "prevailing without striving to prevail." If one claims not to bear, the world can be borne—hence "bearing without striving to bear."

Yuzhi said: "To be firm, one must guard with softness; to be strong, one must preserve with weakness. Accumulate softness, and it becomes firm; accumulate weakness, and it becomes strong. Observe what one accumulates, and you will know the realm of fortune or misfortune."

Strength prevails over those unlike oneself, but when matched, it is rigid. Softness prevails over what surpasses oneself, and its power is immeasurable. Lao Dan said: "Arms that are strong will perish; wood that is strong will break. The soft and weak are the companions of life; the hard and strong are the companions of death."

Guarding with softness ensures enduring firmness; preserving with weakness ensures enduring strength. Constant softness and weakness bring fortune; inability to be soft or weak brings misfortune. Hence it is said: "Observe what one accumulates, and you will know the realm of fortune or misfortune." "Accumulate" means to persist over time. Those who rely on strength to prevail over inferiors disregard their equals, and when their rigidity meets opposition, they do not prevail. Hence it is said: "Strength prevails over those unlike oneself; when matched, it is rigid." Guarding with softness and seeing all others as surpassing oneself, one contends with nothing, and thus always prevails. Hence it is said: "Softness prevails over what surpasses oneself, and its power is immeasurable."

These are the words of Yuzhi, further corroborated by several passages from the *Laozi*. Yuzhi had his own book and was also a disciple of Laozi.

"Arms that are strong will perish" means that those who rely on military force in conflict will surely perish.

"Wood that is rigid will break"—like the vine or willow, which are difficult to snap, while solid wood breaks easily. The supple and weak endure, while the hard and strong perish; these are of the same kind. This saying appears in the *Laozi*, chapter seventy-six. It draws an analogy between humans and plants in life and death, hence the term 'of the same kind.' Following the earlier metaphors of arms and wood, it likewise speaks of 'the same kind,' meaning that softness prevails while rigidity fails—all of this belongs to the same category.

Appearance need not be childlike for wisdom to be childlike; wisdom need not be childlike for appearance to be childlike. The sage values childlike wisdom and disregards childlike appearance, while the common people favor childlike appearance and distance themselves from childlike wisdom. When appearance resembles their own, they draw near and cherish it; when it differs, they keep their distance and fear it.

A being with a seven-foot frame, distinct hands and feet, hair on the head and teeth in the mouth, standing upright and moving forward—this is called a human, yet it may harbor the heart of a beast. Though it has the heart of a beast, its appearance earns it affection. Creatures with wings and horns, fangs and claws, flying aloft or crouching to run—these are called birds and beasts, yet they may possess the heart of a human. Though they have the heart of a human, their appearance sets them apart.

'Childlike' means 'same,' a phonetic variation. The intent is that while human appearance differs from that of birds and beasts, their hearts may be the same. The sage values sameness of heart, not sameness of form—this is a lament against the world. 'Standing upright and moving forward' means relying on one another in shared purpose. 'Aloft' means above; 'crouching' means below.

Fu Xi, Nü Wa, Shen Nong, and the Xia sovereigns had serpent bodies and human faces, ox heads and tiger noses—these bore non-human forms yet possessed the great virtue of sages. Jie of Xia, Zhou of Yin, Huan of Lu, and Mu of Chu had the seven apertures of human appearance, yet harbored the hearts of beasts. Yet the common people cling to a single form in pursuit of supreme wisdom—how can this suffice?

The Yellow Emperor and the Flame Emperor clashed on the wilds of Banquan, leading bears, brown bears, wolves, leopards, lynxes, and tigers as vanguard, with eagles, hawks, falcons, and kites as banners—this was commanding beasts through force. Yao tasked Kui with overseeing music: striking and tapping stones, and the hundred beasts danced in unison; the *Xiao Shao* played nine movements, and the phoenix came to pay homage—this was summoning beasts through sound. If the hearts of birds and beasts differ from those of humans, how so? Their forms and voices differ from ours, yet we know not how to engage them. The sage is all-knowing and all-penetrating, thus able to draw them forth and employ them.

The intelligence of birds and beasts naturally aligns with that of humans in some ways. Their desires and means of preserving life require no borrowed wisdom from humans: males and females pair, mothers and offspring bond; they avoid flatlands and seek high ground, shun cold and favor warmth; when dwelling, they form groups, and when moving, they march in ranks; the small stay within, the strong guard without; when drinking, they accompany one another, and when feeding, they call in flocks.

In the earliest antiquity, they dwelled alongside humans and walked among them. In the age of emperors and kings, they began to scatter in fear and disorder. By the final era, they hid and fled to evade harm. Now, in the eastern land of the Jie people, there are those who frequently understand the speech of the six domestic animals—this is the achievement of partial knowledge. The divine sages of highest antiquity fully comprehended the dispositions of all things and entirely grasped the voices of different kinds. They gathered and assembled them, instructed and received them, making them equal to the people. Thus, they first convened ghosts, spirits, and mountain demons, then reached the people of the eight directions, and finally gathered birds, beasts, insects, and moths. This speaks to the shared heart and intelligence of all beings with blood and breath.

The divine sages, knowing this, left nothing untaught in their instruction. The three sages, though their forms differed from humans, possessed the great virtue of sages—this illustrates that Jie, Zhou, Huan, and Mu, though human in form, truly had the hearts of beasts. This further demonstrates that those who command beasts through force or summon them through sound provide visible evidence to substantiate the argument.

Bears and tigers leading the vanguard can be seen in the tale of Ju Wuba of the Eastern Han, while eagles and kites as banners were released to follow their direction, with people following in their wake—hence the term 'banners.' The intelligence of birds and beasts stems from their desires and the nurturing of their young—how could this be taught by humans? Thus it is said: 'They do not borrow wisdom from humans.' 'All' means 'every'; 'preserving' means 'nurturing.'

In ancient times, humans lived alongside deer and pigs—such things occurred, and this account borrows from them to illustrate the discourse on humans and beasts. 'Partial knowledge' refers to solitary enlightenment, hence 'the achievement of partial knowledge,' whereas the ancient sages possessed complete knowledge. 'Complete' means 'all.' 'Nothing untaught' means that both humans and different kinds were instructed.

The intent is that in the earliest antiquity, even different kinds could be taught to be equal to humans, yet in the final era, people resemble different kinds, and with no sages arising to transform and guide them, this too is a lament of indignation.

In Song there was a man called Ju Gong who loved monkeys and raised them in troops. He understood the monkeys' intentions, and the monkeys grasped his heart. He reduced his household's provisions to satisfy the monkeys' desires. Soon, supplies ran short, and he planned to limit their food. Fearing the monkeys would not submit, he first deceived them, saying: 'I will give you chestnuts—three in the morning and four in the evening. Is that enough?' The monkeys all rose in anger. Then he said: 'I will give you chestnuts—four in the morning and three in the evening. Is that enough?' The monkeys all prostrated themselves in delight.

All things are ensnared by their own abilities and vulgarity—this is no different. The sage ensnares the multitude of fools with wisdom, just as Ju Gong ensnared the monkeys with his cunning. Names and realities remain unharmed, yet their joy and anger are manipulated. This passage aligns with the *Zhuangzi*'s "Equalizing Things" chapter, though the wording differs slightly.

"Three in the morning and four in the evening—first little, then much; four in the morning and three in the evening—first much, then little, yet the total remains seven. *Neng bi* (ability and vulgarity) refers to wisdom and folly. *Wu* (things) means all things can ensnare one another. The sage ensnares the multitude of fools with wisdom, meaning he inspires, transforms, and guides them without their awareness. The *Zhuangzi* uses this as a metaphor for the absence of right and wrong, though its intent differs here.

Master Ji Shengzi reared fighting cocks for King Xuan of Zhou. After ten days, the king asked: 'Are the cocks ready to fight?' He replied: 'Not yet—they are still vain and reliant on their qi.' After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: 'Not yet—they still respond to echoes and shadows.' After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: 'Not yet—they still glare fiercely and bristle with qi.' After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: 'Nearly so. Though other cocks may crow, they show no change. They appear like wooden cocks—their virtue is complete. No other cock dares respond; they turn and flee. To react to sounds or shadows is to have one’s heart still moved by external things. "Glare fiercely and bristle with qi" means their spirit and qi are already full, yet they glare without moving. Initially, they were "vain and reliant on their qi," meaning their qi was still external; here, "glare fiercely and bristle with qi" means their qi is internal. The word *ji* (fierce) carries the sense of anger—direct gazing—yet differs from the commoner’s hand-on-sword, fierce glare. When they appear like wooden cocks, their spirit and qi are both whole. This speaks of the art of guarding qi, using the cock as a metaphor.

Hui Ang visited King Kang of Song. The king stamped his feet, coughed, and spoke rapidly: 'What pleases me are courage and strength—I do not delight in benevolence and righteousness.'"

"What instruction do you bring to me?" asked the guest.

Hui Ang replied, "I possess a Way here: though a man may be brave, he cannot pierce you; though he may be strong, he cannot strike you. Does Your Majesty not wish to hear of it?"

The King of Song said, "Excellent—this is what I desire to hear."

Hui Ang continued, "That one cannot pierce you, that one cannot strike you—this is still a form of humiliation. I possess another Way here: though a man may be brave, he dare not pierce you; though he may be strong, he dare not strike you. That he dare not—this is not because he lacks the will. I possess yet another Way here: he fundamentally lacks the will. One who lacks such will has no mind for love or profit. I possess still another Way here: that all men and women under heaven joyfully desire to love and benefit you. This surpasses mere courage and strength—it is the highest of the four grades. Does Your Majesty not wish to attain it?"

The King of Song said, "This is what I desire to obtain."

Hui Ang replied, "Kong and Mo have already achieved this. Kong Qiu and Mo Di, though they had no land to rule as lords nor office to lead as ministers, yet all men and women under heaven craned their necks and stood on tiptoe, yearning to be at peace and benefit under them. Now, Your Majesty is a ruler of ten thousand chariots—if you truly possess this will, then all within your four borders will receive its benefit. You would surpass Kong and Mo by far."

The King of Song had no reply. Hui Ang hastened out.

The King of Song said to his attendants, "How persuasive the guest was in convincing me!"

This passage resembles somewhat the "Discourse on Swords" in the *Zhuangzi*.

That one cannot pierce you, that one cannot strike you—is this not still contention, with moments of victory or defeat? That one dare not pierce, dare not strike—this still harbors a mind for competition. These are two grades. To fundamentally lack the will is to have no mind for contention with me—this is yet another grade. That all joyfully desire to love and benefit me—this is to subdue the world through nurturing others with goodness, ascending through three grades to reach the highest Way. Thus it is called the highest of the four grades.

This is the work of our sages, yet to speak of Kong and Mo together reflects the discourse of scholars after the Spring and Autumn period.

*Diefu* means stamping the foot; *qingkai* means speaking loudly.

"Swift speech" refers to rapid words—both describe the appearance of his anger.

"One so eloquent" implies deception through his rhetorical skill, meaning this guest possesses great persuasive ability and thus can convince me with his arguments.

*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Yanzhai Kouyi*, Scroll Two, concludes.

---

*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Yanzhai Kouyi*, Scroll Three

By Yanzhai Lin Xiang

**The Third Chapter: King Mu of Zhou**

During the reign of King Mu of Zhou, a *huaren* from the lands of the Four Poles arrived. He could enter water and fire, pass through metal and stone, reverse mountains and rivers, and relocate cities. Riding the void, he did not fall; striking the solid, he did not falter.

**The Third Chapter: King Mu of Zhou**

His transformations were boundless, beyond all measure. Having altered the forms of things, he could also shift a man’s thoughts. King Mu revered him as a spirit and served him as a lord. He yielded his own chambers for the *huaren*’s dwelling, offered the three sacrificial beasts in tribute, and selected female musicians to entertain him.

The *huaren* deemed the king’s palace too crude to inhabit, his banquets too rank to consume, and his consorts too foul to embrace. Thus, King Mu ordered a new construction, sparing no artistry in earth and timber, in ochre and whitewash. The five treasuries were emptied before the tower’s completion—soaring a thousand *ren* high, overlooking the peaks of Zhongnan, it was named the Terrace of Central Heaven. Virgins of Zheng and Wei, maidens fair and supple, were anointed with fragrant balms, their brows painted, their hairpins and rings adorned. Clad in fine silks, trailing skirts of even weave, their faces powdered white and eyes lined black, jade pendants at their waists, they filled the halls with orchid and pollia. The melodies of *Bearing Clouds*, *Six Radiance*, *Nine Shao*, and *Morning Dew* resounded to delight him. Each month, jade robes were presented; each dawn, jade delicacies were offered. Yet the *huaren* remained discontent, and only with reluctance did he deign to ascend.

Before long, he invited the king to wander with him. Grasping the *huaren*’s sleeve, the king ascended, halting only at Central Heaven, then proceeding to the *huaren*’s own palace. That palace was wrought of gold and silver, strung with pearls and jade, rising above clouds and rain, its foundations unseen. Gazing upon it, one beheld only a mass of layered clouds. All that met the eye and ear, all that the nose inhaled or the tongue tasted, was beyond the mortal realm. The king truly believed it to be the Clear Capital, the Purple Tenuity, the Grand Heaven, the Vast Music—the abode of the Emperor. Yet when he looked down, its halls and pavilions appeared no more than heaped clods and piled thatch.

The king, dwelling there for decades, forgot his own kingdom. The *huaren* again invited him to wander. Wherever they went, neither sun nor moon shone above, nor rivers nor seas stretched below. The play of light dazzled the king’s eyes until he could no longer see; the clash of sounds confounded his ears until he could no longer hear. His limbs and organs trembled, unsteady; his mind wandered, his spirit fled. He begged the *huaren* to return. The *huaren* complied, and the king fell as if into the void.

When he awoke, he sat where he had before, his attendants unchanged. Before him, the wine had yet to clear, the dishes had yet to spoil. Only a moment had passed. The king asked whence he had come, and his attendants replied, “The king was lost in silent contemplation.”

From this, King Mu was disoriented for three months before recovering. When he questioned the *huaren* again, the *huaren* said, “I wandered with Your Majesty in spirit—how could your form have moved? Moreover, what difference was there between the place we visited and your own palace? What difference between our journey and your own gardens? Your Majesty mistook the familiar for the strange, doubted what was but a fleeting absence. How can one grasp the full measure of transformation, swift or slow, in its every aspect?”

The *huaren* was a master of illusions. Entering water and fire, piercing metal and stone—these were alterations of form; journeying with the king to the Emperor’s abode was a shifting of perception. *Xinglou* and *shan’e* refer to foul odors. *E’nü miao* describes graceful beauty; *manmi*, slender elegance. *Axi* is fine silk; *qiwan*, even-woven silk. *Zhi* is a fragrant herb; *ruo*, pollia. *Bearing Clouds* was the name of the Yellow Emperor’s music; *Six Radiance*, of Emperor Yu’s; *Morning Dew*, of Tang’s. Jade robes and jade food signify their preciousness. *She* (pronounced *shi*) means discontent. “Unseen foundations” describes the lack of visible base. “Like layered clouds” conveys their multitude. The Clear Capital and Purple Tenuity are celestial palaces; the Grand Heaven and Vast Music, heavenly music. *Leikuai* means piled earth; *jisu*, piled thatch—from above, the palace seemed small and humble. Dazzled by light and confounded by sound, he trembled in fear, his spirit unsettled. *Yunxu* describes falling through emptiness. Wine is finest when rich; if left, it grows thin. “Dishes not spoiled” means they remained fresh. “Silent contemplation” refers to seated meditation. This all occurred in an instant—like Ye Fashan’s journey with Emperor Ming to the Jade Bridge.

The spirit may wander while the body remains still—such is the art of the illusionist. To mistake the familiar for the strange, to doubt what was but a fleeting absence—this is to forget that the present is constant. How can one fully grasp the forms of transformation, whether swift or slow? *Xuji* refers to duration; *mo*, to form. “Fleeting absence” is akin to forgetting. The king was greatly pleased.

He neglected state affairs, took no delight in his ministers and concubines, and indulged in distant travels. Commanding the chariot drawn by the Eight Steeds, with *Siyin* and *Hualiu* as the right and left inner horses, *Chiji* and *Baijian* as the right and left outer steeds—these are their names and meanings. For the lead chariot, *Zaofu* served as driver, while *Li Qishang* stood at the right. The second chariot had *Quhuang* and *Yulun* as the right and left inner horses, *Daoli* and *Shanzi* as the left and right outer steeds, with *Baiyao* as its master, *Canbai* as driver, and *Benjie* at the right. Racing a thousand *li*, they arrived at the land of the *Jusou* people. The *Jusou* offered the blood of white swans for the king to drink and the fresh milk of cows and horses to wash his feet, as well as those of the two chariots’ attendants. After drinking, they departed and lodged at the slopes of Kunlun, on the sunny side of the Red Water.

On another day, they ascended the heights of Kunlun to behold the palace of the Yellow Emperor, sealing it with a monument to bequeath to later generations. Then they paid homage to the Queen Mother of the West, feasting with her upon the Jasper Pool. The Queen Mother sang a song for the king, and he joined in harmony—its words were mournful. Gazing upon the place where the sun sets, covering ten thousand *li* in a single day, the king sighed: "Alas! I, alone, am deficient in virtue yet sated with pleasure. Will later generations recount my faults?" King Mu was nearly a divine being—able to exhaust the joys of his own lifetime, like an ancestor of a hundred years. The world regarded him as having ascended in transcendence.

This account is detailed in *The Travels of King Mu*. Han Yu’s *Stele Inscription for the Temple of King Xu Yan* also references it, and the *Zuozhuan*’s poem "Some like gold, some like jade" pertains to the same matter. *Silu* is another name for *Hualiu*. *Baijian*, *Li Shang*, *Quhuang*, *Yulun*, *Daoli*, *Shanzi*, and *Baiyao* are all names of horses. Liu Zongyuan’s *Disquisition on the Eight Steeds* depicts their forms as even more bizarre—yet it remains uncertain which version is true or false. The land of the *Jusou* is akin to Kunlun and the Red Water. Offering swan’s blood as drink and washing feet with the milk of cows and horses—today’s northern barbarians make wine from mare’s milk, which is of the same kind. The "two chariots" refer to the king’s two carriages. "Another day" means the following day. "Sealing" is like the feng and shan sacrifices. "Paid homage" means to visit. "Feasting" means to entertain with wine. The Queen Mother’s song is *The White Cloud Poem*. "The place where the sun sets" refers to Yan Mountain. "Deficient in virtue" means his conduct was wanting. "Sated" means fulfilled. Though his virtue was wanting, his pleasure was complete—yet he feared later generations would recount it as his fault. The *Qi Zhao* ode speaks of "exerting the people’s strength without the heart of drunkenness and satiety," which conveys the same meaning. To take such joy throughout one’s life, living a hundred years before passing—this the world regarded as "ascending in transcendence," believing him to have died, though in truth he did not.

The purport of this passage is that beyond the world lies vast emptiness, yet boundless joy still exists. Even the abode of emperors and kings is not worthy of envy. People, limited by what their ears and eyes perceive, covet wealth and nobility, unaware that in the eyes of heaven and humanity, such things are but trifles.

Lao Chengzi studied illusion under Master Yin Wen for three years without receiving instruction. Lao Chengzi asked to be told his faults and sought to withdraw, but Master Yin Wen bowed and led him into a chamber, dismissing his attendants before speaking: "When Laozi departed for the west, he turned to me and said: ‘The qi of the living, the forms of beings—all are illusion. That which begins with creation, that which transforms through yin and yang—this is called life, this is called death. To exhaust numbers and comprehend change, to follow form in its shifting—this is called transformation, this is called illusion. The Creator’s craft is subtle, its work profound—truly difficult to fathom or exhaust. But that which follows form is manifest in its artifice, shallow in its work—thus it arises and vanishes in an instant. Only when one knows that illusion and transformation are no different from life and death can one begin to study illusion. You and I are also illusion—what need is there to learn?’"

The purport of this passage is that the arts of worldly illusion and the principles of creation’s transformations in life and death share the same technique. Lao Chengzi returned home and, pondering Master Yin Wen’s words deeply for three months, attained mastery over presence and absence, freely manipulating the four seasons. In winter, he summoned thunder; in summer, he conjured ice. Birds ran, beasts flew—yet throughout his life, he never revealed his art, so the world never transmitted it. Though Lao Chengzi did not fully grasp the technique, through deep reflection he awakened to it, moving effortlessly between being and non-being. Thus it is said: "He mastered presence and absence." *Fanxiao* means to overturn and examine—an allusion to transformation. "Manipulating the four seasons" means altering the nodes of yin and yang. "Summoning thunder in winter" is to transform yin into yang; "conjuring ice in summer" is to transform yang into yin. Birds belong to yang, beasts to yin—thus birds are light, beasts heavy. By altering yin and yang, he made birds run and beasts fly. As his art left no trace, the world never transmitted it.

Liezi said: "Those skilled in transformation follow a subtle and unobtrusive Way, yet their achievements are no different from ordinary people’s. The virtue of the Five Emperors and the accomplishments of the Three Kings were not necessarily the result of wisdom or strength—some were achieved through transformation. Who can fathom this?" "Subtle and unobtrusive" means to employ it silently, unseen by others. Though the Way may be invisible, its effects are no different from human actions. The transformations of the Five Emperors and Three Kings were like the illusions of Lao Chengzi and Master Yin Wen—speaking of their unfathomable mystery.

Waking has eight signs; dreaming, six conditions. What are the eight signs? The first is the familiar; the second, action; the third, gain; the fourth, loss; the fifth, sorrow; the sixth, joy; the seventh, life; the eighth, death. These eight signs are what form engages.

The *Rites of Zhou* speaks of six types of dreams, and here too six dreams are discussed—yet first the eight signs of waking are addressed. "Familiar" refers to affairs, the myriad matters of human life. "Action" denotes the activities undertaken during the day. "Gain," "loss," "sorrow," "joy," "life," and "death" are shared by all that possess form, hence it is said: "what form engages." *Engages* means *responds*—the response of resonance.

What are the six conditions? The first is the *proper dream*; the second, the *startling dream*; the third, the *thought dream*; the fourth, the *waking dream*; the fifth, the *joyful dream*; the sixth, the *fearful dream*. These six arise from the intercourse of spirit. The six conditions of dreams align with those in the *Rites of Zhou*.

Within the human heart lies emptiness, numinous awareness; when affairs bear omens, they appear in dreams—this is the *proper dream*, the dream of foreshadowing. The *startling dream* is one in which one is startled awake. The *thought dream* arises from what one has been pondering. The *waking dream* presents matters of waking life during sleep. The *joyful dream* stems from what brings joy. The *fearful dream* arises from what causes anxiety or dread. Fear and startlement differ, though the commentary in the *Rites of Zhou* makes no such distinction. All these are unified in one’s own spirit, hence it is said: "what spirit intermingles." *Intermingles* means intermingling with external realms.

Those who do not recognize the origin of responsive change are perplexed when events arise, unaware of their cause; those who recognize the origin of responsive change understand the cause when events arise. Knowing their cause, one remains unperturbed. The waxing and waning, the ebb and flow of this singular body all communicate with Heaven and Earth, resonating with the myriad kinds. The mutual resonance of self and other gives rise to transformations, hence it is called *responsive change*. *Events* refer to the eight signs. *Their cause* means all arise from the heart.

People, not knowing that the origin of responsive change begins in the unified heart, harbor doubts and startlements. With understanding, there is no perplexity, no perturbation. Waxing and waning, ebb and flow all follow a single principle, hence it is said: "the waxing and waning, ebb and flow of this singular body." So too with Heaven and Earth, so too with the myriad things. As the *Analects* says: "At forty, one is free from perplexity"—this is that realm.

When yin qi is strong, one dreams of wading through great waters in terror; when yang qi is strong, one dreams of traversing great fires and being scorched; when both yin and yang are strong, one dreams of life and death. These three statements also appear in medical texts. From this, it is evident that dreams emerge from one’s own heart. *Scorched* describes the intensity of fire. Life is yang; death, yin.

When overly sated, one dreams of giving; when ravenously hungry, one dreams of taking. *Giving* means bestowing upon others; *taking*, seizing from them. Such dreams arise from desire in the heart, just as thirst leads to dreams of drinking. Thus, those afflicted by floating emptiness dream of soaring; those burdened by sinking fullness dream of drowning. These are maladies of the heart.

Sleeping with a sash around the waist, one dreams of serpents; if a bird clutches one’s hair in flight, one dreams of flying. The sash and the bird are seen while awake, yet transform in dreams. When yin prevails, one dreams of fire; when illness approaches, one dreams of food. Dwelling in darkness, the mind craves light—hence yin gives rise to dreams of fire. With stomach qi depleted, illness prompts dreams of eating. All emerge from this heart.

Drinkers grieve; dancers weep. Those who dream of drinking may harbor hidden sorrows; those who dream of dancing may conceal tears. Dreams and waking states often stand in opposition. Dream-interpretation manuals abound with such cases.

Master Liezi said: "Spiritual encounter produces dreams; physical contact, events." Thus, daytime thoughts become nighttime dreams—spirit and form meet. When the spirit is concentrated, thoughts and dreams dissolve of themselves. Truly awake, one speaks not; truly dreaming, one comprehends not—these are the comings and goings of transformative things.

The true persons of antiquity forgot themselves in waking and slept without dreams—how could this be mere empty talk? Daytime visions arise from physical encounter; nighttime dreams, from spiritual encounter. Concentration stills the spirit; stillness banishes thought; without thought, there are no dreams. Yet when Gaozong dreamed of Fu Yue or Confucius dreamed of the Duke of Zhou, these were not dreams of mere thought.

In truth, the truly awake remain silent, still and serene. The truly dreaming do not grasp, taking dreams for reality. The flux of transformation—this is the interplay of dream and waking. Unaware of this principle, people dismiss the ancient true persons’ self-forgetting wakefulness and dreamless sleep as idle chatter. How could they fathom the true person’s way?

In waking, one forgets all attachment to the world. With the heart unattached, emptiness reigns, unity prevails—how then could sleep bring dreams? The Buddhist saying "dream and waking are one" captures this perfectly. In his letters, Dahui discusses Gaozong’s dream of Fu Yue, Confucius’ dream of the Duke of Zhou, and the Buddha’s dream of golden drums—his elucidation of the unity of dream and waking is most illuminating.

At the southwestern edge of the farthest reaches lies a land called the Country of Ancient Wilderness. Its borders are unknown, for yin and yang qi do not mingle there, and cold and heat remain undifferentiated.

The light of sun and moon does not shine there, so day and night remain undifferentiated. Its people neither eat nor wear clothing, yet sleep often—waking once every fifty days. To them, what occurs in dreams is real, while what is seen in waking is illusory.

The Country of Ancient Wilderness is but a name. Without yin and yang, without sun and moon, its people neither clothe nor feed themselves, sleeping much and waking but once in fifty days. Thus, they take dreams as real and waking as false. This illustrates the idea of the ordinary being intermittent while the transient seems nonexistent—if one were to dream constantly, the brief moments of waking would instead appear illusory.

The Central Kingdom, as the four seas call it, spans north and south of the Yellow River, east and west of Mount Tai, extending over ten thousand *li*. Its yin and yang are measured precisely, bringing one cold and one hot season; the division of dusk and dawn is clear, yielding one day and one night. Its people possess both wisdom and folly. The myriad things flourish, and skills and arts abound. There are rulers and subjects in governance, rites and laws in maintenance. Their deeds and undertakings are beyond reckoning.

A waking and a sleep—thus they take what waking does as real and what dreams show as false. *Qi* (齐) means "center," and the Central Kingdom is also called *Qizhou*. This passage speaks of how the people of the Central Kingdom likewise regard waking as real and dreams as illusory.

*Shendu* (审度) means to measure with precision; *fencha* (分察) means to distinguish clearly. In the northeasternmost corner of the earth lies a land called the State of Fuluo. Its climate is perpetually warm, bathed in the lingering light of the sun and moon, and no fine crops grow in its soil. Its people eat grass roots and tree fruits, knowing nothing of cooked food. Their nature is fierce and unyielding; the strong trample the weak, prizing dominance over righteousness. They run swiftly and rest little, ever awake and never sleeping.

The State of Fuluo is also an allegory. The alternating glow of the sun and moon illuminates it without end, so its land knows no darkness. As the *Tang Treatise* describes a place where "roasted sheep’s spleen is eaten while the sun still shines," there may well be such lands in the world—who can say? With sunlight and moonlight ever present, its people remain perpetually awake. This suggests that the people of the Central Kingdom take only what they perceive by day as real and what they dream by night as false, unaware that within the Six Directions there exist such lands where one cannot simply trust what the eyes and ears encounter. All this is meant to broaden the narrow views of the common world.

In the Zhou dynasty, a grandee named Yin amassed vast wealth. His subordinates toiled ceaselessly, rising before dawn and resting not even at dusk. Among them was an old laborer, his strength spent, yet pressed to work ever harder. By day, he groaned as he labored; by night, exhausted, he slept deeply, his spirit scattered. Night after night, he dreamed of being a ruler—lording over the people, governing the state, roaming palaces and gardens, indulging every desire, his joy beyond compare. Upon waking, he returned to servitude.

When others consoled him for his toil, the laborer said: "A man’s life spans a hundred years, divided equally between day and night. By day, I am a slave, and though it is bitter, it is but half my lot. By night, I am a lord, and my joy knows no bounds. What cause have I for resentment?"

Yin, meanwhile, was consumed by worldly affairs, his thoughts fixed on his estate. Body and mind wearied, he too slept deeply at night. Night after night, he dreamed of being a servant—running errands, performing every task, enduring scoldings and beatings without end. In his sleep, he muttered and groaned, waking only at dawn. Distressed, he sought counsel from a friend.

His friend said: "Your rank brings you honor, your wealth surpasses others—you are far ahead of most. Yet by night, you dream of servitude, a natural balance of suffering and ease. If you sought to control both waking and dreaming, how could that be achieved?"

Upon hearing this, Yin eased the laborers’ burdens and reduced his own worries. His ailments gradually subsided.

*Xixi* (昔昔) means "nightly," referring to the laborer’s nightly dreams of kingship. *Zhong* (锺) means "to gather," as in concentrating one’s thoughts on managing one’s estate. *Anyi* (啽呓) means sleep-talking. *Bing* (并) means "all," and *jian* (间) means "ease."

He eased the laborers’ tasks and lessened his own concerns, and so both men’s ailments gradually subsided.

This passage uses waking and dreaming to illustrate the nature of suffering and joy—its words carry deep meaning.

There was a man of Zheng who was gathering firewood in the wild when he encountered a startled deer. He pursued it, struck it down, and killed it. Fearing others would see, he hastily concealed it in a dry moat, covering it with plantain leaves, his delight overwhelming. Soon after, he forgot where he had hidden it and took it all for a dream. As he walked along, he chanted about the event. A passerby overheard him, followed his words, and took the deer. Upon returning home, the man told his wife: “A firewood-gatherer dreamed he had caught a deer but forgot where it was. Now I have it—he must truly have been dreaming!” His wife said: “Are you the one who dreamed the firewood-gatherer caught the deer? How could there have been a firewood-gatherer? Now that you truly have the deer, is your dream the real one?” The man replied: “I possess the deer—why trouble myself over whether he dreamed or I dreamed?”

The firewood-gatherer returned home, discontented at losing the deer. That night, he truly dreamed of where he had hidden it and of the one who had taken it. At daybreak, he followed his dream and found the man. They took their dispute to the magistrate. The magistrate said: “At first, you truly caught the deer but mistakenly called it a dream; now you truly dreamed of the deer but mistakenly call it real. He truly took your deer and now contends with you over it. Your wife also claims that if you dreamed of someone else’s deer, then no one truly obtained it. Since you now possess the deer, let it be divided between you.” He reported this to the Lord of Zheng, who exclaimed: “Ah! Is the magistrate about to dream of dividing someone else’s deer?” He consulted his minister, who said: “Whether dreaming or waking, this is beyond my ability to discern. To distinguish between the two, only the Yellow Emperor or Confucius could do so. Now that they are gone, who can settle the matter? Let us simply follow the magistrate’s judgment.”

This passage likewise uses waking and dreaming to show how truth and illusion cannot be fixed—its argument carries its own depth.

In his middle years, Hua of Yangli in Song suffered from forgetfulness. What he took in the morning he forgot by evening; what he gave in the evening he forgot by morning. On the road, he forgot to walk; at home, he forgot to sit. He no longer recognized past or present. His entire household was distressed. He consulted diviners, but no oracle could be cast; he sought shamans, but no prayers could restrain it; he saw physicians, but no treatment could cure him. A scholar from Lu offered his services, claiming he could heal him. Hua’s wife and children offered half their property in exchange for the remedy.
