The scholar said: “This is not a matter for divination, nor for prayer, nor for medicine. I shall attempt to transform his mind and alter his thoughts—perhaps there may yet be hope.” Thereupon, he exposed Hua to the elements until he sought clothing; starved him until he sought food; confined him in darkness until he sought light.

The scholar joyfully told Hua’s son: “The illness can be cured!” Yet he added: “My method is secret, passed down through generations and never disclosed to others. Let me dismiss all attendants and remain alone with him in seclusion for seven days.” They complied, unaware of what he did, yet after years of affliction, the malady vanished in a single morning.

When Hua awoke to clarity, he flew into a rage, cast out his wife, punished his children, and brandished a spear to chase away the scholar. The people of Song seized him and demanded an explanation. Hua said: “Before, I was lost in forgetfulness—vast and boundless, unaware whether heaven and earth existed. Now, suddenly recalling the past, the tangled threads of decades—life and death, gain and loss, sorrow and joy, love and hate—all surge forth. I fear that in the future, the chaos of existence and loss, sorrow and joy, love and hate will again disturb my heart. Could I ever regain even a moment’s forgetfulness?”

Zigong heard of this and found it strange. He reported it to Confucius, who said: “This is not something within your grasp.” Then, turning to Yan Hui, he instructed him to record it.

*Toxin* means suffering; *poison* means bitterness. The practices of divination, shamanism, and medicine persist even today, showing that the nature of people past and present is not so different. *No oracle could be cast* means it did not align with the hexagrams. *No prayers could restrain it* means they took it as a curse but could not suppress it. *No treatment could cure it* means it was incurable. *Offered his services* means he recommended himself as capable of healing the illness. *Transform his mind and alter his thoughts* refers to this being a malady of the heart, untreatable by other methods. *Sought clothing, sought food, sought light*—these show that his heart still retained some awareness. *Remained alone with him without others present*—thus, no one knew what treatment was applied.

His anger upon awakening stemmed from the realization that worldly affairs could burden his heart, making ignorance and forgetfulness preferable. For ordinary people, worries over joy and sorrow, gain and loss, life and death, love and hate disturb the heart. Only those who follow the Way and delight in forgetfulness escape this; otherwise, the illness of forgetfulness is better than clarity.

The passage refrains from full explanation, merely stating, “This is not something within your grasp”—another subtle point.

Among the Feng clan of Qin, there was a son who was clever in youth but developed a delusional illness in adulthood. He mistook singing for weeping, white for black, feasts for rot, sweetness for bitterness, and wrong for right. Whatever he perceived—heaven and earth, the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—all were distorted. The Yang clan told his father: “The gentlemen of Lu possess many arts. Might they cure him? Why not seek them out?”

His father traveled to Lu but passed through Chen, where he encountered Lao Dan. He described his son’s condition. Lao Dan said: “How do you know your son is deluded? Today, all under heaven are confused by right and wrong, blinded by profit and harm. Those sharing his affliction are many, yet none awaken. Moreover, one man’s delusion cannot overturn a household; one household’s delusion cannot overturn a village; one village’s delusion cannot overturn a state; one state’s delusion cannot overturn the world. If all under heaven were deluded, who could overturn it? If all people’s hearts were like your son’s, you would be the deluded one. Joy and sorrow, sound and color, scent and taste, right and wrong—who can correct them? Furthermore, my words may themselves be delusion. How much more so the gentlemen of Lu, steeped in delusion—how could they resolve another’s delusion? Spare your provisions and hasten home.”

This passage on delusional illness inverts the earlier meaning of the illness of forgetfulness.

*Overturn* means to disturb. A household is not overturned by one man’s delusion; a village is not overturned by one household’s delusion. This means that when the deluded are few and the clear-sighted many, confusion cannot prevail. But if all under heaven were deluded, the undeluded would be seen as afflicted. The implication is that today’s people are all deluded by profit and desire, ignorant of the Way, and thus regard those who follow the Way as deluded.

*Steeped in delusion* means deeply deluded. *Spare* means to discard or waste; do not squander your provisions.

A man of Yan was born in Yan, raised in Chu, and in old age returned to his homeland. Passing through Jin, his companions deceived him, pointing to a city and saying: “This is the city of Yan.” His expression darkened. They pointed to a shrine and said: “This is your village’s shrine.”

Then he sighed deeply. Pointing to a house, they said: “This is your ancestors’ dwelling.” He wept silently. Pointing to a mound, they said: “This is your ancestors’ grave.” The man could not restrain his sobs. His companions burst into laughter, saying: “We were only jesting with you—this is but the land of Jin.”

The man was overcome with shame. When he at last reached Yan and truly beheld the city and shrine of Yan, truly saw his ancestors’ dwelling and grave, his sorrow was all the fainter. This passage illustrates how, when the human heart lacks true perception, it may mistake the false for the real and the real for the false.

*Fainter* means nonexistent; *his sorrow was all the fainter* means he no longer grieved. Judging by the incisiveness of this passage, it must belong to the original *Liezi*.

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Oral Exegesis of Yuanzhai, Scroll Three* concludes here.

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Oral Exegesis of Yuanzhai, Scroll Four*
By Lin Xiang of Yuanzhai

**Scroll Four of *The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Oral Exegesis of Yuanzhai***

**Confucius, Part Four**

Confucius was at leisure in his chambers when Zigong entered to attend him. Seeing his master’s troubled countenance, Zigong dared not inquire and went out to tell Yan Hui. Yan Hui took up his zither and began to sing.

Confucius heard him and summoned Yan Hui inside, asking: “Why are you alone so joyful?”

Yan Hui replied: “Why are you alone so troubled?”

Confucius said: “Speak first of your own thoughts.”

Yan Hui answered: “Long ago, I heard you say: ‘Those who delight in Heaven and know fate are therefore without sorrow.’ This is why I am joyful.”

Confucius, his expression turning grave, paused for a moment before saying: “Did I truly say such a thing? You have misunderstood. That was my teaching of former days—let me now correct it with my present words. You merely grasp that delighting in Heaven and knowing fate brings no sorrow, yet you do not know that delighting in Heaven and knowing fate entails a sorrow greater still.

“Now I shall tell you the truth: Cultivating the self, accepting poverty or prosperity, recognizing that going and coming are not of the self, and banishing disorder from the heart’s reckonings—this is what you call delighting in Heaven and knowing fate without sorrow. But in the past, when I studied the *Odes* and *Documents*, rectified rites and music, I did so to govern all under Heaven and bequeath it to later ages—not merely to cultivate the self or govern the state of Lu. Yet the lord and ministers of Lu daily lost their proper order; benevolence and righteousness further declined; human nature grew ever more shallow. If the Dao cannot be practiced in a single state or in the present age, what hope is there for all under Heaven and for future generations? If the Dao fails even in one state and in the present time, what can be done for all under Heaven and the ages to come? Only now do I realize that the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music cannot save the world from chaos, yet I still do not know the means to reform it—this is the sorrow of those who delight in Heaven and know fate.

“Nevertheless, I have grasped it at last. The joy and knowledge of which I speak are not what the ancients called joy and knowledge. True joy and true knowledge lie in neither joy nor knowledge. Thus, there is nothing that is not joy, nothing that is not known, nothing that is not sorrow, nothing that is not done. Why then abandon the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music? Why seek to reform them? To take joy in joy is to have a heart. True joy is joy without a heart that can contain it.”

Yan Hui bowed northward and said: “I, too, have grasped it.” He went out and told Zigong.

Zigong stood in bewilderment, lost in himself. Returning home, he brooded for seven days without sleep or food, until his bones stood out from his flesh. Yan Hui went again to enlighten him, and only then did Zigong return to Confucius’ gate, where he strummed the zither, sang, and recited the classics all day without cease.

This passage shifts three times in meaning: first, it says that delighting in Heaven and knowing fate brings no sorrow; next, that those who delight in Heaven and know fate may yet feel sorrow; and finally, that to know sorrow and joy is not as good as to know neither. Its intent is that to have sorrow and joy is not as good as to have neither; to recognize sorrow and joy as sorrow and joy is not as good as to recognize neither. Such is its general purport. Yet it conveys this through indirection and contrast, its style far removed from that of the *Zhuangzi*. It is doubtful this belongs to the original *Liezi*.

*Yin* means to be deeply immersed, to ponder with intense absorption.

A minister of Chen paid a visit to Lu and privately met with the house of Shusun. Shusun said: “There is a sage in our state.”

The minister of Chen asked, "Is it not Kong Qiu?"
Shusun replied, "It is."
"How do you know he is a sage?"
Shusun said, "I once heard Yan Hui say: 'Kong Qiu can set aside the mind and employ the form.'"

The minister of Chen said, "In our state, too, there is a sage—did you not know?"
Shusun asked, "Of whom do you speak as a sage?"
The minister replied, "Among the disciples of Lao Dan, there is one named Kang Cangzi who has attained the Dao of Lao Dan. He can see with his ears and hear with his eyes."

The Lord of Lu, hearing this, was greatly astonished and sent his highest minister with rich gifts to summon him. Kang Cangzi accepted the invitation and arrived.

"To set aside the mind and employ the form" means to be without mind and forget one’s form, so that even when acting, one does not know it as action. "To see with the ears and hear with the eyes" is the teaching that the six senses share a single source.

The Buddhist tradition takes sound as the object of *Guanyin*’s observation. Indeed, the Japanese monk Tōkō, in his *Vimalakīrti Praise*, composed a *Praise to Guanyin* with this very meaning. Its verses read:

All the manifold sounds of the world,
The multitude hears with the ear, not the eye.
Only this great being’s eyes can perceive,
Yet in the realm of the eye, nothing is grasped.
So too with ear, nose, tongue, body, and mind.
Excellent! The heart penetrates the emptiness of the ten directions,
The six senses mutually manifest thus.

The explanation is found in the *Recorded Sayings, General Exposition*, section fifteen, where it is expounded with great clarity.

The Lord of Lu, speaking humbly, questioned him.

Kang Cangzi said: "Those who transmit this speak falsely. I can see and hear without using eyes and ears, yet I cannot alter the function of eyes and ears."

The Lord of Lu said: "This is even more extraordinary—how does this Way operate? I remain eager to hear it."

Seeing and hearing without eyes and ears is what *Zhuangzi* meant by "the senses cease, yet the spirit moves." Hearing with *qi*, hearing with the heart—this too expresses the same idea. Though one does not use eyes and ears to see and hear, the function of eyes and ears remains the same as in others. Thus it is said: "One cannot alter the function of eyes and ears." "Even more extraordinary" means that this is yet more wondrous.

Kang Cangzi said: "My body aligns with the heart, the heart aligns with *qi*, *qi* aligns with spirit, and spirit aligns with nothingness. Whether something is as subtle as a single blade of grass or as faint as a single sound—whether distant in the eight wilds or near at the edge of one’s brows—whatever comes to disturb me, I will know it. Yet I do not know whether this awareness arises from the seven apertures and four limbs, or from the heart’s knowledge of the six viscera. It is simply self-knowing."

"Body," "heart," "*qi*," and "spirit" all return to nothingness—this is the function of no-mind. "As subtle as a single blade" means even the tiniest visible thing. "As faint as a single sound" means even the slightest audible thing. These eight characters are well placed. When things come to disturb me, I know them—this is stillness undisturbed, yet responsive to stimuli; it is the ability to name things as they arrive and act as events arise.

The seven apertures, four limbs, heart, and six viscera may perceive and know, yet I remain unaware of them—this is the body aligning with the heart, the heart with *qi*, *qi* with spirit, and spirit with nothingness. "It is simply self-knowing" means that though I know myself, there are things beyond words.

The Lord of Lu was greatly pleased. Later, he told Confucius, who smiled but did not answer. This smile without answer is what was meant earlier by "the Master could do it but did not act." The implication is that though the Master knew this Way, he did not speak of it to others, hence his smile and silence.

The Grand Steward of Shang asked Confucius: "Is Qiu a sage?"
Confucius replied: "How would Qiu dare claim sageliness? Yet Qiu is one who has broadly studied and retains much knowledge."

The Grand Steward asked: "Were the Three Kings sages?"
Confucius said: "The Three Kings were skilled in employing wisdom and courage—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know."

He asked: "Were the Five Emperors sages?"
Confucius said: "The Five Emperors were skilled in employing benevolence and righteousness—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know."

He asked: "Were the Three Sovereigns sages?"
Confucius said: "The Three Sovereigns were skilled in following the times—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know."

The Grand Steward was astonished and said: "Then who is a sage?"
Confucius, his expression shifting, paused before saying: "In the West there is a sage who governs without disorder, is trusted without speaking, and transforms without teaching. So vast is he that the people cannot name him. Qiu suspects he may be a sage—yet does not know if he truly is one, or truly is not."

The Grand Steward fell silent, pondering to himself: "Has Kong Qiu deceived me?"

This passage seems to suggest that Buddhist teachings already existed at the time, attributed to the Master’s name in reverence. One who surpasses the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors—who else could this be but the Buddha? Though Buddhist scriptures entered China during the reign of Emperor Ming of Han, their doctrines had long since spread throughout the world.

"Governs without disorder" means his engagement with the world transcends order and chaos. "Trusted without speaking" and "transforms without teaching" describe how sincerity moves others. "Does not know if he truly is a sage or truly is not" conveys a tone of reverence, yet the ambiguity suggests that while such teachings existed, they were not yet widely practiced—hence the refusal to speak definitively.

"Fell silent, pondering to himself: 'Has he deceived me?'" vividly captures his shock, doubt, and astonishment.

"Skilled in employing wisdom and courage" means they could use wisdom and courage to govern the world. "Skilled in following the times" means they could act in accordance with the natural order.

Who, then, is a sage? And what defines a sage?

Zixia asked Confucius, "What sort of man is Yan Hui?"
The Master said, "Hui’s benevolence surpasses my own."
Zixia asked, "What sort of man is Zigong?"
The Master said, "Ci’s eloquence surpasses my own."
Zixia asked, "What sort of man is Zilu?"
The Master said, "You’s courage surpasses my own."
Zixia asked, "What sort of man is Zizhang?"
The Master said, "Shi’s solemnity surpasses my own."

Zixia rose from his mat and asked, "Then why do these four serve you?"
The Master said, "Be seated. I shall tell you.
Hui can be benevolent but cannot adapt; Ci can be eloquent but cannot be restrained; You can be courageous but cannot be timid; Shi can be solemn but cannot blend in. Were I to exchange my qualities for any one of theirs, I would not consent. This is why they serve me without reservation.

To be benevolent yet unable to adapt is to lack the capacity for change—it means one knows benevolence but not transformation, the subtlety of weighing circumstances. Here, *benevolence* carries the same meaning as *sincerity*. The terms *solemnity* and *blending* in the *Zhuangzi* and *Liezi* cannot be compared to those in my text. *Solemnity* means dignity; *blending* means harmonizing one’s light and merging with the dust.

If I possessed all four of their qualities, I could adapt as needed, whereas they cannot. In this light, *adapt* resembles *timeliness*. The sage embodies wholeness: at times eloquent, at times solemn, at times benevolent, at times courageous. The four disciples each possess only a partial virtue.

When I say *I would not consent*, it means they have studied the Way of adaptability and the mean but have not yet mastered it—thus, I do not approve.

Liezi first studied under Master Huqiu Zilin and befriended Bohun Maoren, then resided in Nanguo. Those who followed him were so numerous that they could not be counted daily. Yet Liezi himself was somewhat diminished, for each morning he debated with them, and none went unheard. *Could not be counted daily* means that even if one tried to tally them each day, they could not be exhausted—so great was the number of his followers. *Somewhat diminished* refers to the strain of his constant engagement.

All his daily debates and discourses spread throughout the world, and none failed to hear of them. Though he lived next door to Nanguozi for twenty years, they never paid formal visits to each other. When they met on the road, their eyes seemed not to see one another. Liezi’s disciples assumed there was enmity between them.

A man from Chu came and asked Liezi, "What is the nature of your rivalry with Nanguozi?"
Liezi replied, "Nanguozi is like an infant: his mind is empty, his ears hear nothing, his eyes see nothing, his mouth speaks nothing, his heart knows nothing, and his form shows no agitation. What could I possibly do with him? We do not exchange visits or formal greetings, yet we meet without pretense."

"Enmity" means contention. "Without doubt" means decisively. People all assumed the two had decisive contention in their hearts, which was why they did not meet. "His countenance full" describes a face so replete it seems to overflow the nape of the neck. "No hearing, no seeing, no speaking, no knowing" means that though he hears, he does not hear; though he sees, he does not see; though he speaks, he does not speak; though he knows, he does not know. "His form shows no agitation" means his virtue is complete and nothing external startles him. "What could I possibly do with him?" means: if I were to go see him, what would I say? Nevertheless, let us go together and see. Liezi selected forty disciples to accompany him. When they saw Nanguozi, he indeed appeared like a deceptive wraith—impossible to engage. Looking back at Liezi, his form and spirit were not in accord, and he could not join their company. Suddenly, Nanguozi pointed to one of Liezi’s disciples in the rear rank and spoke with him, cheerful and self-assured as one who stands alone in supremacy. Liezi’s disciples were astonished. Returning home, all wore expressions of doubt.

"Selected disciples" means choosing those to accompany him. "Deceptive wraith" describes a form so inert it resembles a wraith yet is not one. "Deceptive" means doubtful. Compared to Nanguozi’s wraith-like deceptiveness, Liezi’s form and spirit were not in accord, so he could not join their company—Nanguozi was beyond comparison. "Form and spirit not in accord" means form and spirit were separate and not yet unified. "Pointed to one in the rear rank and spoke with him" means he chose the lowest among them to address, implying Liezi was not worth speaking to. "Cheerful" means harmonious; "self-assured" means singular; "in supremacy" means uniquely exalted, as if no one else were present. Their doubt upon returning home was that Nanguozi had slighted Liezi.

Liezi said: The one who has attained the meaning speaks no words, and the one who advances in knowledge also speaks no words. To use no words as words is still words; to use no knowledge as knowledge is still knowledge. No words and not speaking, no knowledge and not knowing—these are still words and knowledge. They are also without anything not spoken, without anything not known; they are also without anything spoken, without anything known. It is only thus. Why this groundless astonishment?

One who has attained the meaning has penetrated the Dao and gained realization. One who advances in knowledge has penetrated the Dao and gained insight. Below this, the text unfolds in three turns.

"No words" means forgetting words. To use "no words" as words, to use "no knowledge" as knowledge—this "also words, also knowledge"—implies that though words are forgotten, the absence of words remains; this is the first turn. "No words and not speaking, no knowledge and not knowing—also words, also knowledge"—here, "no" and "not" are distinguished. "Not" means knowledge and words still linger; "no" means the absence of words and knowledge still lingers. "Also" conveys the sense of incompleteness; this is the second turn. Only when there is nothing not spoken, nothing not known, yet also nothing spoken, nothing known, does one reach the subtlety of penetrating the Dao; this is the third turn. This aligns with the saying "the heart does not transgress the measure," though here it is expressed with fervor. Modern Chan schools employ this very mechanism, and this passage’s phrasing closely resembles the dialectical exchanges in the *Transmission of the Lamp*.

"Why this groundless astonishment?" means: this is the affair of the perfected person—why do you falsely take it as shocking? The implication is that you are startled only because you do not yet understand the affairs of the perfected. How could I compare to such a one?

Liezi was a student. After three years, his heart dared not entertain judgments of right and wrong, his mouth dared not speak of profit and harm, and only then did Master Shang grant him a single glance. After five years, his heart once more entertained judgments of right and wrong, his mouth once more spoke of profit and harm, and only then did Master Shang relax his expression and smile. After seven years, whatever his heart conceived bore no trace of right or wrong; whatever his mouth uttered bore no trace of profit or harm. Only then did the Master draw him near to share his mat. After nine years, whether his heart wandered or his mouth spoke, he no longer knew whether these were his own judgments of right and wrong or profit and harm, or another’s—inner and outer had merged. Thereafter, his eyes were like ears, his ears like nose, his nose like mouth—all were indistinguishable. His heart congealed, his form dissolved, bones and flesh fused entirely. He no longer sensed the body’s support, the feet’s tread, the heart’s thoughts, or the words’ storehouse. Only thus. Then the principle was nowhere concealed.

This chapter recounts the beginning of Liezi’s studies, as seen in the previous section. "Heart congealed" means settled; "form dissolved" means forgetting one’s form. Bones and flesh all melt away—he no longer knows his own body. Standing where the body leans, walking where the feet tread, conceiving what the heart conceives, uttering what the mouth utters—all are beyond his awareness. "Storehouse" means repository: the principles uttered are all contained within. Only thus—merely this state of unawareness, and nothing more. When principle is nowhere concealed, the supreme principle is immediately evident.

Initially, Liezi delighted in wandering. Master Huqiu asked: "Youkuo delights in wandering—what is it about wandering that delights you?" Liezi replied: "The joy of wandering lies in savoring the ceaseless transformation. Others wander to observe what they see; I wander to observe what changes. Wandering, ah, wandering—none have been able to discern the nature of my wandering."

Master Huqiu said: "Is Yourkuo’s wandering truly the same as others’, or do you claim it is fundamentally different? Whatever one sees, one always sees its changes. You savor the ceaselessness of things yet do not realize you yourself are ceaseless. You pursue external wandering yet do not know to cultivate inner observation. Those who wander externally seek completion in things; those who observe inwardly find sufficiency in themselves. Finding sufficiency in oneself is the perfection of wandering; seeking completion in things is the failure of wandering."

Thereupon, Liezi never again ventured out, believing himself ignorant of wandering. Master Huqiu said: "This is the perfection of wandering! The perfect wanderer knows not where he goes; the perfect observer knows not where he gazes. All things wander, all things observe—this is what I call wandering, this is what I call observation. Thus it is said: ‘Has he reached the perfection of wandering?’ The perfection of wandering, ah!"

Wandering means roaming and observing between heaven and earth. "Ceaseless" means daily renewal. Others take what they see as the joy of wandering and observation; I take the ceaseless transformation of creation as the joy of wandering and observation—thus none have been able to discern or know it. Hence it is said: "None have been able to discern his wandering."

Master Huqiu criticized him, saying: "Your wandering is the same as others’, yet you claim it is fundamentally different. Your seeing is always like this—how can you say you see its changes?" Thus he said: "Whatever one sees, one always sees its changes"—meaning Liezi falsely claimed to see its changes. Things’ ceaselessness presents itself anew day and night before one’s eyes, yet one sees only the new and not the ceaseless. The self as self is likewise. To savor the daily renewal of things while unaware that the self, too, follows transformation and departs, changing day by day, is to observe both constancy and change as external wandering. Those who seek completion in things merely exhaust external observation. Finding sufficiency in oneself and observing nothing without—this is the perfection of wandering. One who never attains it knows his learning has not yet reached perfection.

"Not knowing where he goes" means he has no destination. "Not knowing where he gazes" means he has no sight. Without destination or sight, there is no thing, no self. There is nothing that is not wandering, nothing that is not observation. This is what I mean by wandering and observation. Thus it is said: "All things wander, all things observe." Hence: "This is what I call wandering, this is what I call observation." Repeating "has he reached perfection?" is an emphatic expression of admiration.

Long Shu said to Wen Zhi: "Your art is subtle indeed."

"I suffer from an affliction—can you cure it?"

Wen Zhi replied, "I await your command. But first, tell me the symptoms of your illness."

Long Shu said: "Praise from my village brings me no honor; slander from the state brings me no disgrace. Gain does not delight me, nor does loss distress me. I regard life as death, wealth as poverty, others as swine, and myself as one among them. Dwelling in my home, I feel as though in a traveler’s inn; gazing upon my village, I see it as a land of barbarians. All these are my many afflictions—titles and rewards cannot sway me, punishments cannot intimidate me, prosperity or ruin, profit or harm cannot alter me, sorrow or joy cannot move me. Thus I am unfit to serve a lord, befriend companions, govern a wife and children, or command servants. What manner of illness is this? How could any remedy cure it?"

Wen Zhi then instructed Long Shu to stand with his back to the light. Wen Zhi himself stepped behind him, facing the light to observe. After a moment, he exclaimed: "Ah! Now I see your heart. The square inch of your mind is empty—you are nearly a sage. Your heart has six apertures, all flowing freely; only one remains blocked. Is it not those who take sagely wisdom as an affliction who suffer from this? My shallow art cannot cure it.

To view honor and disgrace, gain and loss, life and death, wealth and poverty as one—this is to forget the affairs of the world. To see others as swine is to erase distinctions of noble and base. To see myself as one among them is to dissolve the boundary between self and other. To treat one’s home as a travelers’ inn is to hold even kin at a distance. To regard one’s village as a land of barbarians is to see the far as near. All this arises from a heart free of entanglements. To be unfit to serve a lord, befriend others, govern a wife and children, or command servants—this is to lack the mind for worldly engagement. These are the traits of the perfected, yet you call them illness, much like the scorn of Chan masters.

Standing with one’s back to the light reveals the heart—just as Bian Que could see the five organs through a wall. Though this passage speaks in metaphors, its final turn concludes thus: sagely wisdom resides within us, yet if one cannot forget it, it too becomes an affliction. This is why the argument shifts so. As the Buddhists say: clinging to medicine to cure illness makes the medicine itself the illness. This is much the same.

That which arises without cause and is ever-living is the Dao. That which arises from life and thus lives on, though its end comes, is constancy. That which arises from life yet perishes is misfortune. That which arises without cause and is ever-dying is also the Dao. That which arises from death and thus dies, though it has not yet ended, is also constancy. That which arises from death yet lives on is fortune.

Thus, to live without purpose is called the Dao; to follow the Dao and attain one’s end is called constancy. To die with purpose is also called the Dao; to follow the Dao and attain death is also called constancy.

To live without cause means to arise from nowhere, unaware of how life comes to be. To dissolve all knowledge—this is the Dao. To live from life is to know how life arises and persists, though the body ends, its end does not truly perish—this is the view of ordinary people. To know that life entails death is to grasp the principle of no-life through life—this is misfortune, for such knowledge and awareness become a burden.

To know life through the principle of no-life is to live as though without life—this too is the Dao. The ‘too’ suggests nearness to the Dao. To know constant death through the principle of no-life, though the body has not yet ended, is to live as though already dead—this is also the view of ordinary people. Yet to know life through the principle of no-life is fortune.

To live without purpose is to hold no mind for life—this is called the Dao. To follow this Dao and know the principle of one’s end—this is called constancy. To die with purpose—here, ‘with’ is an error; it should be ‘without.’ To die without purpose, holding no mind for death, following nature—this too is called the Dao. To see the Dao and grasp the principle of death—this is called constancy.

The meaning here is that those who know the Dao are merely ordinary people, not those of lofty wisdom. It is those who do not know who are called the Dao. The arguments of Zhuangzi and Liezi largely follow this shifting pattern. The Buddhist debates on constancy and impermanence also trace their source here.

When Ji Liang died, Yang Zhu sang before his gate; when Sui Wu died, Yang Zhu wept over his corpse. The birth of a servant is met with song, his death with weeping. The eyes, about to go blind, first see the autumn down; the ears, about to go deaf, first hear the gnat in flight; the tongue, about to lose taste, first distinguishes the waters of the Zi and Sheng; the nose, about to be blocked, first detects the scent of burning rot; the body, about to stiffen, first hastens in restless motion; the mind, about to be deluded, first discerns right and wrong. Thus, when things do not reach their extreme, they do not return.

A servant is an ordinary person. Ji Liang and Sui Wu were also ordinary. Yang Zhu sang for one and wept for the other—thus Yang Zhu too was ordinary. The point is that to live and die without purpose is the same in principle, yet song and weeping differ—this is not knowing the Dao. When things do not reach their extreme, they do not return; when they do, they must reverse.

The lines from ‘the eyes about to go blind’ onward are like a lamp about to go out, which first flares brightly—this is the principle that when things reach their extreme, they must reverse.

In Zheng’s gardens and marshes, many worthies dwell; in Dongli, many talents reside. During the marshes’ labor service, a man named Bo Fengzi passed through Dongli and encountered Deng Xi. Deng Xi turned to his disciples and laughed: "Dance for him—what do you make of this one approaching?"

His disciples replied, "We wish to know."

Deng Xi said to Bo Fengzi: "Do you understand the meaning of ‘nurturing nurturance’? To receive nurture from others yet be unable to nurture oneself—this is the way of dogs and swine."

To nurture things and make them serve one’s use—this is the power of man. To make your disciples eat their fill, clothe themselves, and rest—this is the achievement of governance. Yet if the old and young gather in droves to become fodder for pens and kitchens, how is this different from dogs and swine?

Bo Fengzi did not respond.

One of Bo Fengzi’s followers stepped out of turn and said: "Has the Master never heard of Qi and Lu, where many are skilled in craft? Some excel in working earth and wood, some in metal and leather, some in music, some in writing and calculation, some in military affairs, some in ancestral rites—all talents are present. Yet without those who assign positions, without those who command, those who hold office lack knowledge, and those who command lack ability. Thus, the knowing and the capable are made to serve the unknowing and the incapable. The governors are but those whom we employ—why should you take pride in this?"

Deng Xi had no reply. He looked at his disciples and withdrew.

Deng Xi was a master of debate; Bo Fengzi, a man of virtue. Deng Xi, seeing Bo Fengzi approach, wished to mock and provoke him. *Ruo* means "you"; *his disciples* refers to Deng Xi’s followers; *that one approaching* is Bo Fengzi. The meaning of "nurturing nurturance" is like what Mencius called "being employed by others" and "employing others." Dogs and swine receive nurture from men, but those who nurture dogs and swine for their own use are men. The implication is that Bo Fengzi’s followers, fed by the state’s emoluments and sustained by the governors, are but tools of governance.

*Many are skilled* means many possess techniques. *Assigning positions* refers to appointing to office; *commanding* means directing others. Though their skills are equal, each excels in their own domain, yet none can appoint or direct the others. Those who appoint and command them possess neither knowledge nor ability. Thus, the knowing and capable are made to serve the unknowing and incapable. The governors are men of talent, while Bo Fengzi conceals himself in the Way. He says: "I conceal myself in the Way, appearing as one without knowledge or ability, yet those governors of Zheng who are employed in their time are but servants to me. How then can they nurture me?" *Why take pride* means: "Why do you boast and dance before me?"

Gongyi Bo was renowned among the feudal lords for his strength. Tangxi Gong spoke of him to King Xuan of Zhou, who prepared gifts and invited him. When Gongyi Bo arrived and the king observed his appearance, he seemed a feeble man. The king, perplexed, asked: "What is your strength like?"

Gongyi Bo replied: "My strength can break the leg of a spring katydid and lift the wing of an autumn cicada."

The king’s expression changed: "My strength can rend the hide of a rhinoceros or a young buffalo and drag the tails of nine oxen, yet I still feel it insufficient. You break the leg of a katydid and lift the wing of a cicada, yet your strength is renowned throughout the world—why?"

Gongyi Bo sighed deeply, withdrew from his mat, and said: "Excellent is the king’s question. I dare speak the truth. My teacher, Shangqiu Zi, was unmatched in strength under heaven, yet even his closest kin did not know it, for he never employed his strength. I served him unto death. He instructed me: 'One who wishes to see what others cannot see must train to observe what others do not; one who wishes to obtain what others cannot must cultivate what others will not. Thus, those who learn to see begin with perceiving a cartload of firewood, and those who learn to hear begin with hearing the striking of a bell. What is easy within presents no difficulty without. With no difficulty without, one’s name does not leave a single household.'

Now my name is known among the feudal lords—this is my violation of my teacher’s teaching, my display of my own ability. Yet my renown does not come from bearing my strength but from knowing how to use it. Is this not still better than bearing one’s strength in vain? *Kan* means 'to bear'—to say one can lift the wing of an autumn cicada is playful speech to provoke the king’s question."

Shangqiu Zi’s strength was unmatched under heaven, yet even his closest kin did not know of his courage, for he could conceal himself. "To see what is unseen, observe what is unobserved; to obtain what is unobtained, cultivate what is uncultivated"—this is the meaning of "knowing the unknown" and "acting through non-action." Those who learn to see begin with a cartload of firewood, and those who learn to hear begin with the striking of a bell—these are the coarse aspects of perception, which must progress to seeing the unseen and hearing the unheard before reaching subtlety. "What is easy within" refers to what is neither heard nor seen. *Easy* means that what is simple in itself is sought through difficulty. If one can see what is unseen and hear what is unheard, and what one seeks within is already easy, then externally, perception presents no difficulty. With no difficulty externally, even perception is unnecessary—how then would others know of it? Thus, his name did not leave a single household, meaning even his neighbors did not know. Now, I cannot help but employ my strength, so my fame for strength has spread throughout the world. Those who delight in victory and boast of their strength, unable to conceal themselves, bring their names to prominence—this is to violate the teacher’s teaching and lose the Way. Yet my use of strength, though I cannot conceal it, is still better than boasting of it. This is meant to satirize the king’s fondness for valor.

Yet the intent of this text is to embody "having as if not having, being full as if empty," to endure offense without retaliation. Thus, this parable is set forth.

*Long sigh* means a deep sigh.

Zhongshan Gongzi Mou was a worthy prince of Wei. He delighted in associating with worthy men, neglected state affairs, and took pleasure in the company of Gongsun Long of Zhao. Le Zhengzi Yu and his followers laughed at him.

Gongzi Mou said: "Why do you laugh at my delight in Gongsun Long?"

Zi Yu said: "Gongsun Long’s character is such that he follows no teacher, studies with no companions, is glib but misses the mark, rambles without substance, delights in the strange, and speaks recklessly."

He sought to bewilder men’s minds and overpower their speech, practicing such arts alongside Han Tan and his ilk.

*Without teacher or friend* means he studied alone.
*Glib* refers to his eloquence.
*Missing the mark* means failing to hit the principle.
*Rambling* means overflowing without restraint.
*Without school* means he adhered to no single school of thought.
Han Tan was a follower of Gongsun Long. With his theories and his disciples, they debated among themselves, aiming to confound and delude their contemporaries—far from the true principle.

Gongzi Mou’s expression changed as he said: "Why do you depict Gongsun Long’s faults so harshly? Pray, tell me the truth of the matter."

Zi Yu replied: "I laugh at how Long deceived Kong Chuan, claiming that a master archer could make the rear arrowhead strike the front nock, so that each shot followed the last without break, each shaft linked to the next, the foremost arrow reaching the target without a single miss, while the nock of the following arrow still clung to the bowstring—appearing as one continuous shaft.

*The nock* is the base of the arrow where the string rests.
*Making the rear arrowhead strike the front nock* means each shot follows the previous without interruption.
*Each shaft linked to the next* means no arrow stands alone.
*All the foremost arrows hit the target* means not one fell short.
*The following arrow’s nock still clung to the bowstring* describes how the nock, struck by the rear arrowhead, seemed to remain on the string.
*Reaching the target* means hitting the mark.
*The mark* refers to the standard.
*Reaching the mark* means conforming to the principle.
*Front and rear* denote the sequence of the shots.
*Still clung to the bowstring* means the nock, receiving the arrowhead, appeared as if still strung.

Kong Chuan was astonished.

Long said: 'This is not yet the height of skill. Hong Chao, a disciple of Feng Meng, once grew enraged at his wife and sought to frighten her. He drew the famed *Wuhao* bow and the prized *Qiwei* arrows, aiming at her eye. The arrow flew straight toward her pupil, yet her eyelid did not so much as flutter; the arrow fell to the ground without stirring a speck of dust. Is this the speech of a wise man?'

Gongzi Mou said: 'The words of the wise are not meant for fools to grasp. The rear arrowhead striking the front nock aligns what follows with what precedes. The arrow flying toward the pupil without the eyelid blinking—this is the full force of the shot. Why do you doubt it?'

*The Wuhao* was a renowned bow of the Yellow Emperor.
*The Qiwei* must also have been a famed arrow.
*The eyelid did not flutter* means she did not blink.
*The arrow fell without stirring dust* describes how lightly it landed.
*Aligning what follows with what precedes* means the force of the arrows, front and rear, was perfectly balanced.
*The full force of the shot* means the arrow reached the very edge of the pupil before falling—its momentum exhausted precisely at that point, a testament to the archer’s precision.

Le Zhengzi Yu said: 'You are one of Long’s followers—how could you not gloss over his flaws? Let me tell you of his most egregious claims.'"

Long deceived the King of Wei, saying:
"Intention exists without mind.
Pointing occurs without reaching.
Things exist without being exhausted.
Shadows exist yet shift.
A single hair can draw a thousand jun.
A white horse is not a horse.
An orphaned calf has never had a mother."

Such inversions of kind and reversals of principle are beyond counting.

Gongzi Mou said: "You take what is most profound as most egregious—yet the fault lies with you. Without intention, minds are the same; without pointing, all is reached. One who exhausts things always possesses them. That shadows do not move is explained by change. A hair drawing a thousand jun is due to equalized force. That a white horse is not a horse arises from the separation of form and name. That an orphaned calf has never had a mother means it is no orphaned calf at all."

Le Zhengzi Yu said: "You take all of Gongsun Long’s pronouncements as orderly and sound. If such words issued from another orifice, you would still accept them." Gongzi Mou fell silent for a long while, then withdrew, saying: "Let me return another day to discuss this further."

*You, a follower of Long*—meaning Gongzi Mou was a disciple of Long—*how could you not strain to gloss over his lapses?* *Lapses* refers to omissions. *Yet more egregious*—he pressed further, selecting the most extreme examples to make Mou recognize their absurdity.

*Intention arises from mind*—today he speaks of "intention without mind," though mind and intention are but different names. *Without intention, minds are the same*—for if one speaks of intention, it cannot be called mind; if one speaks of mind, it cannot be called intention. To say "without intention" is to say minds are the same, just as to say "with intention" is to say minds are the same—yet having intention does not make it mind.

*Pointing at one thing with the eye leaves many unpointed at*—hence "pointing occurs without reaching." But if nothing is pointed at, all is reached—hence "without pointing, all is reached."

*What exists is called a thing*—if one takes "having" as a thing, how can all things in the world be exhausted? If one does not take things as things, then all things in the world can be exhausted and possessed. Hence: "One who exhausts things always possesses them."

*That shadows do not move*—this is what Master Hui meant when he said the shadow of a flying bird never moves. *Explained by change*—this refers to movement. A thing has one shadow; as soon as it moves, the later shadow is not the earlier one. Seeking the earlier shadow from the later, one finds it in the moment before movement—hence "that shadows do not move is explained by change." *Change* means transformation, referring to the moment of alteration.

*A hair is most weak; a thousand jun, most heavy.* While a single hair cannot draw a thousand jun, if the force of the hair accumulates until it equals a thousand jun, then it can draw it—hence "equalized force." Though this is forced reasoning, it can be made coherent.

*White* is a color, named by its color. *Horse* is a form, named by its form. To call a color "white" is acceptable; to call a form "horse" is acceptable. But to call a white horse a horse is to confuse two things: white is color, horse is form. How can they be one? Hence: "A white horse is not a horse—form and name are separate." This aligns with the *Kong Congzi* and Gongsun Long’s own writings.

*Though an orphaned calf is born of a mother, while the mother lives it is not called orphaned; once called orphaned, it has never had a mother.* To call it "having a mother" means it is no orphaned calf. The *Zhuangzi* also contains similar passages.

*Orderly*—meaning methodical. Le Zhengzi Yu, angered by such forced sophistry and unable to refute it further, said: "Do you take all of Gongsun Long’s words as conforming to method?" *Another orifice*—a base or vulgar source—meaning if such words came from elsewhere, you would still accept them.

*"Let me return another day to discuss this further"*—akin to the modern saying, "Let us continue this another time." He withdrew in silent displeasure, unwilling to engage further.

When Yao ruled the world for fifty years, he did not know whether the world was well governed or not, nor whether the multitudes wished to support him or not. He asked his attendants—they did not know. He asked the outer court—they did not know. He asked those in the wilds—they did not know.

Yao then disguised himself and wandered through the bustling thoroughfares, where he heard children singing a folk rhyme:

*"He who establishes our teeming multitudes—*
*None but you set the ultimate norm.*
*Unknowing, unperceiving,*
*We follow the celestial pattern."*

Delighted, Yao asked, "Who taught you these words?"
The children replied, "We heard them from a grandee."
He questioned the grandee, who answered, "It is an ancient poem."

Yao returned to his palace, summoned Shun, and thereupon abdicated the realm to him. Shun accepted without demurral.

This passage depicts how the sage transforms the world without leaving any trace of transformation—though the world is transformed, none are aware of it. *"He who establishes us"* means he who brings us to life and sets us upright between heaven and earth. *"The ultimate norm"* is the Dao. *"The celestial"* refers to the principles of heaven. The poem was originally composed to praise Yao’s virtue, yet the grandee called it an *"ancient poem"*—this too illustrates how Yao and the world had become as one, oblivious to the source of such transformation. Thus, when Yao raised Shun and abdicated to him, and Shun accepted without refusal, it shows that Yao’s abdication and Shun’s acceptance both arose from non-deliberation.

Guan Yinxi said: *"Abiding nowhere within oneself,*
*Yet manifesting through things.*
*Its movement is like water,*
*Its stillness like a mirror,*
*Its response like an echo—*
*Thus is its Way like things themselves.*
*Things depart from the Dao,*
*But the Dao does not depart from things.*

*One who is good at the Way*
*Uses neither ears,*
*Nor eyes,*
*Nor strength,*
*Nor mind.*
*To seek the Dao through seeing, hearing, form, or intellect*
*Is to miss the mark.*

*Gazing ahead, it is suddenly behind;*
*Used, it fills the six directions;*
*Discarded, none know where it resides.*
*Neither the mindful can attain it from afar,*
*Nor the mindless draw near.*
*Only those who silently grasp it and let nature perfect it attain it—*
*Knowing yet forgetting emotion,*
*Capable yet not acting,*
*This is true knowledge, true capability.*

*If one acts from ignorance, how can there be true emotion?*
*If one acts from incapacity, what can be accomplished?*
*Like a clod of earth, like piled dust—*
*Though non-action, it is not the principle.*

*Abiding nowhere within oneself means no attachment.*
*Appearing with things, manifesting in use,*
*Formed through things, the Dao naturally becomes evident.*
*Its movement like water—no contrivance within.*
*Its stillness like a mirror—beauty and ugliness lie in things, not in oneself.*
*Responding to what comes, following sound,*
*It is naturally so.*

*Its Way is like things because it accords with them.*
*There is nothing that is not the Dao.*
*Those who do not know the Dao depart from it themselves—*
*How has the Dao ever departed from things?*

*Not using the ears—it is not attained through hearing.*
*Not using the eyes—it is not attained through seeing.*
*Not using strength—it is not attained through effort.*
*Not using the mind—it is not attained through thought.*
*To seek the Dao through sight, hearing, form, or intellect*
*Is to fail to grasp it rightly.*
*Form means the body, strength.*
*Intellect means mental deliberation.*

*Gazing ahead, it is suddenly behind—*
*It has no fixed place.*
*Used, it appears, filling the six directions;*
*Unused, it vanishes, and none know where it resides.*
*Those who seek with intent are far from the Dao—*
*How could the Dao be distant for the intentional?*
*Those who seek without intent are near the Dao—*
*How could the Dao ever be near for the unintentional?*

The Śramaṇa say: *"The Dao cannot be sought with intent,*
*Nor can it be attained without intent."*
This expresses the same idea.

*Silently grasping it, naturally awakening,*
*Nature perfecting it—this is innate knowledge.*
*Knowing through unknowing—thus, knowing yet forgetting emotion.*
*Capable through incapability—thus, capable yet not acting.*
*Unknowing is true knowledge;*
*Incapability is true capability.*

*"Acting"* here means *"turning toward"*—modern speech retains similar phrasing. Chan teachings say: *"No single thing is ever entirely so."* *"Emotion"* means reality. If one were utterly devoid of knowledge, how could one grasp reality? If one were utterly incapable, what could one accomplish? For to know through unknowing is not true ignorance—it is unknowing yet all-knowing. To be capable through incapability is not true incapacity—it is incapability yet all-capable. To act through non-action is not true inaction—it is inaction yet all-accomplishing. If one were like piled dust or a clod of earth, though non-acting, it would not accord with principle—meaning the principle of non-action is not like this.

From this perspective, how could the teachings of Zhuangzi and Liezi ever take withered wood or dead ashes as their ideal? Chan masters say: *"No night travel allowed—you must arrive by daylight. Revive only after death—no deceiving the sovereign."* This conveys the same meaning.

This passage is a major thread running through the books of Zhuangzi and Liezi.

*The Record of True Purity in the Five Patriarchs' Expository Treatises* states: *"A single thought—like the cold incense burner of an ancient temple in late autumn—becomes an obstruction to radiant clarity for ten thousand years."* This meaning is precisely the same.

This passage appears in *The Recorded Sayings of Dahui: General Teachings*. In the *"Below in the World"* chapter of *Zhuangzi*, the discussion of Tian Pian and Shen Dao—how a clod never loses the Dao—is called the learning of the dead. This, too, refers to the same "clod," meaning the clod of piled earth.

*The Oral Exegesis of the Perfect Virtue of the Primal Vacuity by Master Yanzhai*, Scroll Four (End)

*The Oral Exegesis of the Perfect Virtue of the Primal Vacuity by Master Yanzhai*, Scroll Five
By Lin Xiang of Yanzhai

*The Oral Exegesis of the Perfect Virtue of the Primal Vacuity by Master Yanzhai*, Scroll Five

**Tang’s Fifth Inquiry**

King Tang of Yin asked Xia Ge: *"Did things exist in the primordial beginning?"*
Xia Ge replied: *"In the primordial beginning, there were no things—how could there be things now? Would later generations be right to say there are no things now?"*

King Tang asked: *"Then do things have no before or after?"*
Xia Ge replied: *"The end and beginning of things have no ultimate limit. What was once a beginning may become an end, and what was once an end may become a beginning—how could one know their boundary? Yet beyond things and before events, this I do not know."*

King Tang asked: *"Then do the eight directions—above and below—have limits?"*
Ge replied: *"I do not know."*
Tang pressed further.
Ge said: *"If there is nothing, then there is no limit; if there is something, then there is an end—how could I know this? Yet beyond the limitless there is no further limitlessness, and within the endless there is no further endlessness. The limitless has no further limitlessness, and the endless has no further endlessness—by this I know there is no limit and no end, yet I do not know if there is a limit or an end. Though things arise from nothing into being, once they exist, they must have a beginning—how can one say that in the primordial beginning there were no things? This phrasing is also well turned."*

*"Ji"* (极) means *"to reach an end."* The beginning and end of things have no fixed stopping point—just as spring precedes summer, yet when spring ends, summer begins. Is what came first truly the beginning? Is what follows truly the end? *"Ji"* (纪) means *"limit."* *"How could one know their limit?"* speaks of the limitless.

*"Beyond things and before events, this I do not know"* refers to the four directions and above and below, which cannot be fathomed—the *"six directions beyond"* of which *Zhuangzi* speaks, set aside and not discussed.

*"Pressed further"* means he questioned insistently, and only when forced did Ge reply: *"To call it nothing is to say it is limitless; once the name 'being' exists, there must be an end—but it cannot be known."* *"Beyond the limitless there is no further limitlessness"*—the following lines share the same vein as *Zhuangzi*’s *"There is a beginning,"* *"There is a time before that beginning,"* and *"There is a time before that time before the beginning."*

In *Zhuangzi*’s *"Free and Easy Wandering"* chapter, Tang questions Ji; here, he questions Xia Ge. *"Ji"* and *"Ge"* are near-homophones, likely a transmission error—but these are mostly allegorical, and differences in names need not be scrutinized.

Tang asked again: *"What exists beyond the four seas?"*
Ge replied: *"The same as the Central States."*
Tang said: *"How do you verify this?"*
Ge said: *"When I traveled east to Ying, the people were the same. Asking about what lay east of Ying, it was still like Ying. When I traveled west to Bin, the people were the same. Asking about what lay west of Bin, it was still like Bin. By this I know that the four seas, the four wastes, and the four poles are no different."*

Beyond the four seas, whether there are lands or no lands—this cannot be known. Just as one in Ying sees only Ying’s people, and one in Bin sees only Bin’s people, how could they know that east of Ying lies another Ying, or west of Bin lies another Bin? If even what is seen in the Central States is thus, how much more so beyond the four seas, the four wastes, and the four poles?

*"Central States"* refers to China. *"Verify"* means to use the near to illustrate the far. Beyond the seas lies the *"Great Waste,"* and beyond the Great Waste lies the *"Limitless"*—hence *"the four seas, the four wastes, the four poles."* Such discourse aims at vastness and grandeur. The books of Zhuangzi and Liezi are all like this—thus great and small contain one another without end.

What contains the myriad things is like what contains heaven and earth. Containing the myriad things is inexhaustible; containing heaven and earth is thus limitless. How could I know that beyond heaven and earth there are not greater heavens and earths? This, too, is what I do not know.

Great and small contain one another—like tiles on rafters, rafters on beams, beams on pillars, pillars on the ground. Small and large support things in succession, and all things are thus—beyond exhaustive inquiry.

Since all things are like this, then heaven and earth exist within the Great Vacuity. The Great Vacuity contains heaven and earth, and beyond the Great Vacuity there must be what contains the Great Vacuity. Since what contains the myriad things is inexhaustible, how could what contains heaven and earth have a limit? How could one know that beyond heaven and earth there is not something greater than heaven and earth?

*"Contain"* means *"to hold."* Such arguments reject narrow views. Those bound by self-interest do not grasp the vastness of the world, hence such empty and expansive discourse—though seemingly absurd, it has its own flavor. The following passages in this chapter are all like this; if one seeks principle in each, it cannot be done. To read the books of Zhuangzi and Liezi, one must see with a different eye.

Yet heaven and earth are also things. Things have insufficiencies, so in ancient times Nüwa smelted the five-colored stones to mend their gaps and severed the legs of the great turtle to prop up the four poles. Later, Gonggong contended with Zhuanxu for the throne, and in rage struck Mount Buzhou, breaking the pillar of heaven and severing the cords of earth. Thus heaven tilted to the northwest, and the sun, moon, and stars moved there; earth sank in the southeast, and the hundred rivers and floods flowed there.

Nüwa’s mending of heaven and Gonggong’s breaking of the pillar of heaven and severing of earth’s cords—these are all shocking claims, not to be taken as literal. Heaven’s tilt to the northwest is the subtlest work of creation. Without this leaning canopy, how could the movements of the stars, the waxing and waning of the sun and moon, or the alternation of day and night be manifest? This requires knowledge of astronomy. If one understands the theory of earth’s fourfold motion and that heaven is like an egg, how could there be pillars or cords?

Tang asked again: *"Do things have greatness and smallness? Do they have length and shortness?"*

"Do things share commonalities and differences?"

Ge said: "To the east of the Bohai Sea, beyond countless millions of *li*, lies a great abyss—truly a bottomless valley. Its depths know no end, and it is called the ‘Returning Gulch.’ The waters of the Eight Directions and Nine Wildernesses, the currents of the Celestial River—all pour into it, yet neither increase nor diminish. Within it stand five mountains: the first is Daiyu, the second Yuanqiao, the third Fanghu, the fourth Yingzhou, and the fifth Penglai. Each mountain rises and winds for thirty thousand *li*, its flat summit spanning nine thousand *li*. The distance between their midpoints is seventy thousand *li*, yet they are neighbors. Their terraces and pavilions are all of gold and jade; their birds and beasts, pure white. Trees of pearl and jade grow in clusters, their blossoms and fruits rich in flavor—whoever eats them never ages nor dies. The inhabitants are all of the seed of immortals and sages; day and night, those who fly between them are beyond counting.

Yet the roots of the five mountains have no anchor, drifting ceaselessly with the tides, rising and falling without pause. The immortals and sages, distressed, appealed to the Emperor. Fearing the mountains would drift to the westernmost reaches and scatter the dwellings of the sacred throng, he commanded Yuqiang to dispatch fifteen giant turtles to bear them on their heads. They took turns in three shifts, alternating every sixty thousand years. Only then did the five mountains stand firm and still.

But in the land of Longbo there lived giants whose strides spanned the five mountains in but a few steps. One cast his line and hooked six turtles at once, then carried them back to his country and scorched their bones to divine the future. Thus, the mountains Daiyu and Yuanqiao drifted to the northern pole, adrift in the great sea, and the immortals and sages who were displaced numbered in the countless millions. The Emperor, enraged, diminished the land of Longbo to narrow its bounds and shrank its people to shorten their stature. By the time of Fuxi and Shennong, its people still stood thousands of *zhang* tall.

The ‘Returning Gulch’ is none other than the ‘Tail Socket.’ The ‘Eight Directions’ are the eight cardinal points; the ‘Nine Wildernesses,’ the nine provinces. *Chungao* means pure white; *zhugan*, pearls and jade. *Zhi* means to halt. *Du zhi* means to suffer. Yuqiang is the name of a god. *He fu yi liu* means they bore the turtles away together. *Qu* means to go; *shu*, to count their bones. *Shi ai* means to make narrow. The immortals of the five mountains, the three shifts of the fifteen turtles, the giant of Longbo who fished for turtles, and the Emperor’s wrath—all are allegories. Many Buddhist scriptures contain the like, such as the Thirty-Three Heavens, the Land of Fragrant Accumulation, and the Western Pure Land.

Four hundred thousand *li* east of the Central States lies the land of Jiao Yao, where people stand but a foot and a half tall. In the far northeast lives a people called the Contentious, who measure nine inches. Though the people of Longbo were diminished, they still stood thousands of *zhang* tall; the Jiao Yao, a foot and a half; the Contentious, nine inches. The tallest reach the utmost in height, the shortest the utmost in shortness—yet this only shows that transformations between heaven and earth are not constant, and one cannot take what the eyes and ears perceive as fixed.

South of Jing lies the Dark Numinous, where five hundred years make a spring and five hundred years an autumn. In high antiquity stood the Great Camphor, where eight thousand years made a spring and eight thousand years an autumn. Upon rotting soil grow fungi and mushrooms that sprout at dawn and wither by dusk. In the spring and summer months appear midges and gnats, born with the rain and dying at the sun’s touch.

North of the Northernmost Peaks lies the Dark Sea—the Celestial Pond. There dwells a fish, thousands of *li* in breadth, its length proportionate, named the *Hun*. There too is a bird, named the *Peng*, its wings like clouds trailing from the sky, its body in equal measure. Who in the world would know of such creatures? Great Yu encountered them in his travels; Boyi named them; Yijian heard of them and recorded them."

The *Mingling* is the name of a tree. *Zhongfa* is the same as *qiongfa*—the farthest reaches of the north, a land ever more remote. *Cheng* is read in the falling tone, meaning its length matches its breadth. If its wings are so vast, its body must match them in scale—thus its enormity may be imagined. What mortals see is small; how could they know there are such boundless expanses in heaven and earth? These are all allegories, yet Yu and Yi lend them substance.

The world says the *Shanhaijing*’s *Dahuangjing* was written by Yu, much as people today claim Zhang Qian reached the Celestial River—its purpose merely to invoke Yu’s governance of the Nine Provinces and Boyi’s stewardship of mountains and marshes, thus lending credence to the tale. *Yijian* serves the same role as Zhuangzi’s *Qixie*.

Between the riverbanks grows a tiny insect called the *jiaoming*, swarming in flight to alight upon a mosquito’s lashes, jostling one another in a seething mass. The mosquito remains unaware as they come and go. Li Zhu and Zi Yu, though they rubbed their brows and strained their eyes in broad daylight, could not perceive its form; Jiao Hu, Chou Zhi, Wen Er, Pu Mi, and Shi Kuang, though they cupped their ears and bent their heads in the dead of night, could not catch its sound.

Only the Yellow Emperor and Rongchengzi dwelled upon the heights of Kongtong, aligning their spirits for three months, their hearts stilled and forms abandoned. Gradually, with vision of the spirit, they beheld it as a block—like the flank of Mount Song. Gradually, with hearing of qi, they perceived it with a crash—like the sound of thunder. Thus arose the contention of petty scholars and the discourse on the *jiaoming*, the smallest of the small.

Jiaohu Yu was also one of the ancients skilled in hearing. This aligns with Zhuangzi’s claim that listening with the ear is not as keen as listening with qi, and listening with qi is not as keen as listening with the heart.

In the lands of Wu and Chu there stands a great tree called the *you*, its verdant branches bearing fruit in winter, its crimson flesh tart to the taste. Drinking its sap cures the ailment of *fenjue*. The Central States prize it, yet when carried north across the Huai River, it transforms into the *zhu*. The stork does not cross the Ji River, and the raccoon dog perishes if it crosses the Wen—such is the nature of earthly qi.

Yet though forms and qi differ, their natures remain equal; none may exchange places, for all life is whole, and all portions are sufficient. How then do I discern the great from the small? The long from the short? The same from the different?

The *you* is the mandarin orange. These several lines echo the *Kaogong Ji*, illustrating the instability of form and qi, thereby revealing the workings of creation. Observing all things impartially, their natures are alike—each thing possesses its own nature, none may be interchanged. Each thing fulfills its life, each thing suffices in its allotted part. Whether great or small, long or short, all follow the principle of creation—what is different? What is the same? These lines stand firm in their rectitude.

*Yi* marks the end of speech.

The Taihang and Wangwu Mountains spanned seven hundred *li* square, towering ten thousand *ren* high, originally situated south of Jizhou and north of Heyang. There lived an old man of ninety from the Northern Mountains, named the Foolish Old Man, who dwelt facing the mountains. Vexed by the blockage to the north and the circuitous paths for entry and exit, he gathered his household to deliberate, saying: "Shall we join forces to level the perilous heights, open a passage south to Yu, and reach the northern bank of the Han River? Is this possible?" They all assented in unison.

His wife raised a doubt: "With your strength, you cannot even reduce the mound of Kuifu—how then can you move the Taihang and Wangwu? Moreover, where would you dispose of the earth and stones?" The assembly replied: "Cast them into the tail of the Bohai Sea, north of the Hidden Land." Thereupon, he led his children and grandchildren—three men capable of bearing loads—striking stones and tilling soil, transporting the debris in baskets to the tail of the Bohai Sea. The widow of the Jingcheng family from the neighborhood had a young orphan, just past his first teeth-shedding, who leapt forth to assist them. Winter and summer changed seasons before they made a single return trip.

The Wise Old Man of Hequ laughed and sought to dissuade them, saying: "How utterly lacking in wisdom you are! With your waning years and remaining strength, you cannot even strip a single hair from the mountain—what then can you do with the earth and stones?" The Foolish Old Man of the Northern Mountains sighed deeply and replied: "Your heart is so obstinate, so utterly impervious—you are not even the equal of a widow or a weak child. Though I may die, I have sons who survive. Sons beget grandsons, grandsons beget sons, sons again beget sons, and sons again beget grandsons—descendants without end. Yet the mountains will not grow taller. How then can they not be leveled?" The Wise Old Man of Hequ had no reply.

The Spirit Who Wields the Serpent heard of this and, fearing their unceasing efforts, reported it to the Emperor. Moved by their sincerity, the Emperor commanded the two sons of Kuafu to bear the two mountains away—one placed east of Shuofang, the other south of Yong. From then on, there were no more ridges or barriers south of Ji or north of the Han River.

Taihang is the same as Taihang, the names being phonetically similar. "Open a passage" means to clear a road southward. Hidden Land is a place name in the north. "Leapt forth" signifies rushing forward with vigor. "Changed seasons" refers to a full year. "Lacking in wisdom" means lacking understanding. "Obstinate" means closed off. Though the words of this passage may seem impractical, they vividly convey the unyielding resolve, which is deeply meaningful.

The Buddhist scriptures speak of the Bodhisattva Putuo, who in his early practice endured hardship and saw nothing. As he prepared to descend the mountain, he encountered a man by the water grinding an iron bar. Asking him, "What is the use of grinding this?" The man replied, "I will make it into a needle." The Bodhisattva laughed and said, "Are you not foolish? Can an iron bar be ground into a needle?" The man replied, "If not in this life, then in the next? If not in the next, then in the one after?" The Bodhisattva was greatly enlightened, returned to Putuo, and later attained the Way. Though such words may seem impractical, I have always found them meaningful and beneficial to scholars. If all people held such resolve, what task could not be accomplished? What learning could not be mastered?

Su Dongpo said: "Proceed gradually, and in ten years, what cannot be established? The only fear is that this resolve is not steadfast, or the effort not vigorous enough." Though Dongpo’s words seem simple, upon reflection, the unwavering diligence of Yao, the meticulous care of Shun, the daily renewal of Tang, and the ceaseless purity of King Wen—all arise from this single thought.

The Spirit Who Wields the Serpent and Kuafu are names of deities. "No ridges or barriers" means the land was entirely flat, without even small mounds or anthills.

Kuafu, overestimating his strength, sought to chase the sun’s shadow, pursuing it to the edge of Yugu. Thirsting for drink, he rushed to the Yellow and Wei Rivers, but they were insufficient. He then set out to drink from the Great Marsh in the north. Before reaching it, he died of thirst on the way. His abandoned staff, soaked in the fat and flesh of his corpse, grew into the Deng Forest. The Deng Forest spread thousands of *li* wide. Yugu is where the sun sets. Kuafu’s staff transformed into the Deng Forest, which stretched thousands of *li*—Kuafu was likely of the same kind as the Longbo giants. This must be an ancient legend of strange marvels, which the Daoist adepts incorporated into their texts as material for grand discourse.

Yu the Great said: "Within the Six Directions and the Four Seas, illuminated by the sun and moon, ordered by the stars, governed by the four seasons, and constrained by the Great Year—all things born of the divine and spiritual take their forms, some dying young, some living long. Only the sage can comprehend their principles."

Xia Ge replied: "Yet there are also those not born of the divine and spiritual, not shaped by yin and yang, not illuminated by the sun and moon, not cut short by violence, not prolonged by nurture, not nourished by the five grains, not clothed in silk and floss, not conveyed by boats and carts. Their Way is natural, beyond the sage’s comprehension."

Xia Ge engaged in dialogue with Tang and later with Yu—were these two different Xia Ges? Or the same one? If the same, he must have lived for hundreds or thousands of years.

"Born of the divine and spiritual" refers to the sun, moon, yin, yang, and the Great Year. The previous passage concludes with the divine and spiritual, while this one begins with it—such is the flow of the text.

Yu said: "All things with form, whether dying young or living long, have their principles, which only the sage fully understands." Ge replied: "Yet there are also those not dependent on yin and yang or the sun and moon to exist. Stone eggs and stone seeds—what need have they for the qi of yin and yang? Earth ants and ground dragons—what need have they for the light of the sun and moon? Morning mushrooms and cicadas—do they die young by violence? Pines and cypresses of the southern mountains—do they live long by nurture? Moths and reeds—do they eat the five grains to be full? Oxen and horses—do they wear silk and floss to be warm? Birds—do they ride boats and carts to travel?"

These phenomena arise of themselves, beyond the reach of ordinary reason—even the sage cannot fully comprehend them.

The *Tai Sui* is the spirit that governs the year. The practice of divination by days has existed since antiquity, yet this illustrates how some matters in the world may be deduced by common principles, while others defy them—such is the marvel of creation’s workings.

When Yu governed the waters and lands, he became lost and strayed into a foreign realm. North of the northern sea, beyond the known distance of thousands or tens of thousands of *li* from the Central States, lay a land called *Zhongbei*, its borders unbounded, its limits uncharted. There, no wind or rain, frost or dew fell, nor did birds, beasts, insects, fish, grasses, or trees grow. All directions stretched flat, encircled by towering ridges.

At the land’s center stood a mountain named *Huling*, shaped like an overturned jar. At its summit yawned an opening, round as a ring, called the *Zi Xue* ("Nourishing Cavern"). From it gushed forth a spring named *Shenfen* ("Divine Spring"), its fragrance surpassing orchid and pepper, its taste richer than fine wine. A single source divided into four channels, flowing down the mountain to irrigate the entire land without exception.

The earth’s *qi* was harmonious, sparing the people from plague or affliction. Their nature was gentle, yielding to things without contention; their hearts were soft, their bones supple, free from arrogance or envy. Young and old dwelled together without rulers or subjects; men and women mingled freely without matchmakers or betrothals. They settled along the waters, neither plowing nor sowing; the earth’s warmth sufficed, so they neither wove nor wore clothes. They lived a hundred years without premature death or illness.

Their people multiplied endlessly, ever joyful, never aging or sorrowful. Fond of music, they clasped hands and sang in turns, their voices unceasing all day. When hungry or weary, they drank from the *Shenfen*, their strength and spirits harmonized. If they overindulged, they grew drunk, waking only after ten days. Bathing in the *Shenfen* left their skin sleek and lustrous, its fragrance lingering for ten days.

*Zhongbei* lay at the world’s northernmost extreme—*Qi* means "limit."

Limit means extremity. Its borders stretch without end or bound. *Qiaozhi* is a lofty mountain; *Huling* belongs to the same class as *Fanghu* and *Yuanqiao*. *Danzhui* are earthenware vessels. The waters of *Zixue* are called *Shenfen*, flowing from a single source yet branching to all four sides. *Lie* means "boundary." "Encircling a kingdom" describes how this water winds around a land. "Gentle and yielding to things" means compliance. "Weak bones" means no striving for contention. *Zifu* means to multiply; *fu* means abundance. From "its fragrance lingering for ten days" onward, this passage recounts what Yu witnessed.

King Mu of Zhou journeyed north and passed through that land, lingering for three years and forgetting to return. Once back in the Zhou court, he longed for that kingdom, lost in melancholy. He refused wine and meat, summoned no concubines, and only after months did he recover. This account, building upon the legend of King Mu’s eight steeds, adds further testimony.

Guan Zhong urged Duke Huan of Qi to follow the example of the northern expedition, nearly succeeding in reaching that land. Xi Peng remonstrated: "My lord, why forsake the vastness of Qi, its teeming people, its majestic mountains and rivers, its fertile lands, its flourishing rites and virtues, its splendid robes, its palace filled with beauty, its court brimming with loyal ministers? With a shout, a million soldiers rise; with a gesture, the feudal lords obey. Why envy that land and abandon Qi’s altars of soil and grain to follow barbarians?" He continued: "Is this the senility of our elder statesman? How can you heed such counsel?" Duke Huan desisted and relayed Xi Peng’s words to Guan Zhong. Guan replied: "This is beyond Peng’s understanding. I fear that land cannot be ascended. Why cling to Qi’s wealth? Why heed Peng’s words?" This passage also alludes to Qi’s southward voyage along the coast to Langya.

"Nearly succeeding" means they almost set out at once. "Shouting at will" means issuing commands with unrestrained authority. "Directing with a glance" means guiding troops with a mere look. "That land cannot be ascended" means they feared it might prove unattainable. Such words merely suggest that beyond the known world lie realms surpassing human perception.

In the southern lands, people shave their heads and go bare; in the north, they wear felt caps and furs; in the Central States, they don crowns and robes. Across the nine domains, people rely on farming or trade, tilling or fishing—winter furs and summer linen, boats for water and carts for land—all acquired instinctively, shaped by nature.
