This idea appears elsewhere in my writings. Beyond the Central States, customs differ, and ways of dress and sustenance vary. Each follows its nature and finds joy in it. This is no different: it merely broadens what people have seen.

East of Yue lies the land of *Zhexiu*, where the eldest son is slaughtered and eaten at birth, deemed "fitting for younger brothers." When a grandfather dies, they carry the grandmother away and abandon her, saying: "A ghost’s wife cannot dwell with the living." South of Chu lies the land of the *Yanren*, where upon a relative’s death, they carve and share the flesh before discarding it, then bury the bones, thus becoming filial. West of Qin lies the land of *Yiqu* and *Wenkang*, where upon a relative’s death, they pile firewood and burn the body. The rising smoke is called "ascending afar," and only then are they deemed filial. Such practices govern both governance above and customs below, yet they are not so strange.

*Zhexiu*, *Yanren*, and *Yiqu* are all names of lands. *Xiu* means "to carve." This passage also appears in the *Mozi*; the *Annals of the Eastern Yi* from the Two Han and the records of the Jin dynasty contain similar accounts. Liezi’s intent is simply this: within the world, lands differ, and customs vary. Must all resemble the Central States to be deemed good? What we cherish, how do we know it is not what they despise?

Confucius traveled east and saw two children arguing. He asked why. One child said: "I believe the sun is nearer at dawn and farther at noon." The other insisted the opposite: "The sun is farther at dawn and nearer at noon." The first child argued: "At dawn, the sun is as large as a carriage canopy, but at noon, it shrinks to the size of a plate or bowl. Is this not proof that what is far appears small, and what is near appears large?" The second child countered: "At dawn, the sun is cool and refreshing, but at noon, it burns like boiling water. Is this not proof that what is near is hot, and what is far is cool?" Confucius could not decide. The children laughed: "Who says you are so wise?" This debate resembles the argument between Prince Chang’an of Jin and others over the sun’s distance.

The passage’s meaning is that near and far, right and wrong, cannot be settled by a single principle. Balance is the supreme principle of the world. Even in tangible things, this holds true. When hair is evenly weighted and suspended, it may bear a load without breaking; if uneven, it snaps. If balanced, it will not break even when strained. Some deny this, but there are those who know its truth.

This passage introduces the concept of *balance*: the supreme principle of the world applies even to all things with form. *Lian* means "all." *Xingwu* refers to tangible things. "This holds true" means that just as the principle applies, so do things. When hair is evenly suspended, it can bear weight, hence "evenly weighted, evenly suspended." If the load is uneven, the hair breaks. The cause of breaking is imbalance, hence "uneven weight causes breaking, for it is not balanced." If weight is evenly distributed, it will not break even when strained, hence "balanced, it will not break." This line is particularly profound. "Balanced" is one phrase; "it will not break" is another.

This expounds the argument of Gongsun Long regarding a single hair lifting a thousand *jun*. Most people deem it impossible, yet there are those who recognize its truth—meaning the world at large remains unaware, while those who understand the Dao know its validity.

Zhan He, using a single silkworm’s thread for line, a needle’s barb for hook, a bramble stalk for rod, and a grain husk for bait, drew forth fish as large as carts from abyssal depths and rushing currents. His line never snapped, his hook never bent, his rod never bowed. The King of Chu, hearing of this marvel, summoned him to inquire the reason.

Zhan He replied: "I recall the words of my late master. When Pujuzi shot birds, he used a supple bow and fine cord, riding the wind to strike, bringing down a pair of wagtails amid the azure clouds. His mind was single, his hand steady and balanced. I took his method as my model and applied it to angling, mastering its Way only after five years. When I stand by the river with rod in hand, my heart holds no stray thoughts—only the fish. Casting the line and sinking the hook, my hand moves with neither lightness nor heaviness, and nothing can disturb it. To the fish, my hook and bait appear no more than drifting dust or gathered foam, swallowed without hesitation. Thus the weak overcomes the strong, the light attains the heavy. If Your Majesty governs the state with such focus, the realm may be held in the palm of one’s hand—what further effort would be needed?"

The king said, "Well spoken."

Zhan He’s angling, Pujuzi’s archery, and the Hunchback Elder’s cicada-catching share a common principle: governing a state or the world is like stilling the mind—without force or weakness, without lightness or weight. The weak may then overcome the strong, the light may overcome the heavy, echoing Laozi’s teaching that the yielding prevails over the rigid.

Lu Gongfu and Zhao Qiying, both afflicted with illness, sought treatment from Bian Que. Bian Que healed them.

Having cured them both, Bian Que addressed Gongfu and Qiying: "The illnesses you suffered before were external invasions of the six bowels and five viscera, readily healed by medicine and stone. Now, however, you bear afflictions born with your bodies, grown with your growth. If I were to treat these for you, how would you respond?"

The two replied, "We would first hear the proof."

Bian Que said to Gongfu, "Your will is strong but your qi weak—thus you excel in planning yet falter in decision." To Qiying he said, "Your will is weak but your qi strong—thus you lack forethought yet act with reckless force. Were I to exchange your hearts, both would attain balance in virtue."

Thereupon Bian Que gave them a poisoned wine to drink, plunging them into a three-day stupor. He split open their chests, extracted their hearts, swapped them, and restored them; then administered a divine medicine. When they awoke, they were as before, and took their leave to return home.

Yet Gongfu went to Qiying’s house and claimed his wife and children, who did not recognize him. Likewise, Qiying went to Gongfu’s house and claimed his wife and children, who also did not recognize him. The two households then brought suit against each other, seeking Bian Que’s judgment.

Bian Que explained the cause, and the dispute was settled.

This passage depicts how the heart is shaped by qi, leaving one not entirely free. Its words carry profound meaning—though seemingly impractical, they are not. As Master Mingdao said: "Of the one hundred and four illnesses, all arise from another’s heart; only when they begin in mine may they be mastered." This teaching rises even higher.

Liezi’s analogy speaks of the heart of qi-nature; Mingdao’s words, of the heart of principle. Only by transforming the heart of qi-nature with the heart of principle may one attain true freedom.

When Huba played the zither, birds danced and fish leapt. Hearing of this, Zheng Shiwen abandoned his home to study under Master Xiang.

Pressing his fingers upon the pegs to tune the seventh string, for three years he could not complete a single piece. Master Xiang said, "You may return home now." Shiwen set aside his zither and sighed, "It is not that my fingers cannot tune the strings, nor that I cannot complete a piece. What I seek lies not in the strings, nor does my aspiration rest in sound. Within, it does not settle in my heart; without, it does not respond to qi. Thus I dare not lift my hand to strike the strings. Grant me a little more time to observe what follows."

Before long, he again sought out Master Xiang. Master Xiang asked, "How fares your zither now?" Shiwen replied, "I have grasped it. Pray, let me demonstrate." Thereupon, in spring, he struck the *shang* string to summon *Nanlü*—a chill wind suddenly arose, and plants bore fruit. In autumn, he struck the *jiao* string to rouse *Jiazhong*—a warm breeze gently returned, and plants flourished. In summer, he struck the *yu* string to invoke *Huangzhong*—frost and snow fell together, and rivers and ponds froze over. In winter, he struck the *zhi* string to harmonize with *Rui Bin*—the sun’s rays blazed fiercely, and solid ice instantly melted. As the piece drew to a close, he commanded the *gong* string to unite the four, and a balmy breeze soared, auspicious clouds floated, sweet dew descended, and sweet springs welled forth.

Master Xiang then clapped his chest and leapt in praise, exclaiming, "How subtle is your playing! Even Shi Kuang’s *Qingjiao* or Zou Yan’s breath through the pitch-pipes cannot surpass it. They would take up zither and flute to follow in your wake."

"Not completing a piece" means he could not yet finish even one melody. "Pressing his fingers upon the pegs" refers to steadying his fingers, and "tuning the strings" to adjusting them. "Lies not in the strings, nor does my aspiration rest in sound" signifies that his heart was not yet at ease. When the heart is settled and the hands respond, one forgets the instrument. Until this is achieved, he dared not move the strings. "Grant me a little more time" means a brief respite, and "to observe what follows" to see how things would unfold afterward.

In spring, he played an autumnal sound, and autumnal qi responded; in autumn, he played a vernal sound, and vernal qi responded; in summer, he played a wintry sound, and wintry qi responded; in winter, he played a summery sound, and summery qi responded. The *shang* string belongs to autumn, the *jiao* to spring, the *yu* to winter, and the *zhi* to summer. *Gong* is the central tone, thus harmonious qi answered. The zither has five strings, each governing one note. When it says he struck a particular string, it does not mean he played only one while neglecting the others, but rather that a given piece was led by *shang*, another by *jiao*, and so on. This illustrates how the marvel of sound can penetrate the workings of creation.

Shiwen’s visits to Master Xiang seem to occur within a single day—how could he traverse all four seasons, striking and eliciting responses in turn? From this, we may know it is an allegory.

"How subtle is your playing" means his zither playing was exquisitely subtle. *Qingjiao* is the name of a melody. "Take up zither and flute to follow in your wake" means that even Shi Kuang and Zou Yan would have to study under him.

Xue Tan learned singing from Qin Qing but had not mastered his teacher’s skill. Believing he had exhausted it, he took his leave to return home. Qin Qing did not stop him. At the crossroads outside the city, he gave a farewell feast, beating time and singing a mournful song. The sound shook the trees, and its resonance halted the passing clouds. Xue Tan then apologized and begged to return, vowing never again to speak of leaving. Qin Qing turned to his friends and said, "Long ago, Han E traveled east to Qi, her provisions exhausted. Passing through Yongmen, she sold her songs to beg for food. After she left, her lingering notes clung to the roof beams for three days without fading, and the townsfolk thought she had not yet departed. When she stayed at an inn, the innkeeper insulted her. Han E then let forth a long, sorrowful wail, and the elders and children of the village grieved, weeping face to face, going three days without eating. They hastily pursued her. When Han E returned, she sang a long, joyous song, and the villagers leapt and danced, unable to restrain themselves, forgetting their former sorrow. They then richly rewarded and sent her on her way. Thus, to this day, the people of Yongmen are skilled in singing and weeping, imitating Han E’s lingering tones."

"Beating time" means keeping the rhythm. "Provisions exhausted" means lacking supplies. "Sold her songs to beg for food" means she sang to earn her meals. "Sent her on her way" means they thanked and escorted her. This story also appears in the *Mencius*.

Building upon Master Wen’s discourse on the zither, the account now turns to the matter of singers, recording both to illustrate how the mastery of craft may attain such divine wonder. If mere skill can achieve this, how much more might those who study the Dao attain the supremely divine and wondrous? This is the unspoken implication.

Boya excelled at playing the zither, while Zhong Ziqi excelled at listening. When Boya played, his intent was upon the lofty mountains. Zhong Ziqi said, “How splendid! Towering like Mount Tai!” When his intent was upon flowing waters, Zhong Ziqi said, “How splendid! Vast like the Yangtze and Yellow River!” Whatever Boya conceived in his mind, Zhong Ziqi invariably grasped.

Once, Boya traveled to the northern slopes of Mount Tai when suddenly a violent storm arose. He took shelter beneath a cliff, and his heart grew sorrowful. Taking up his zither, he began to play. First came the melody *Endless Rain*, then he composed the sound *Mountain’s Collapse*. With each piece he performed, Zhong Ziqi exhaustively fathomed its essence. Boya then set aside his zither and sighed, “How splendid! How splendid! Your listening is truly remarkable. Your thoughts and imaginings are as my own heart. How could I ever conceal my music from you?”

*Endless Rain* and *Mountain’s Collapse* are both names of zither compositions. “Your thoughts and imaginings” refers to Zhong Ziqi, meaning his heart was one with Boya’s. Since music arises from the heart, if your heart is the same as mine, you naturally understand my music. “How could I conceal?” means there is no hiding it. This tale, handed down from ancient times, was adopted into the text to illustrate that in all things under heaven, whether refined or coarse, there exists the possibility of attaining divine wonder.

King Mu of Zhou, on his western expedition, crossed the Kunlun Mountains but did not reach Yan Mountain. On his return journey, before reaching the Central States, he encountered a craftsman named Yan Shi who had come to offer his services. The king received him and asked, “What skills do you possess?” Yan Shi replied, “Your servant will attempt whatever Your Majesty commands. However, I have already created something I wish you to see first.” The king said, “Come together tomorrow, and we shall both observe it.”

The next day, Yan Shi presented himself to the king, who received him and asked, “Who is the person accompanying you?” He replied, “This is a singing and dancing performer I have created.” The king gazed at it in astonishment. It walked, bowed, and lifted its head, behaving exactly like a real person. When the craftsman pressed its chin, it sang in perfect harmony with the rhythm. When he took its hand, it danced in precise accord with the beat, transforming endlessly at will. The king believed it to be a real person and, together with Lady Sheng and his inner attendants, observed it closely.

As the performance neared its end, the performer winked at the king’s concubines on his left and right. The king grew furious and was about to execute Yan Shi on the spot. Terrified, Yan Shi immediately disassembled the performer before the king’s eyes, revealing it to be an assemblage of leather, wood, glue, lacquer, white, black, red, and blue materials. The king examined it carefully: within were liver, gallbladder, heart, lungs, spleen, kidneys, intestines, and stomach; without were sinews, bones, joints, skin, hair, teeth, and nails—all artificial yet perfectly complete. When reassembled, it appeared as it had at first.

The king then tested it: when he disabled its heart, its mouth could not speak; when he disabled its liver, its eyes could not see; when he disabled its kidneys, its feet could not walk. King Mu at last marveled and sighed, "Can human skill truly rival the work of creation?" He ordered his secondary chariot to carry it back.

Now, Lu Ban’s cloud ladder and Mo Di’s flying kite were deemed the pinnacle of their craft. Yet when their disciples Dongmen Jia and Qin Guli heard of Yan Shi’s skill, they dared not speak of their own arts for the rest of their lives, and from then on wielded only compass and square.

Yanshan lies west of Kunlun.

*Jian* means "to present." In the *Book of Han*, *jinshen* is written with *jian* as well.

"To come together tomorrow" means to arrive in company the next day.

Its every movement—hurrying, stepping, bowing, rising—was entirely lifelike.

*Xin* means "truly."

The king marveled at the artisan’s skill.

*Zhen* means "to press."

When its mouth was pressed to make it sing, its voice harmonized with the pitch; when its hands were taken to make it dance, its steps matched the rhythm. At first, it seemed merely to resemble a person, but after a time, it became indistinguishable from one.

Lady Sheng was one among the king’s many consorts.

*Zhao* means "to tease."

*Di* means "to examine closely."

*Liao* means "to inspect."

"Reassembled as before" means that after being disassembled, it was put back together, and its singing and dancing were just as they had been at first.

That a wooden figure could walk and dance was astonishing enough—how then did it produce sound? This suggests that the human body, too, is but an assemblage of parts: the eyes correspond to the liver, the feet to the kidneys, the mouth to the heart—none of it truly under one’s control. The Buddhist doctrine of the Four Elements is not unlike this.

If human skill can rival creation, how much greater is the skill of creation itself?

The secondary chariot was a spare carriage.

The cloud ladder was a siege engine; the flying kite was also made of wood. This is akin to the wooden ball of Snow Peak.

Lu Ban and Mo Di considered their works the height of craftsmanship. Yet compared to Yan Shi, their skills were nothing—so much so that they never again dared claim mastery of their arts. "From time to time wielding compass and square" means that the two abandoned their crafts, resorting to them only when necessary.

Dongmen Jia and Qin Guli were the names of the two disciples.

Gan Ying was an ancient master archer who, when he drew his bow, caused beasts to crouch and birds to descend. His disciple, Fei Wei, learned archery from him and surpassed his teacher. Another disciple, Ji Chang, studied under Fei Wei.

Fei Wei said to him, "First learn not to blink, and only then may we speak of archery." Ji Chang returned home and lay beneath his wife’s loom, gazing upward at the heddle rod. After two years, even when a needle’s tip was turned toward his eye, he did not blink. He told this to Fei Wei.

Fei Wei said, "Not yet. Next, learn to see, and only then may you return." He instructed him to see the small as large, the faint as clear, and only then report back. Ji Chang hung a louse from the window with a yak’s hair and faced south to observe it. Within ten days, it gradually grew larger; after three years, it was like a cartwheel. When he looked at other things, they all appeared as mountains and hills. Then he took a bow of Yan horn and an arrow of northern reeds and shot the louse, piercing its heart while the hair remained unbroken. He told this to Fei Wei.

Fei Wei leapt up, clapped his chest, and exclaimed, "You have mastered it!"

The heddle rod was the part of the loom that moved up and down with the treadles. Though a needle’s tip was turned toward his eye, he did not blink—just as Mencius spoke of "not fleeing from the eye."

"Next, learn to see" means to proceed step by step, to further refine his vision.

Once the louse appeared as large as a cartwheel, all other things seemed like mountains and hills.

The bow of Yan horn was made from the horn of a Yan ox; the arrow of northern reeds was made from the reeds of the northern plains. These were the finest of bows and arrows.

To shoot a louse as if it were a cartwheel—such precision in craft is like Bian Que, who could see through walls. Truly extraordinary.

In all worldly matters, without mastery, one cannot speak of them.

After Ji Chang had mastered Fei Wei’s techniques, he calculated that only one man in the world could rival him and plotted to kill Fei Wei. They met in the wilds, and the two exchanged arrows. Midway, their arrowheads clashed and fell to the ground without stirring dust. Fei Wei’s arrows were exhausted first. Ji Chang loosed one more, but as it flew, Fei Wei deflected it with the tip of a thorn, not missing by a hair. Thereupon, the two wept, cast aside their bows, bowed to each other on the road, and pledged to become father and son. They cut their arms and swore an oath never to reveal this to outsiders.

"Their arrows clashed midway" means they shot at each other on the road. Their arrows met and fell to the ground, dust undisturbed, so evenly did they land.

To deflect an incoming arrow with the tip of a thorn and meet it without error—such was their skill.

"Cutting their arms" means they sliced their arms as a mark of their oath.

This tale seems far-fetched.

Once, while traveling along the Huai River, I met a man who divided the paths at Xuanlu. He was nearly seventy years old and had won first place in the *Spring and Autumn Grand Review* every year. He once told me: "When I first captured Wang Xin, we met in the mountains of Liu’an. Wang Xin drew his bow to shoot me. All I held was a short wooden spear. I called out to him: ‘Here, I’ll let you loose three arrows. If you miss, surrender to me, and I’ll take you to Prefect Zhao to secure you an official post.’ Wang Xin loosed three arrows, but I struck each one down with my spear. He then bowed to me. Later, Wang Xin became the military commissioner of Guangzhou. When I arrived in Anfeng, one of Wang Xin’s former officers also recounted this tale, matching the account I had heard from the old man."

The old man’s name has slipped my mind. Yet if even the archery exchanges of Jichang and Fei Wei are not to be dismissed as fabrications, then how much more must this be true?

Zaofu’s master was called Taidou. When Zaofu first began his apprenticeship in charioteering, he observed the rites with utmost humility; Taidou gave no instruction for three years. Zaofu’s reverence only deepened, until at last Taidou spoke: “An ancient poem says, ‘The son of a master bowyer must first weave baskets; the son of a master smith must first sew furs.’ First observe my gait. When your gait matches mine, then you may grasp the six reins and drive the six horses.” Zaofu replied, “I shall follow your command.”

Taidou then planted wooden stakes to form a path barely wide enough for a foot. He measured the steps and placed them, then walked the line, pacing back and forth without stumbling. Zaofu learned this in three days, mastering its intricacy. Taidou sighed, “How swift your grasp! You have acquired it with such speed. All charioteering is like this. When you walked just now, you gained it through your feet, and your heart responded. Extend this to driving: harmony in the reins and bit, swiftness or slowness in the harmony of the lips; correct measure within the chest, and rhythmic control in the hands. Inwardly centered in the heart, outwardly aligned with the horse’s intent—thus you may advance and retreat along a cord’s breadth, turn in perfect arcs, traverse great distances with energy to spare, truly mastering the art.

Gained through the bit, it responds in the reins; gained through the reins, it responds in the hands; gained through the hands, it responds in the heart. Then you need not rely on sight or whip; the mind is at ease, the body aligned, the six reins untangled, and the twenty-four hooves strike without error. In turning, advancing, or retreating, all is in perfect measure. Beyond the wheels’ track, no trace remains; beyond the horses’ hooves, no ground is wasted. Neither steep valleys nor level plains are perceived as different—they are as one.

My art is exhausted; remember it well.” Taidou was indeed one of the ancient masters of charioteering.

“Fur and basket” is an old saying, already seen in the *Record of Learning*. Learning archery begins with basket-weaving—both are bamboo crafts. *Ye* means metalworking; what does it have to do with fur? This phrase has long been obscure. Yet the *Artificers’ Record* mentions a “Fur Official,” though his duties are unknown. Since the post is now vacant, perhaps his role once related to smithing—now lost to time.

“Observe my gait” means to learn his way of walking. “Acquired it swiftly” refers to his rapid mastery.

The reins and bit rest in the horse’s mouth; the chest and hands belong to the driver. “Walking the cord and turning” means that though the path is as narrow as a cord, one may still turn upon it. “Perfect arcs” means mastery in alignment. “Energy to spare” means the driver is not wearied—akin to the *Artificers’ Record*’s saying, “his lap does not cover [the reins].” “Mind at ease, body aligned” describes the driver’s composure. “Twenty-four hooves” refers to a six-horse chariot. “Strike without error” means the horses move in unison. *Zhe* means the track, the path the chariot follows. “No trace beyond the wheels” means the chariot does not stray from the track. “No ground beyond the hooves” means that if the hooves do not falter, no extra space is needed. Though mountains and valleys are perilous, plains and lowlands level, to the driver they are all the same.

Walking on wooden stakes may seem far-fetched, but seeing acrobats today who climb poles, tread ropes, and balance with ease, we know such feats exist. When *Liezi* recounts this, it is merely to show that even worldly skills attain such marvels—how much more so the Dao?

Wei Hei Luan killed Qiu Bingzhang out of personal enmity. Bingzhang’s son, Lai Dan, vowed to avenge his father. Dan’s qi was fierce, his form exposed; he ate by counting grains, moved with the wind, and though enraged, could not match Qiu’s strength to exact revenge. Ashamed to borrow another’s power, he swore to slay Hei Luan with his own hands.

Hei Luan’s ferocity surpassed all others; his strength equaled a hundred men. His sinews, bones, skin, and flesh were not of mortal kind. He stretched his neck to meet blades, bared his chest to arrows, and though blades bent against him, his body bore no mark. Confident in his might, he regarded Lai Dan as a mere fledgling.

Lai Dan’s friend Shen Ta said, “Your hatred for Hei Luan is profound, and his disdain for you is extreme. What plan will you devise?” Lai Dan wept, “I beg you to counsel me.” Shen Ta replied, “I have heard that Kong Zhou of Wei, a descendant of his lineage, possesses a treasure sword from the Yin emperor. When a mere boy wielded it, he repelled an entire army. Why not seek his aid?”

Lai Dan then traveled to Wei and met Kong Zhou, serving him with the rites of a groom. He first entrusted his wife and children to Kong, then stated his request. Kong Zhou said, “I have three swords; choose as you will. None can kill a man, but first let me describe them.

The first is called *Han Guang* (Containing Light). It cannot be seen when looked upon, and its presence is unknown when wielded. Where it touches, all is indistinct, passing through things without their awareness.

The second is called *Cheng Ying* (Bearing Shadow). At the cusp of dawn, in the dim light between night and day, or at dusk, when light and dark mingle, if you face north and observe it, it appears faintly as if something exists, though its form cannot be discerned. Where it touches, there is a faint sound, passing through things without harming them.

The third is called *Xiao Lian* (Night Forged). By day, its shadow is visible but not its light; by night, its light is visible but not its form. Where it touches, it passes swiftly, sealing the wound as it goes, cutting without drawing blood.

These three treasures have been passed down for thirteen generations, yet never put to use. They lie sealed in their caskets, never opened.” Lai Dan said, “Even so, I must request the lowest of them.”

Kong Zhou then returned Lai Dan’s wife and children and fasted with him for seven days. At dusk, he knelt and presented the lowest sword. Lai Dan bowed twice, received it, and departed.

Lai Dan then grasped the sword and followed Black Egg. At that moment, Black Egg lay drunk beneath the window, and Lai Dan struck him three times from neck to waist. Black Egg remained unaware. Taking Black Egg’s death for granted, Lai Dan hurried away. At the gate, he encountered Black Egg’s son and struck him three times, as if swinging into emptiness. Black Egg’s son laughed and said, “Why so foolish as to wave at me thrice?” Lai Dan, realizing the sword could not kill, sighed and returned home.

When Black Egg awoke, he raged at his wife, “You let me drink until I exposed myself, leaving my throat sore and my waist stiff.” His son said, “Earlier, Lai Dan came and met me at the gate, waving at me three times. It left my body aching and my limbs rigid—he must have cursed me.” *Nìxián* refers to private grudges. His qi was fierce, his might sharp. His form was exposed, his frame gaunt. Fleeing with the wind, his steps were weak. His resolve surpassed all, his courage and ferocity unmatched. *Héntà* means marks or traces. *Chúkòu* refers to newly hatched birds. “Exchanging children” is a grave insult. The names of the three swords are regional terms for hexing techniques. *Mǐnrán wú jì* means vanishing without trace or boundary. *Jiāng dàn mèi shuǎng zhī jiāo* is the moment when dawn breaks and the sun rises. *Dàn xī hūn míng zhī jì* is the time when day fades into dusk. *Dàn xī* means “evening.” *Hūn míng* is the threshold between darkness and light. By day, only its shadow appears; by night, only its light—no form at all. *Mǎ shàn* should be written as *huō*. *Huōrán* describes a faint sound.

Seeking his inferior, he requested the third sword. Taking advantage of his drunkenness, he struck him down, suspecting him already dead, and swiftly departed. The blow fell as if into emptiness—his sword passed through as though through nothing.

*Chī* is akin to *chī* (foolish). The three strikes of Dan were taken by his opponent as mere feints, like child’s play. *Ài jí* refers to a constricted throat. *Zhī qiáng* describes limbs stiff and unyielding, lacking suppleness.

This illustrates that even hexing arts possess numinous efficacy—how much more so the study of the Dao? Such accounts, when included in these writings, carry deliberate intent; they are not mere boastful or exaggerated speech.

King Mu of Zhou launched a great campaign against the Western Rong, who presented him with the *Kūnwú* sword and fire-washed cloth. The sword measured a foot and a span, its blade forged of refined steel with a crimson edge, capable of slicing jade as though it were mud. The fire-washed cloth, when laundered, must be cast into fire; the cloth turns the color of flame, while the grime takes on the cloth’s hue. Removed from the fire and shaken, it gleams as dazzlingly as snow.

The prince deemed such objects nonexistent, dismissing their chroniclers as fabricators. Xiao Shu remarked: “The prince is indeed resolute in his convictions—yet resolute, too, in his distortion of reason!” *Liàn gāng* refers to tempered steel. *Chì rèn* denotes the essence of metal, its hue crimson. *Rú qiē ní* describes its softness. Though swords that cut jade remain unseen today, fire-washed cloth is still witnessed in the world—such marvels do exist.

To be “resolute in self-trust” is to rely solely on what one’s eyes and ears perceive, unaware of the numinous wonders beyond, and thus dismissing their chroniclers as false—this is a distortion of reason. The preceding exposition concludes here with these two lines, observing that those who doubt such vast and wondrous accounts do so from the limits of their own narrow perspective, lacking true understanding of principle.

*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Yanzhai Kouyi*, Scroll Five (End)

*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Yanzhai Kouyi*, Scroll Six
By Yanzhai Lin Xiayi

**Chapter Six: Strength and Fate**

Strength addressed Fate, saying: “What are your achievements compared to mine?”
Fate replied: “What have you done for things that you would compare yourself to me?”
Strength said: “Longevity or early death, success or failure, nobility or lowliness, wealth or poverty—all lie within my power.”
Fate said: “The wisdom of Peng Zu did not surpass that of Yao or Shun, yet he lived eight hundred years. The talent of Yan Yuan did not fall below that of the common crowd, yet he died at eighteen. The virtue of Confucius did not rank beneath that of the feudal lords, yet he was besieged in Chen and Cai. The conduct of King Zhou of Yin did not rise above that of the Three Benevolent Ones, yet he occupied the throne. Ji Zha held no rank in Wu, while Tian Heng monopolized the state of Qi. Boyi and Shuqi starved at Shouyang, while the Ji clan grew wealthier than Zhan Qin. If these are within your power, why do you grant long life to one and early death to another, obstruct the worthy and advance the wicked, debase the wise and exalt the foolish, impoverish the good and enrich the evil?”

Strength said: “If this is so, then I truly have no merit in things. Yet if things are as they are, is this not your doing?”
Fate said: “Since I am called Fate, how could there be one who controls me? I merely follow the straight and yield to the crooked. Each lives long or dies young, succeeds or fails, rises or falls, grows rich or poor—how could I comprehend it? How could I comprehend it?”

Strength is human effort; Fate is Heaven’s decree. The meaning here is that longevity or early death, success or failure, wealth or poverty—if these were determined by human effort and not by Heaven’s decree, then they would not be the work of creation. To follow the straight and yield to the crooked is to say that both straight and crooked arise naturally; I merely follow and yield to them. “How could I comprehend it?” means that even Fate cannot control them, for there is something yet more primal that governs them—what *Zhuangzi* calls “that upon which I depend, which itself depends on something else to be so.”

The general purport of this chapter is thus, and its language is direct and unadorned, which is why some doubt it is from the original *Liezi*.

---

Beigongzi said to Ximenzi: “You and I are of the same generation, yet people esteem you. We are of the same clan, yet people respect you. We share the same appearance, yet people love you. We speak alike, yet people heed you. We act alike, yet people trust you. We serve alike, yet you are honored. We farm alike, yet you grow rich. We trade alike, yet you profit. I wear coarse cloth, eat coarse grain, dwell in a thatched hut, and walk on foot. You wear brocade, feast on fine meats, live in a mansion with long eaves, and ride in a carriage drawn by four horses. At home, you cheerfully dismiss me; at court, you haughtily scorn me. You do not deign to visit me, nor do we travel together—this has been so for years. Do you consider your virtue superior to mine?”

Ximenzi said: “I have no way of knowing the truth. You undertake affairs and fail; I undertake them and succeed. Is this proof of our differing worth?”
Beigongzi, unable to respond, departed in dismay.

On the road, he encountered Master Dongguo, who said: “Where are you coming from, walking so hesitantly, with such a look of deep shame?”
Beigongzi recounted what had happened.

Master Dongguo said: “Shall I relieve you of your shame? Let us go together to question Ximen again.”
He said: “Why do you so deeply humiliate Beigongzi? Speak plainly.”

Ximenzi said: “Beigongzi claims that we share the same generation, clan, age, appearance, speech, and conduct, yet differ in status, wealth, and poverty. I told him: ‘I have no way of knowing the truth. You undertake affairs and fail; I undertake them and succeed. Is this not proof of our differing worth?’ And yet you say we are the same—your face is shameless indeed.”

Master Dongguo said: “Your notion of worth differs from mine. When you speak of worth, you refer only to differences in talent and virtue. What I call worth is this: Beigongzi is rich in virtue but poor in fate; you are rich in fate but poor in virtue. Your success is not due to wisdom, nor is his failure due to folly. Both are Heaven’s doing, not man’s. Yet you boast of your favorable fate, while Beigongzi feels shame for his virtue—neither of you understands the principle of what is inherently so.”

Ximenzi said: “Enough, Master. I dare not speak further.”

After Beigongzi returned home, he donned his coarse cloth, which was as warm as fox or badger fur; he ate his coarse beans, which tasted as fine as rice and millet; he sheltered in his thatched hut, which shaded like a grand hall; he rode in his humble cart, which was as splendid as an ornate carriage. For the rest of his life, he remained at ease, unaware of honor or disgrace in others or himself.

Master Dongguo, hearing of this, said: “Beigongzi has slept long indeed. A single word awakened him—how easily he was enlightened!”

The esteem, respect, and love people show to the successful are merely their partiality toward them. *Lian li* refers to the long eaves of a mansion. *Zaoshi* means the affairs one undertakes. Success or failure—failure is called “thick,” success “thin”; “thick” and “thin” denote ability or its lack. *Yu yu er bu* describes a hesitant gait. *He yin shi* is a phonetic gloss, meaning “to relieve your shame.” *Geng zhi* means to go again, to revisit Ximen together. Success is not due to wisdom, nor failure to folly—how can one boast of fate? *Guran* refers to the inherent principle of nature. *Rong shu* are large beans. To be “rich in virtue but poor in fate” is to have great ability yet no opportunity.

Rich in fate yet poor in virtue—this is to encounter opportunity without ability. Here, *de* (virtue) shares the same meaning as *neng* (ability), not the virtue of moral cultivation.

Guan Yiwu and Bao Shuya were close friends, living together in Qi. Guan Yiwu served Prince Jiu, while Bao Shuya served Prince Xiaobai. The Qi royal clan was favored and numerous, with legitimate and illegitimate heirs contending equally. The people feared chaos. Guan Zhong and Shao Hu escorted Prince Jiu in flight to Lu, while Bao Shuya escorted Prince Xiaobai to Ju. Later, when Gongsun Wuzhi rebelled and Qi had no ruler, the two princes raced to return. Guan Yiwu fought Xiaobai on the road to Ju, shooting him in the belt hook. Once Xiaobai ascended the throne, he forced Lu to execute Prince Jiu; Shao Hu died for him, while Guan Yiwu was imprisoned.

Bao Shuya said to Duke Huan: “Guan Yiwu is capable—he can govern the state.”
Duke Huan replied: “He is my enemy. I wish to kill him.”
Bao Shuya said: “I have heard that a worthy lord bears no private grudges. Moreover, one who can serve his master can surely serve a lord. If you wish to become hegemon, none but Yiwu will suffice. You must pardon him.”
Thereupon, Guan Zhong was summoned. Lu returned him to Qi, and Bao Shuya welcomed him beyond the city walls, releasing him from captivity. Duke Huan honored him, placing him above the Gao and Guo clans. Bao Shuya humbled himself before him, entrusting him with state affairs, and he was styled “Zhongfu.” Duke Huan thus became hegemon.

Guan Zhong once sighed: “When I was young and poor, I once traded with Bao Shuya, taking more than my share of the profits. Bao Shuya did not think me greedy—he knew I was impoverished. I once devised a plan for Bao Shuya that led to great hardship. He did not think me foolish—he knew the times were inauspicious. I served three times and was thrice dismissed by my lords. Bao Shuya did not think me worthless—he knew I had not met my moment. I fought three battles and fled thrice. He did not think me cowardly—he knew I had an aged mother. When Prince Jiu was defeated, Shao Hu died for him, while I endured imprisonment and humiliation. Bao Shuya did not think me shameless—he knew I did not scorn minor setbacks but grieved that my name would not resound through the world. My parents gave me life, but Bao Shuya truly knew me.”

This is why the world praises Guan and Bao for their fine friendship, and Xiaobai for his skill in employing talent. Yet in truth, there was no fine friendship, nor any skill in employing talent. To say there was no fine friendship and no skill in employing talent is not to claim that some other fine friendship or skill existed. Shao Hu did not choose to die—he had no choice but to die. Bao Shuya did not choose to recommend the worthy—he had no choice but to recommend. Xiaobai did not choose to employ his enemy—he had no choice but to employ him.

*Shen qi* means “very close.” The Guo and Gao clans were two noble families of Qi. Bao Shuya knew I was poor, knew the times were inauspicious, knew I had an aged mother—these words are most excellent. To “skillfully employ talent” is to skillfully manage human affairs. To have “no choice but to recommend” and “no choice but to employ” is the mandate of Heaven.

When Guan Yiwu fell ill, Xiaobai asked him: “Zhongfu’s illness is grave. You need not avoid speaking plainly. If it should become critical, to whom should I entrust the state?”
Yiwu said: “Whom do you desire?”
Xiaobai said: “Bao Shuya will do.”
Yiwu replied: “He will not. His character is that of a pure and upright scholar. Toward those unlike himself, he does not associate with them. Once he hears of a man’s fault, he never forgets it. If he were to govern the state, above he would offend the lord, below he would alienate the people. His offense against you would not be long in coming.”

Then Xiaobai asked, “If so, who is suitable?”
He replied, “If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do.
His character is such that above, he forgets his lord, yet below, none rebel against him. He feels shame for not equaling the Yellow Emperor and pities those who do not equal himself.
To share virtue with others is the mark of a sage; to share wealth with others is the mark of a worthy. To rule others with worthiness never wins their hearts; to humble oneself before the worthy never fails to win them.
In governing the state, there are things he does not hear; in managing his household, there are things he does not see.
If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do.”

“His illness is grave” means his illness has become severe. “Do not avoid speaking plainly” means one must not avoid what others say. This marks the end of the sentence.
“Those who do not equal himself” refers to people he despises, whom he does not regard as of the same kind.
“To offend the lord” means to entangle and constrain him.
“To alienate the people” means to control them with laws and governance.
“Above, he forgets” means he serves his lord without calculation.
“Below, none rebel” means he does not go against reason.
“Shame for not equaling the Yellow Emperor” shows his high regard for himself.
“Pity for those who do not equal himself” shows his great forbearance toward others.
“To share virtue with others” means he does not claim virtue as his own.
“To rule others with worthiness” means he deliberately seeks to subdue them.
“To humble oneself before the worthy” means he lowers himself to honor others.
“In governing the state, there are things he does not hear; in managing his household, there are things he does not see” means he does not rely on his own intelligence.

Guan Yiwu does not slight Bao Shuya out of preference, but he cannot but slight him; he does not favor Xi Peng out of partiality, but he cannot but favor him.
To favor someone at first may lead to slighting them later; to slight someone later may follow from favoring them at first. The ebb and flow of favor and disfavor do not depend on me.
Though Guan and Bao’s friendship was as described, Guan’s dying words seemed to slight Bao Shuya and favor Xi Peng. Though he claimed to choose a minister for the state, in truth, this too was a matter of fate, not something Yiwu could control.
The words of favor and disfavor are not meant as actual judgments but serve to illustrate the power of fate.

Deng Xi wielded an argument of two possibilities, crafting endless words. When Zichan was in power, Deng Xi composed the *Bamboo Penal Code*. The state of Zheng adopted it, and Deng Xi repeatedly challenged Zichan’s governance. Zichan yielded to him. Zichan then arrested and executed him, swiftly putting him to death.
Thus, Zichan was not truly able to use the *Bamboo Penal Code*—he could not but use it. Deng Xi was not truly able to humble Zichan—he could not but humble him. Zichan was not truly able to execute Deng Xi—he could not but execute him.
“Two possibilities” means to deceive and follow, making right and wrong interchangeable. “Endless words” are beyond questioning.
“Repeatedly challenged Zichan’s governance” means that during Zichan’s rule, Deng Xi often opposed him.
“Zichan yielded to him” means he was troubled by Deng Xi’s objections.
After Zichan adopted Deng Xi’s *Bamboo Penal Code*, yet found opposition troublesome, he blamed the code. Thus, he arrested and executed Deng Xi, and having executed him, he then put him to death.
The *Bamboo Penal Code* refers to a penal code written on bamboo slips.
“Could not but use it, could not but execute him”—the *Bamboo Penal Code* was Deng Xi’s creation. Zichan first used it, yet Deng Xi was later executed because of it. Favor and disfavor reversed, and fortune and misfortune arose—all by the natural course of fate, not by human effort. Even Zichan had no freedom in this.

To live when one can live is Heaven’s blessing; to die when one can die is Heaven’s blessing.
To live when one cannot live is Heaven’s punishment; to die when one cannot die is Heaven’s punishment.
To be able to live or die, and to live or die accordingly—this happens.
To be unable to live or die, yet to live or die nonetheless—this too happens.
Yet life and death, neither of things nor of oneself, are all a matter of fate, beyond the reach of wisdom.
Thus it is said: Vast and boundless, the Dao of Heaven meets of itself; empty and undivided, the Dao of Heaven moves of itself.
Heaven and Earth cannot transgress it; sages and wise men cannot interfere; ghosts and spirits cannot deceive.
The natural course silently completes it, levels it, guides it, and welcomes it.

“To be able to live or die” means each follows their desire, meeting death or life without regret—people call this Heaven’s blessing.
To cling to life yet not live, to suffer in hardship and disgrace, to seek death yet not die—people call this Heaven’s punishment.
Such things certainly occur in the world, yet they do not know that life and death, neither of things nor of oneself, are not free, beyond the reach of wisdom and effort—all is fate.
Even wisdom can do nothing about it.
“To live or die accordingly” is the same as “to be able to live or die,” merely reiterated for emphasis.
“To live or die nonetheless” also conveys the meaning of “unable to.”

“Vast and boundless” means dark and limitless, unfathomable—this is where the Dao of Heaven converges.
“Empty and undivided” is the wonder of the Dao of Heaven’s movement.
Who can know it? “Heaven and Earth cannot transgress it”—Heaven, though of firm virtue, does not interfere with the seasons; waxing and waning, Heaven itself does not violate them.
“Sages and wise men cannot interfere” means even sages and wise men cannot defy the times.
“Ghosts and spirits cannot deceive” means even ghosts cannot know it, nor use it to deceive others.
“Silently” means to comprehend it in silence.
“Completes it” means it naturally achieves itself, without effort.
“Levels it” means to listen with an impartial heart.
“Guides it” means to guide its departure.
“Welcomes it” means to welcome its arrival.
*Zhuangzi* says: “When the Master came, it was the time; when he went, it was the proper course. To be at ease with the time and dwell in the proper course, sorrow and joy cannot enter.” This is the same idea.

Yang Zhu’s friend was named Ji Liang. Ji Liang fell ill, and after seven days, his condition worsened.

His son circled around him, weeping, and sought out physicians. Ji Liang said to Yang Zhu: “My son is so unworthy—why do you not sing for him to enlighten him?” Yang Zhu sang:

“Heaven does not know—
how could man awaken?
Not blessed by Heaven,
nor cursed by man.
You or I—
who understands?
Physician or shaman—
do they truly know?”

His son did not comprehend and ultimately summoned three physicians. The first was named Jiao, the second Yu, and the third Lu. They examined his illness. Jiao said to Ji Liang: “Your cold and heat are unregulated, your deficiency and excess imbalanced. The illness arises from hunger and satiety, lust and desire, scattered essence and troubled thoughts—neither Heaven nor ghosts are to blame. Though grave, it can be treated.” Ji Liang said: “A common physician—quickly send him away.”

Yu said: “From the beginning, your prenatal qi was insufficient, while your postnatal nourishment was excessive. The illness did not arise in a single day; its causes have accumulated gradually. It cannot be cured.” Ji Liang said: “A good physician.”

He then gave him food to eat.

Lu said: “Your illness comes neither from Heaven, nor from man, nor from ghosts. At birth you received your form, and from the beginning there was one who governed it, one who knew it. What can medicine or stone needles do for you?”

Ji Liang said: “A divine physician.”

He then richly rewarded him.

Soon after, Ji Liang’s illness resolved of itself.

No blessing descends from Heaven, no affliction arises from man—all is natural.

*Jing lü fan san*: thoughts and cares grow excessive, and the spirit scatters and is lost.
*Ru du you yu*: drinking too much milk.
Jiao’s words attributed the illness to human affairs, so Ji Liang dismissed him as a common physician.
Yu’s words spoke of an illness received at birth, tracing its origin to Heaven rather than human action, so Ji Liang deemed him a good physician and gave him food, for his words approached the truth.
Lu’s words spoke of a governing force unknown and a knowing force equally unknown—even Heaven does not comprehend it—so Ji Liang called him a divine physician and lavished gifts upon him, for his insight was lofty and subtle.
Soon after, the illness resolved of itself—this too was simply natural, and medicine played no part.

Life is not preserved by esteem, nor the body enriched by love;
Life is not cut short by lowliness, nor the body diminished by neglect.
Thus, esteem may not bring life, lowliness may not bring death;
Love may not enrich, neglect may not diminish.
This seems contrary, yet it is not contrary—
Life and death, enrichment and impoverishment, proceed of themselves.
Some live despite lowliness, some die despite esteem;
Some are enriched by love, some diminished by neglect.
This seems natural, yet it is not natural—
Life and death, enrichment and impoverishment, proceed of themselves.
Esteem and lowliness, enrichment and impoverishment, have no bearing on longevity or early death.
These words seem to defy the norm, yet they do not—
They appear to contradict reason, yet they do not.
To attribute longevity or early death to esteem or lowliness, enrichment or impoverishment—
These words seem to follow reason, yet they do not.
Know that longevity and early death arise from the natural, from what cannot be otherwise, and the doubt of contrary or natural vanishes.

Xiong Yu said to King Wen: “What is long is not made longer, what is short is not made shorter. What is lost in calculation—what is it? Long and short proceed of themselves, as in Zhuangzi’s discourse on the wild duck and crane. What lies beyond calculation—what can be done? If it does not fall within calculation, what can man do? Since man cannot control it, how can he do otherwise than let it be natural?”

Laozi said to Guan Yin: “Who knows the reason for Heaven’s dislikes? To divine Heaven’s intent, to weigh profit and harm—better to cease.”

This chapter echoes Zhuangzi’s words: “Heaven’s gentleman is man’s petty man; man’s gentleman is Heaven’s petty man.”
Yan Yuan and Robber Zhi—who was good? Who was evil?
To judge Heaven’s intent by human affairs, to seek its likes and dislikes, its profit and harm—where can one find them? Better to cease. To let things be is superior.

Yang Bu asked: “Consider two men: they are brothers in age, brothers in talent, brothers in appearance, yet in longevity they are father and son, in rank father and son, in reputation father and son, in love and hatred father and son. I am perplexed.”

Yangzi said: “The ancients had a saying—I have long remembered it, and shall tell it to you.”

What occurs without knowing why it occurs—this is fate. Now, in this murky confusion, in this ceaseless flux, whether one acts or does not act, as days pass and return, who can fathom its cause? All is fate.

He who trusts in fate transcends life and death;
he who trusts in principle transcends right and wrong;
he who trusts in the heart transcends compliance and defiance;
he who trusts in nature transcends safety and peril.
Thus, one may speak of complete trust—or of complete distrust.
Truly sincere, truly steadfast—what to reject? What to embrace?
What to grieve? What to delight in?
What to do? What not to do?

"Brothers" speaks of their age and appearance, their words and talents being alike. "Father and son" speaks of the vast distance between their rank, longevity, honor, and repute.

The ancients had a saying—which I have long remembered—what was it? "What occurs without knowing why it occurs—this is fate." This is it.

"In ceaseless flux"—this means multiplicity. "Restlessly moving"—this means unceasing motion. The *Book of Han* speaks of "pendant cords swaying restlessly."

To wish to act yet be unable, to wish not to act yet be compelled—fate’s constraint: who knows its cause? To know fate is to transcend life and death; to know the principle of spontaneity is to transcend right and wrong; to know the heart’s truth is to transcend compliance and defiance; to know the nature of Heaven’s mandate is to transcend safety and peril.

Fate, principle, heart, nature—though they seem trustworthy, they are not wholly so. Thus it is said: "Complete trust, complete distrust." Truly sincere, truly steadfast—unified and unwavering. If one could be thus, what to reject? What to embrace?

What is there to grieve over? What is there to rejoice in? What is there to be done? What is there to be left undone? In all these, let the heart remain unburdened.

The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* states: "The perfected person dwells as though dead, moves as though mechanical." They neither know why they dwell nor why they do not dwell; neither know why they move nor why they do not move. They do not alter their demeanor because others observe them, nor do they maintain it because others do not. Alone they come, alone they go—who can obstruct them?

To "dwell as though dead" echoes *Zhuangzi*’s "corpse-like stillness," where the body is like withered wood and the heart like dead ashes. To "move as though mechanical" is like a shadow following its form, dependent on something else to be so—like the wooden puppet of the artisan Yan Shi, whose movements are governed by hidden mechanisms. Since it does not originate from the self, one neither knows why one dwells or does not dwell, moves or does not move. Whether others see my demeanor or not, how has it ever changed? Beyond the reach of ears and eyes, all is forgotten, so that in coming and going, entering and exiting, one attains the marvel alone—who can restrain or hinder this? This is to forget the self, abandon the form, and wander with the Creator.

Mo (眉), Xi (杘), Chi (敕), Yi (夷), and Nü (女) wander together in the world, each following their own will. Though they spend their years together, they never truly know one another, so deep is their cunning.

"Each following their own will" means these four types move through the world according to their own inclinations. Though they spend their lives together, they remain unknown to one another—such is the depth of their cleverness. The five passages below invent such names to depict human emotions and worldly attitudes, akin to *Zhuangzi*’s "flaunting and revealing one’s nature."

Mo (墨) sounds like *mei* (眉), *shǐ nǚ lǚ* (屎女履). Xi (杘) means weakness. Chi (敕) means *dan zhi* (单至), an appearance of unease. Yi (夷) means *tan xuan* (啴咺), a look of fear. Nü (女) means *bie fu* (憋懯), a manner of haste.

The crafty and obsequious, the foolish and upright, the graceful (婩) and the coarse (鱼), the blunt (斫) and the fawning (夫约)—these four types wander together in the world, each following their own will. Though they spend their years together, they never reveal their methods to one another, so subtle is their artifice.

"They never reveal their methods" means they do not share their techniques, believing in the subtlety of their own skill. *Xue* (硸) means *zhuo* (斫), an appearance of dullness.

The cunning (犭廖), the fierce (犽), the emotionally exposed (情露), the inarticulate (言蹇), the overbearing (凌谇)—these four types wander together in the world, each following their own will. Though they spend their years together, they never come to understand one another, so assured are they in their own talents.

Here are four more types who flaunt their abilities. *Liao ya* (犭廖犽) means slyness. *Qing lu* (情露) refers to what people now call "showing off." *Yan jian dan ji* (言蹇诞极) means stammering and urgency. *Ling sui* (凌谇) means interrogation. *Zhuangzi* says: "The wise take no delight in idle debates." "Never come to understand" means they never come to comprehend one another.

The timid (眠), the hesitant (娗), the shirking (諈), the evasive (诿), the brave, and the indecisive—these four types wander together in the world, each following their own will. Though they spend their years together, they never expose one another’s faults, so blameless do they believe their conduct to be.

Here are four more types with differing behaviors. *Mian tian* (眠娗) means shrinking and unsteady. *Zhui wei* (諈诿) means nagging and shifting blame. "Never expose one another’s faults" means they never criticize or correct one another.

The accommodating (多偶), the self-reliant (自专), the powerful (乘权), and the solitary (只立)—these four types wander together in the world, each following their own will. Though they spend their years together, they never glance at one another, so perfectly do they believe the times suit them.

*Duo ou* (多偶) means agreeable, easily aligning with others. *Zi zhuan* (自专) means self-reliant, unable to align with others. *Cheng quan* (乘权) means those who gain power through circumstance. *Zhi li* (只立) means standing alone without fear. "Never glance at one another" means each believes they have seized the right moment.

These are the many attitudes—though their appearances differ, all are subsumed by the Dao, all return to fate. "The many attitudes" refer to the five types of people above. "Dao" means spontaneity. "Subsumed by the Dao" means all proceed from the natural. Though their emotions, appearances, and demeanors vary, none act freely—all return to fate. This suggests that the myriad human emotions and worldly attitudes, though diverse, are all shaped by fate.

All *guigui* seem to achieve completion—these are but semblances of success, not true success. All *guigui* seem to fail—these are but semblances of failure, not true failure. Thus delusion arises from semblance, for in the moment of semblance, all is dim. Yet if one remains undimmed amid semblance, one will not be startled by external misfortune nor delighted by inner fortune; moving with the time, halting with the time—even wisdom cannot fathom it.

Those who trust in fate harbor no duality between self and other. Those who divide self and other would do better to cover their eyes and plug their ears, for even with their backs to the precipice and faces to the moat, they will not stumble. Thus it is said: life and death come from fate, poverty and hardship from the times. Those who resent early death do not understand fate; those who resent poverty do not understand the times. Facing death without fear, enduring hardship without sorrow—this is knowing fate and accepting the times.

Let the clever calculate gains and losses, weigh the real and the empty, gauge human nature—they may succeed, they may fail, yet both outcomes align. Let the foolish not calculate gains and losses, not weigh the real and the empty, not gauge human nature—they too may succeed, they too may fail, and both outcomes align. What difference lies between calculation and non-calculation, between weighing and not weighing, between gauging and not gauging? Only when there is nothing to calculate, nothing not to calculate, is wholeness preserved and loss averted. Yet this is neither knowing wholeness nor knowing loss—wholeness is self-wholeness, loss is self-loss, ruin is self-ruin.

*Guigui* describes the appearance of nearness. *Qiao* means semblance. Success seems success yet is not success; failure seems failure yet is not failure. People are deluded by these semblances, bewildered by success and failure—hence it is said: delusion arises from semblance. Yet in the moment of semblance, though all seems dim and unknowable, the principle is in truth quite clear. It was never truly dim. If one remains undimmed amid semblance, then misfortune is not startling, fortune not delightful. External misfortune is what people shun; inner fortune is what they desire. Yet fortune and misfortune have no inherent inner or outer—people divide them by love and hate, thus giving rise to fear and joy. Moving with the time, halting with the time, advancing and retreating in accord—even wisdom cannot fathom it, for there is no room for intention.

Standing with one’s back to a steep slope, facing a deep moat—this is perilous indeed. To cover one’s eyes and plug one’s ears is peril upon peril. Yet if one knows fate lies in Heaven and harbors no intention, there is no peril. Such words differ from Mencius’ saying that those who know fate do not stand beneath crumbling walls—for the words of sages and worthies differ from those of heterodox teachings.

The clever may calculate, yet success and failure remain evenly matched. The foolish may not calculate, yet success and failure remain evenly matched. "Succeeding aligns, failing aligns"—this means both outcomes are half. Even the most cunning cannot exhaust all calculations, and even the most foolish may sometimes succeed. But if one neither calculates nor refrains from calculation, then gain and loss vanish entirely—what is of Heaven is preserved, and nothing is lost. Yet wholeness cannot be known, loss cannot be known, and the absence of wholeness or loss cannot be known either. Thus it is said: neither knowing wholeness nor knowing loss. The preceding line speaks of wholeness without loss, yet concludes with self-wholeness, self-loss, self-ruin—this is stirring prose. Its meaning is: wholeness is self-wholeness, loss is self-loss, and the absence of wholeness or loss is itself the absence of wholeness or loss.

Duke Jing of Qi was traveling on Ox Mountain when he looked north over his capital and wept, saying: "How beautiful is this land! So lush and verdant—why must I one day leave it and die?" If the ancients had never died, where would I go from here? Historians Kong and Liang Qiu Ju both wept with him, saying: "We rely on Your Lordship’s grace. Though we eat coarse grain and tough meat, though we ride broken-down horses and rickety carts, we still do not wish to die—how much less our lord?" Only Yanzi laughed beside them. The duke wiped his tears and turned to Yanzi: "Today’s outing has made me sorrowful. Kong and Ju weep with me, yet you alone laugh—why?" Yanzi replied: "If worthy men had always guarded this land, then the Great Duke and Duke Huan would still guard it. If brave men had always guarded it, then Duke Zhuang and Duke Ling would still guard it. With such rulers guarding it, where would Your Lordship be? You would be wearing a straw raincoat and hat, standing in the fields, worrying only about your livelihood, with no time to ponder death. How then would you have attained this position? It is because rulers succeed one another, departing one after another. That you alone weep for this is unkind. Seeing an unkind ruler and flattering ministers—these two things are why I could not help but laugh." Duke Jing was ashamed. He raised his cup to punish himself and made the two ministers drink two cups each as punishment.

*Didi* describes the appearance of decline. *Coarse grain* refers to the food of the lowly. *Rickety carts* are small, roughly made vehicles. *Wiping tears* means drying them. *Worrying only about livelihood* means being anxious about survival. *With no time* is a scribal error for "how could there be time." This passage speaks of those who, in their folly, do not understand life and death, coming and going, but cling only to present pleasures.

There was a man of Wei named Dongmen Wu whose son died, yet he was not grieved. His steward said: "No one in the world loved their son more than you. Now that your son has died, why are you not grieved?" Dongmen Wu replied: "I once had no son, and when I had no son, I was not grieved. Now that my son has died, it is the same as when I had no son—why should I grieve?"

The *steward* was the manager of his household. This passage presents the doctrine of "what is originally possessed is fundamentally real; what is originally lost is fundamentally nonexistent."

*Minister* and *how* are interchangeable terms. The farmer pursues the seasons, the merchant seeks profit, the artisan refines his craft, and the official chases power—such is the compulsion of circumstance. Yet the farmer faces drought and flood, the merchant meets gain and loss, the artisan encounters success and failure, and the official finds favor or neglect—such is the decree of fate.

*To pursue* means to refine, as in the pursuit of carving and polishing. Though the farmer follows the seasons, Heaven sends drought and flood; though the merchant chases profit, circumstances bring gain and loss; though the artisan excels in skill, times yield success and failure; though the official curries favor with the powerful, he may meet with fortune or misfortune—all is fate.

The earlier statement that "such is the compulsion of circumstance" means that once one becomes a farmer, merchant, artisan, or official, the situation leaves no alternative—worldly conditions compel it, allowing no self-determination.

*The Authentic Classic of Perfect Virtue, Master Yan’s Oral Exegesis, Scroll Six* ends here.

*聪*: Originally written as *听* ("hearing"), emended according to the Ming edition.
*疏*: Originally written as *跪* ("kneeling"), emended according to the Ming edition.

*The Authentic Classic of Perfect Virtue, Master Yan’s Oral Exegesis, Scroll Seven*
By Lin Xiyi of Yan Studio

**Yang Zhu, Chapter Seven**

Yang Zhu traveled to Lu and lodged at the Meng residence. Meng asked him: "Man is but man—why strive for fame?"

Yang Zhu replied: "Fame is sought for wealth."

"If one is already wealthy, why not cease?"

"For nobility."

"If one is already noble, why not cease?"

"For death."

"If one is already dead, what then?"

"For one’s descendants."

"What benefit does fame bring to descendants?"

Yang Zhu said: "Fame wears the body and wearies the heart. Yet those who ride its wave bestow blessings upon their clans, extend benefits to their villages, and how much more to their descendants? Man is but man—since all are equally human, mere life suffices; why pursue fame? Fame wears the body and wearies the heart, for those who chase it labor in vain. Yet through such toil, they secure fame and thus leave blessings for their clans, benefits for their villages, and all the more for their descendants. This is how fame brings advantage."

"Yet all who seek fame must be incorruptible, and incorruptibility leads to poverty; they must be yielding, and yielding leads to lowliness." (Here, a transition is implied.) "All who seek fame must be both incorruptible and yielding. If one is incorruptible and yielding, one cannot be wealthy or noble—how then can one benefit descendants?"

Yang Zhu said: "When Guan Zhong served as minister of Qi, he matched his lord’s excesses and extravagance. Their wills aligned, their words concurred, and the Way was enacted, making the state hegemonic. Yet after his death, only the Guan clan remained. When the Tian clan served as ministers of Qi, if the lord was full, they diminished themselves; if the lord levied, they bestowed. The people all turned to them, and thus they seized the state of Qi. Their descendants have enjoyed it to this day without end."

"True fame leads to poverty; false fame leads to wealth." (Another transition, now distinguishing true and false fame.) "Guan Zhong followed his lord in excess and extravagance, seeking no self-praise. Loyal in his counsel, he swiftly achieved hegemony—this was true fame, yet its benefits extended no further than himself. The Tian clan’s actions all countered their lord’s: when he was arrogant, they were modest; when he was oppressive, they were benevolent. Their modesty and benevolence were self-serving, seeking reputation—this was false fame, yet they ultimately gained the state of Qi. Thus, the false grow wealthy while the true remain poor."

Yang Zhu said: "Truth has no fame; fame has no truth. Fame is but pretense."

"In ancient times, Yao and Shun feigned offering the world to Xu You and Shan Juan yet did not lose it, reigning for a hundred years. Boyi and Shuqi sincerely ceded the rulership of Guzhu yet ultimately lost their state and starved to death on Mount Shouyang. The distinction between truth and pretense is thus made clear." (Another transition: all fame is pretense.) "Those of true virtue do not seek fame; those who seek fame lack true conduct. All who pursue fame do so falsely. Having deemed fame pretense, the text invokes Yao, Shun, Boyi, and Shuqi to illustrate its point—this is why the book is considered heterodox. *Sheng* means ‘clear’: the distinction between truth and pretense is thus made evident."

This passage first asserts that fame can benefit oneself, yet ultimately concludes with the word *pretense*. "Truth has no fame; fame has no truth"—these six characters are well put. But to say, "Fame is but pretense," is a doctrine that corrects the world.

Yang Zhu said: "A hundred years is the greatest span of life. Of a thousand, not one attains it. Even if one does, half is spent in infancy, old age, and senility. Half of what remains is lost to sleep at night and distraction by day. Another half is consumed by pain, illness, sorrow, loss, anxiety, and fear. In the few decades that remain, moments of unburdened joy, free from even the slightest concern, are but fleeting. What, then, is the purpose of human life? What joy does it bring? It is but fine food and rich garments, music and beauty. Yet fine food and rich garments cannot always sate, nor can music and beauty always delight the senses. Then one is further constrained by rewards and punishments, advanced or withdrawn by fame and law. Restlessly, one competes for fleeting reputation, schemes for posthumous glory, cautiously guards what one sees and hears, and spares no thought for the body or mind. Thus, one forfeits the supreme joy of the present, unable to indulge even for a moment. How is this different from a prisoner in shackles?"

"The people of high antiquity knew that life is but a brief sojourn and death a fleeting departure. Thus, they followed their hearts and acted without defying their natural desires. They did not forsake present pleasures for the sake of fame. They roamed in accord with their nature, not resisting the inclinations of all things. They did not seek posthumous fame, nor did they invite punishment. They did not weigh reputation before or after, nor did they measure the length of life. *Qi* means ‘measure.’ *Mǐ* means ‘dissipated,’ as in ‘dissolved.’ *Yí* means ‘lost.’ *Jiè* refers to the slightest thing—joy is rare in life, and even in moments of joy, can one be entirely free of minor concerns? This is to say, joy is never complete. Even if one could fully enjoy a hundred years, not a single moment of perfect bliss would remain. *Měi hòu* means fine food and rich garments. *Huánghuáng* means restless. *Yǔyǔ* means aimless. Restlessly competing for empty fame, aimlessly avoiding right and wrong—how is this different from a prisoner? *Yì* and *yì* are the same: to follow the heart is to act, merely without violating natural principle. For present pleasures, one pursues what one loves without abandoning it for the sake of fame. One roams in accord with one’s nature, not resisting the desires of all things. Posthumous fame is indeed what people desire, yet one does not court punishment to attain it—this means one does not die for fame."

Yet such writings reveal too much of their sinews and bones, seeming unfit for the purpose of imparting instruction—*Zhuangzi* is not thus.

Yang Zhu said: "All things differ in life, yet are the same in death. In life, there are the worthy and the foolish, the noble and the base—this is how they differ. In death, there is putrefaction and dissolution—this is how they are the same. Though so, worthiness and foolishness, nobility and baseness are not within one’s power; putrefaction and dissolution are likewise not within one’s power. Thus, life is not what one lives, death is not what one dies; worthiness is not what one deems worthy, foolishness is not what one deems foolish; nobility is not what one deems noble, baseness is not what one deems base. Yet all things are equal in life, equal in death, equal in worthiness, equal in foolishness, equal in nobility, equal in baseness. Ten years or a hundred years—both end in death. The benevolent sage and the cruel fool—both end in death. Alive, one may be Yao or Shun; dead, one is but rotting bones. Alive, one may be Yu or Zhou; dead, one is but rotting bones. All is one—who knows the difference? Moreover, one should attend to the present life—why hasten to consider what comes after death? Though life differs, death is the same, just as Du Zimei said: the sage Confucius and the bandit Zhi alike become dust. *Qu* means ‘to turn toward.’ Having understood the present, why trouble oneself with what follows death? Thus it is said: *Attend to the present life—why hasten to consider what comes after death?* Zhang Han said: *Let us first drain this cup of life.* Letian said: *Do not think of the endless matters beyond the self—just empty the limited cups before you.* Both express this same idea."

Yang Zhu said: "Boyi did not lack desires—his excessive pride in purity led him to starve to death. Zhan Ji was not without passions—his excessive pride in chastity led him to leave no descendants. The error of purity and chastity lies in this. The character *xie* is likely a scribal corruption of *you*. *You* and *you* are the same, meaning ‘excessive’—the ancient characters were interchangeable. They were not without emotions or desires, for their likes and dislikes were the same as others’. Yet by clinging too rigidly to purity and chastity, Boyi thus brought about his own starvation, and Zhan Ji thus brought about the extinction of his lineage. *Gua zong* means the end of his clan. Such is how one errs by oneself. Yet the reputation for purity and chastity can mislead even the virtuous to such an extent. Thus it is said: *The error of purity and chastity lies in this.*"

Yang Zhu said: "Yuan Xian was impoverished in Lu, while Zigong amassed wealth in Wei. Yuan Xian’s poverty harmed his life, and Zigong’s wealth burdened his person. If poverty is not permissible, nor is wealth—where then lies what is permissible? It lies in delighting in life and freeing the self. Thus, one who delights in life does not suffer poverty, and one who frees the self does not amass wealth. *Zhi* means to burden oneself with the pursuit of wealth. Poverty brings no joy, and riches bring self-exhaustion—neither is the Way of nurturing life."

Yang Zhu said: "An ancient saying goes: *In life, we cherish one another; in death, we cast one another aside.* This saying is perfect. The Way of cherishing is not merely about affection—it is to labor so that others may be at ease, to hunger so that others may be full, to be cold so that others may be warm, to be poor so that others may prosper. The Way of casting aside is not without sorrow—it is to refrain from placing pearls and jade in the mouth, from dressing the dead in brocade, from offering sacrifices, from setting out funerary objects. *In death, we cast one another aside*—the ancients abandoned the dead, as the *Yi* says: *No mounding, no planting, no fixed mourning period.* The lines about not placing pearls and jade, etc., satirize those who practiced lavish burials in his time. The arguments of Yang Wangsun and Huangfu Mi for unclothed burial seem to derive from this."

Yan Pingzhong asked Guan Yifu about nurturing life. Guan Yifu said: "Let it be free—do not obstruct or restrain it." Yan Pingzhong said: "What does this entail?" Yifu said: "Indulge the ears in what they wish to hear, the eyes in what they wish to see, the nose in what it wishes to smell, the mouth in what it wishes to speak, the body in what it wishes to find comfortable, and the mind in what it wishes to do. If the ears wish to hear music but are not allowed to listen, this is called *obstructing hearing*; if the eyes wish to see beauty but are not allowed to look, this is called *obstructing sight*; if the nose wishes to smell pepper and orchid but is not allowed to inhale, this is called *obstructing scent*; if the mouth wishes to speak of right and wrong but is not allowed to speak, this is called *obstructing wisdom*; if the body wishes for comfort but is not allowed to follow it, this is called *obstructing ease*; if the mind wishes to act freely but is not allowed to do so, this is called *obstructing nature*. All these obstructions are the tyrants of self-torment. Cast off these tyrants of self-torment, and live joyfully awaiting death—whether a day, a month, a year, or ten years—this is what I call nurturing. Clinging to these tyrants of self-torment, bound without release, living in sorrow to a great age—whether a hundred years, a thousand years, or ten thousand years—this is not what I call nurturing. *E* means to restrain and control oneself, thereby tyrannizing one’s own heart—nothing but self-inflicted suffering. *A day, a month, a year, ten years*—this means to indulge body and mind so that one day is like another’s month, one year like another’s decade. Otherwise, even with a lifespan of a hundred, a thousand, or ten thousand years, what benefit is there? *Not what I call nurturing*—this means it is not the Way of nurturing life."

Guan Yifu said: "I have already told you about nurturing life—what of sending off the dead?" Yan Pingzhong said: "Sending off the dead is a trivial matter—what more is there to say?" Guan Yifu said: "I would still like to hear it." Pingzhong said: "Once dead, what is it to me? Burn the body, or sink it, or bury it, or expose it; wrap it in firewood and cast it into a ravine, or dress it in embroidered robes and place it in a stone coffin—whatever one encounters will do."
