Guan Yifu turned to Bao Shu and Huangzi and said: "The Way of life and death—we two have advanced it."

The phrase "trivial matter" means it requires no deliberate arrangement—letting things take their course suffices. The dead should swiftly decay; those who prepare stone coffins do so merely to defy worldly convention.

Bao Shu and Huangzi are the names of two men—though Huangzi may well be a fictional figure. When Zichan served as minister of Zheng and held sole authority over the state’s governance, after three years, the virtuous submitted to his transformative influence while the wicked feared his prohibitions. Zheng thus achieved order, and the feudal lords stood in awe of him. Yet he had an elder brother named Gongsun Chao and a younger brother named Gongsun Mu. Chao was fond of wine, and Mu was fond of women.

In Chao’s residence, wine was amassed by the thousand *zhong*, yeast piled into mounds. From a hundred paces outside the gate, the scent of sweet ale assailed the nostrils. When he was lost in drink, he knew nothing of the world’s stability or peril, the regrets or resentments of human affairs, the prosperity or ruin of his household, the closeness or estrangement of his nine clans, or the sorrow and joy of life and death. Even if fire, flood, or blades raged before him, he remained oblivious.

Mu’s rear courtyard contained dozens of adjoining chambers, each filled with women of dazzling youth and delicate beauty, their voices soft and alluring.

When he was lost in sensual indulgence, he cast aside close kin, severed friendships, and fled to his rear courtyard, sating himself day and night. He would emerge but once every three months, yet his desires remained unfulfilled. Whenever he heard of a maiden of surpassing beauty in the village, he would bribe and summon her, employ matchmakers to entice her, and relent only when he had secured her.

Zichan grieved over this day and night. Secretly, he sought Deng Xi’s counsel, saying: “I have heard that governing the self leads to governing the household, and governing the household leads to governing the state—this speaks of proceeding from the near to the far. I have brought order to the state, yet my own household is in chaos. Does this mean my Way is perverse? What remedy can save my two brothers? I beg you, instruct them.”

Deng Xi replied: “I have long marveled at this but dared not speak first. Why not admonish them in due time, illustrating the weight of life and destiny, and alluring them with the dignity of ritual and righteousness?”

Zichan followed Deng Xi’s advice. Seizing an opportunity, he met with his brothers and said: “What elevates humans above beasts is wisdom. What wisdom guides is ritual and righteousness. When ritual and righteousness are perfected, renown and rank follow. But if one acts on impulse, indulging in desires, life and destiny are imperiled. Heed my words, and by evening you will regret your ways and enjoy emolument by dawn.”

Chao and Mu replied: “We have long known this and long deliberated upon it. Do we need your words to recognize it? Life is hard to attain yet death comes easily. With a life so difficult to obtain, why wait for death, which comes so readily? Why should we revere ritual and righteousness merely to impress others, or feign restraint to seek fame? We deem this worse than death. We wish only to exhaust life’s joys, to drain the pleasures of our years. Our sole concern is that our bellies may swell before we have drunk our fill, or our strength may fail before we have indulged our passions. We have no leisure to fret over tarnished reputations or the peril to our lives. Moreover, do you flaunt your governance of the state to sway us? Do you seek to confuse our hearts with fine words, to delight us with honors and emoluments? How contemptible and pitiable! We wish to part ways with you.

Those skilled in governing externals may not bring order to the world, yet they exhaust themselves. Those skilled in governing the self may not plunge the world into chaos, yet their nature remains at ease. Your governance of externals may be temporarily enforced in one state, but it does not accord with human hearts. Our governance of the self can be extended to all under heaven, and the Way of ruler and minister will cease. We have long wished to explain this art to you, yet you turn and instruct us with yours instead.”

Zichan was left speechless. Later, he recounted this to Deng Xi, who said: “You have dwelt with true men yet failed to recognize them. Who would call you wise? The order of Zheng is but a coincidence, not your achievement. Piles of yeast form mounds, and heaps of dregs become towers of wine. *Woduo* and *ejiao* both mean ‘beautiful women.’ ‘Relent only when secured’ means exhausting every scheme until success is achieved. ‘Deep reflection’ means profound contemplation. ‘Bellies may swell before drinking their fill, strength may fail before indulging their passions’—this is Guo Pu’s argument that the resources for wine and women may not be exhausted. Deng Xi called them ‘true men’ because they grasped the principles of nurturing life. ‘Those skilled in governing the self may not plunge the world into chaos’ means that those who delight in their own hearts do not necessarily bring disorder to the world—order and chaos are both natural destinies. This passage resembles the *Zhuangzi*’s ‘Robber Zhi’ chapter, with its sprawling branches and leaves. Guo Pu’s words seem profoundly irrational, yet considering his habit of girding a sword, loosening his hair, and ascending the privy, he likely knew his fate. Foreseeing that he could not protect himself, he espoused such theories. Yet fortune and misfortune lie with heaven, while cultivation rests with us. One should fulfill human effort and leave the rest to destiny. Girding a sword and loosening one’s hair is not the conduct of one who understands principle—how much less indulgence in wine and women? Reflecting on this, *Mencius* said: ‘Whether one lives long or dies young is not a matter of doubt; cultivate the self and await it.’ What richness of flavor, what abundance of principle, what inexhaustible enjoyment! Confucius said: ‘If one hears the Dao in the morning, one may die in the evening.’ His meaning lies here. The books of Zhuangzi and Liezi were originally written to lament the world, yet deluded people manipulate their arguments, and later generations have further adulterated them. Those who read these works without grasping their intent, or who fail to discern their authenticity, may be led astray—this is why they are called heterodox teachings.

Wei’s Duanmu Shu was a descendant of Zigong. Relying on his family’s wealth, his household amassed ten thousand gold. He paid no heed to worldly affairs, giving free rein to his desires.”

He indulged in all that the people desired to do, all that human inclination longed to enjoy—nothing was left undone, nothing left unplayed. His walls and halls, terraces and trees, gardens and parks, ponds and pools, food and drink, carriages and robes, music and concubines rivaled those of the lords of Qi and Chu. Whatever his heart craved, whatever his ears wished to hear, his eyes wished to see, his mouth wished to taste—even if it came from distant lands or remote states, from places where Qi’s soil did not nurture it—he would inevitably procure it, as if it were but a thing within his garden walls. Wherever he traveled, though mountains and rivers blocked the way, though the roads were long and the paths distant, he would inevitably go, as if it were but a step away. Guests filled his courtyard daily by the hundreds; his kitchens never ceased their smoke and flame; his halls and corridors never stilled their music.

After providing for his own needs, he first distributed the surplus to his clan; after the clan, he gave to his village; after the village, he scattered it throughout the state. At sixty, when his vitality began to wane, he abandoned his household affairs and dispersed all his storehouses—his treasures, jewels, carriages, robes, concubines, and attendants. Within a year, he exhausted it all, leaving no wealth for his descendants. When he fell ill, he had no medicines stored; when he died, he had no funds for burial. All in the state who had received his gifts then pooled their resources to bury him and restore his wealth to his descendants.

Qin Guli heard of this and said: “Duanmu Shu was a madman—he shamed his ancestors.” Duan Yuansheng heard of it and said: “Duanmu Shu was an enlightened man—his virtue surpassed his ancestors.” What he did, what he pursued, shocked the common mind, yet it was affirmed by the truth of principle.

The gentlemen of Wei, who held fast to ritual propriety, were certainly not those who could grasp this man’s heart. “Descendant of Zigong” refers to his later generations. “Pooled their resources to bury him” means they collected funds to inter him. “Shocked the common mind” means that ordinary people found his actions strange. “Affirmed by the truth of principle” means that, viewed through the lens of natural principle, his conduct was worthy of emulation. How could those who rigidly cling to ritual propriety ever understand this? His intent, it seems, was to mock our Confucians.

"Qi-drying" is akin to the strength of one’s qi-bones.

Meng Sunyang asked Yangzi: “Suppose there were a man here who values life and cherishes his body, seeking thereby not to die—would that be permissible?”
Yangzi replied: “In principle, there is no not-dying.”
“Then, seeking long life—would that be permissible?”
Yangzi replied: “In principle, there is no long life. Life is not preserved by valuing it, nor is the body made more substantial by cherishing it. Moreover, what is the purpose of long life? The five passions of love and hate remain the same in ancient times as now; the safety and peril of the four limbs remain the same in ancient times as now; the bitterness and joy of worldly affairs remain the same in ancient times as now; the alternations of order and chaos remain the same in ancient times as now. Having heard of these things, having seen them, having experienced them—even a hundred years would seem too many, let alone the suffering of an even longer life. Love and hate, safety and peril, bitterness and joy—these are all that human affairs amount to. The life of the world moves from order to chaos in an unending cycle, hence it is said: the alternations of order and chaos remain the same in ancient times as now. A thousand years, ten thousand years—it is all the same. *Geng* means to have passed through. My life, whether ten years or a hundred, has seen and heard and passed through nothing more than this; even a thousand years or ten thousand would be no different.”

Du Mu said: “The work of this floating world is eating and sleeping.” This, too, is the same idea.

Meng Sunyang said: “If that is so, then swift death is better than long life.”

Then to tread upon blades, plunge into boiling water and fire—this would fulfill one’s resolve.

Master Yang said: “Not so. Once born, then set aside all deliberation and yield to nature, exhausting all desires while awaiting death. When death comes, then set aside all deliberation and yield to nature, exhausting all destinations until release in dissolution. There is nothing not set aside, nothing not yielded to—why then hasten or delay between them? This turn of thought is well made.

The life of man is by nature without sufficient joy, yet one cannot abandon life to seek death. *To set aside* means to be without intent; to set aside one’s thoughts and follow nature—thus it is said: set aside and yield to it. If one can fully grasp this notion, then even the distinction between setting aside and yielding vanishes—how then could one find leisure to calculate the swiftness or slowness between them?”

Yang Zhu said: “Bo Cheng Zigao would not sacrifice even a hair’s breadth to benefit others, relinquishing his state to retire and plow in seclusion. Great Yu would not seek personal gain even at the cost of his own body, enduring partial withering. The ancients would not surrender a single hair to benefit the world, nor would they accept the whole world to serve a single self. If each person refrains from sacrificing even a hair, if each person refrains from benefiting the world, the world will be well-ordered.”

Qinzi asked Yang Zhu: “If plucking a single hair from your body could save the age, would you do it?”

Yang Zhu said: “The age cannot truly be saved by a single hair.”

Qinzi said: “Suppose it could—would you do it?”

Yang Zhu did not answer.

Qinzi went out and told Meng Sunyang, who said: “You do not comprehend the Master’s heart. Allow me to explain. If someone could gain ten thousand gold by cutting into your flesh, would you do it?”

He replied: “I would.”

Meng Sunyang said: “If severing one of your limbs could win you a state, would you do it?”

Qinzi fell silent for a moment.

Meng Sunyang said: “A single hair is finer than flesh, flesh finer than a limb—this is evident. Yet flesh is but the accumulation of hairs, and a limb the accumulation of flesh. A single hair is indeed one ten-thousandth of the body—how can it be lightly discarded?”

Qinzi said: “I have no answer for you. Yet if you were to ask Lao Dan or Guan Yin, your words would be correct; if I were to ask Great Yu or Mo Di, my words would be correct.”

Meng Sunyang then turned and spoke of other matters with his disciples.

*Partial withering of the body* refers to Yu’s hands and feet calloused from labor. To use even a single hair of mine to benefit the world—I would not give it. To offer all things under heaven to me—I would not accept them.

This is what is called the teaching of "for oneself." The world is not a place that can be saved by a single hair—what benefit would sacrificing even one hair of mine bring to the world? And what use does the world have for a single hair? The hypothetical question of salvation asks: if a single hair could save the world, would you do it? Yangzi did not respond, for he did not express this idea in full. The body is the accumulation of limbs, and limbs the accumulation of hairs. To move even a single hair is to disturb something within one’s own body—how can it be lightly discarded for its smallness? This implies that even a slight inclination toward external affairs is not the Way of self-nurturance.

Qinzi said: “Given your argument, I find it difficult to answer. Yet Lao Dan and Guan Yin would agree with you, while Great Yu and Mo Di would not.” Meng Sunyang then turned to his disciples and spoke of other matters, implying: “Great Yu and Mo Di are not models my teacher follows—how can you compare them thus?”

Meng Sunyang was a disciple of Yang Zhu. Yang Zhu said: “The world’s praise is heaped upon Shun, Yu, the Zhou kings, and Confucius, while its blame falls upon Jie and Zhou. Yet Shun tilled the fields at Heyang and made pottery at Leize, his limbs never at rest, his mouth never tasting fine food, his parents displeased, his siblings estranged. At thirty, he married without informing his family. When he received Yao’s abdication, he was already aged and his wisdom in decline. When Shangjun proved unworthy, he passed the throne to Yu, living in constant sorrow until his death—such was the bitter fate of this sage. Gun failed in his governance of flood and earth, and was executed at Feather Mountain. Yu inherited his task, avenging his father’s failure, laboring over barren lands, his son left unraised, passing his own door without entering. His body withered, his hands and feet calloused, and when he received Shun’s abdication, he lived in humble quarters, adorned in fine robes and regalia, yet remained anxious until his death—such was the toil of this sage. After King Wu’s death, King Cheng was young and weak, and the Duke of Zhou took up the reins of governance. The Duke of Shao was displeased, and rumors spread among the four states. After three years in the east, he executed his brother and exiled another, barely preserving his own life, living in constant fear until his death—such was the peril of this sage. Confucius, who illuminated the Way of emperors and kings, responded to the summons of rulers of his time. He was driven to fell a tree in Song, erase his traces in Wei, impoverished in Shang and Zhou, besieged in Chen and Cai, humiliated by the Ji family, insulted by Yang Hu, living in perpetual distress until his death—such was the harried life of this sage. These four sages lived without a day of joy, yet died with names that endured for ten thousand generations. But a name is not something one can truly possess. Though praised, they knew it not; though rewarded, they knew it not—no different from a clod of earth. Jie, inheriting generations of wealth and the throne of the Son of Heaven, possessed intelligence to overawe his subjects and power to shake the realm. He indulged his senses and exhausted every desire, living in carefree abandon until his death—such was the dissolute life of this tyrant. Zhou, too, inherited generations of wealth and the throne of the Son of Heaven. His will was unopposed, his commands obeyed without fail. He gave free rein to his passions in the Leaning Palace, indulged his desires through long nights..."

They did not torment themselves with ritual and righteousness, but lived in carefree abandon until their execution—such were the wanton among heaven’s people. Those two villains enjoyed the pleasures of desire in life, yet died bearing the names of folly and brutality. Reality is not something names can bestow; though reviled, they knew it not; though praised, they knew it not—how did they differ from a clod of earth? Those four sages, though the objects of all praise, suffered to the end, yet all met the same death; those two villains, though the objects of all condemnation, reveled to the end, yet all met the same death.

The term *tianren* refers to the people under heaven. Among them, the most destitute and solitary, the most anxious and afflicted, the most fearful and harried—these are the most pressed and unable to find leisure. *Huangju* means pressed and unable to find respite. *Tianmin* is synonymous with *tianren*. *Zhukuai* refers to rotting wood and clods of earth. Once the body perishes, neither praise nor blame is known—what difference, then, between worthy and unworthy?

This passage is too stark in its argument. When Yang Zhu met King Hui of Liang, he claimed governing the world was as easy as turning one’s palm. The king replied: “You cannot even manage one wife and one concubine, nor tend a garden of three *mu*—how can you claim governing the world is as easy as turning your palm?” Yang Zhu answered: “Have you seen a shepherd? A hundred sheep in a flock, led by a five-foot boy carrying a switch—where he wishes them to go east, they go east; where he wishes them to go west, they go west. But if Yao were to lead a single sheep, with Shun carrying the switch behind him, they could not move forward. Moreover, I have heard that fish large enough to swallow a boat do not swim in shallow streams, and swans soar high without alighting in filthy ponds. Why? Their ambitions are too vast.”

The Yellow Bell and Great Lǚ cannot accompany frantic dances—why? Their tones are too sparse. He who would govern the great does not govern the petty; he who would achieve great deeds does not fuss over trifles. This is the meaning of it. Yao and Shun herding sheep could not match a five-foot boy—these words are most excellent, for they show that those capable of greatness are incapable of smallness. "Shallow streams" refers to minor tributaries. The *Zhuangzi*’s "Autumn Floods" chapter expresses a similar idea.

Yang Zhu said: The affairs of the distant past are extinguished—who records them? The deeds of the Three Sovereigns exist as if they exist not, as if waking or dreaming; the affairs of the Five Emperors seem half-real, half-illusory; the doings of the Three Kings appear now hidden, now revealed—of a hundred, not one is known. The events of one’s own lifetime may be heard or seen, yet of ten thousand, not one is truly grasped. The matters before one’s eyes may endure or vanish—of a thousand, not one is certain. From the distant past to the present, the years are too many to count. Since Fuxi’s time, over three hundred thousand years have passed—wisdom and folly, beauty and ugliness, success and failure, right and wrong—all have dissolved, differing only in how swiftly they fade. To fret over fleeting praise or blame, tormenting one’s spirit and body for the sake of a name lingering a few hundred years after death—can this moisten dry bones? Where is the joy in life?

"Extinguished" means obliterated and untransmitted. "As if they exist not," "as if waking or dreaming," "now hidden, now revealed"—the general sense is that the more distant the past, the more unknowable it becomes. Even momentary fame and reputation will vanish without a trace after a few centuries. Those who do good merely torment themselves in vain.

Yang Zhu said: Man resembles the class of Heaven and Earth, bearing the nature of the Five Constants. Of all living things, he is the most spiritually endowed. Yet man’s claws and teeth are insufficient for defense, his flesh too frail to shield himself, his speed inadequate to evade harm or pursue advantage. Lacking fur or feathers to withstand cold and heat, he must rely on external things to nourish his nature, depending on wisdom rather than strength. Thus, what wisdom values is preserving oneself; what strength degrades is encroaching upon others. Yet the body does not truly belong to oneself—once born, one cannot but preserve it; external things do not truly belong to oneself—once possessed, one cannot discard them. The body is the master of life, just as things are the masters of sustenance. Though one preserves life, one cannot claim ownership of the body; though one does not discard things, one cannot claim ownership of them. To claim ownership of the body or things is to wrongfully appropriate the world’s body and the world’s things. Only the sage does not do so. To share the world’s body and the world’s things—only the perfected person achieves this. This is called the utmost of the utmost.

"Nourishing one’s nature" means nourishing life. Relying on wisdom rather than strength—wisdom preserves the self, while strength contends with things. Preserving the self is noble; encroaching upon things is base. Encroaching upon things means clashing and struggling with them. One’s body and life cannot but be preserved. Things outside the self are not one’s own—if not one’s own, they become burdens, and one cannot but distance oneself from them. Yet the body is the very means by which one lives, just as things are the means by which one sustains life. Though the body is dear, it is not always free—how can one claim to possess it? Though things may be discarded, some cannot be abandoned—how can one harbor the intent to discard them? This is what *Zhuangzi* meant by "things cannot be relied upon, yet cannot be done without." To regard things or the body as one’s own is to defy the principles of Heaven and act selfishly—hence, "wrongful appropriation." The sages of the world are like this; these words subtly mock all from Yao and Shun onward. Only by entrusting one’s body to no-body and external things to no-thing, free from selfish desires, does one become a perfected person. "The utmost of the utmost" means reaching the ultimate limit, beyond which nothing can be added.

Yang Zhu said: The people cannot rest because of four things—longevity, fame, rank, and wealth. Possessing these four, one fears ghosts, fears others, fears authority, and fears punishment—such a person is called a fugitive. Life and death are controlled by external forces. If one does not defy fate, why crave longevity? If one does not pride oneself on nobility, why crave fame? If one does not seek power, why crave rank? If one does not covet riches, why crave wealth? Such a person is called a compliant one. In all the world, none can oppose them; their fate is controlled from within.

Thus, the saying goes: Without marriage or office, half of human desires vanish; without clothing or food, the way of ruler and subject ceases. A Zhou proverb says: "A farmer can be killed by sitting." Rising at dawn and returning at dusk, he takes his nature as constant; sipping beans and chewing greens, he considers his fare the finest. His flesh is coarse and thick, his sinews and joints sturdy and supple. But if one morning he is placed upon soft furs and silken curtains, served fine grains, meat, orchids, and citrus, his heart will grow restless, his body agitated, and internal heat will breed illness. If the lords of Shang and Lu were to share the farmer’s lot, they would not last a day before exhaustion. Only when one has desires does one have fears. Authority brings hidden and manifest fortunes and misfortunes; punishment refers to the execution and mutilation of royal law. A fugitive is one who flees from Heaven and turns against principle. Such a person’s life and death are controlled by others—hence, "fate is controlled externally." The compliant one, free from pride, envy, or worldly desires, stands alone above all—hence, "in all the world, none can oppose them." Their fate lies within, not controlled by others—hence, "fate is controlled internally." Marriage and office give rise to human desires; the way of ruler and subject is maintained through clothing and food. Without marriage and office, desires could be halved.

If freed from the burden of clothing and food, then ruler and subject could no longer employ one another. This must be an observation from ancient times. That a farmer may be killed by sitting idle means that if one from the fields, coarse and lowly, were made to sit at leisure without need of blade or spear, they would surely fall ill—bones aching, body wasting—for they are accustomed to toil, and sudden ease would bring sickness. Were the lords of Shang and Lu to exchange places with field laborers, they could not endure even a moment. As Zimei said: *"Without noble or base, there is no sorrow; without wealth or poverty, there is sufficiency."* The meaning of this passage seems akin to this. It speaks of how human life is but a matter of habit—if all toiled bitterly and ate plainly, unaware of the world’s honors and pleasures, then none would feel want. The moment thoughts cease, all places become peaceful. These words carry weight.

Thus, what rustics find contentment in, what they deem beautiful, they believe nothing in the world surpasses. Formerly, in the state of Song, there was a field laborer who wore only tattered hemp to endure the winter. When spring came and he began his eastern work, he basked in the sun, unaware of the world’s grand halls, warm chambers, silk floss, or fox-fur robes. Turning to his wife, he said: *"The warmth of the sun upon my back is a joy unknown to others. If I were to offer it to our lord, I would surely receive great reward."* A wealthy man of the village told him: *"Once, a man praised the excellence of wild beans, sweet hemp stalks, water celery, and duckweed seeds to a local magnate. The magnate took them and tasted them—bitter on the tongue, agonizing in the belly. The crowd laughed and reviled him, and the man was deeply ashamed. You are of the same sort."* The rustic’s contentment, his sense of beauty, comes from believing nothing in the world surpasses what he knows—he is at peace with what his eyes have seen and unaware of anything beyond.

*"Tattered hemp"* refers to rags of broken flax. To seek reward for the joy of basking in the sun is to illustrate his narrow vision, seeing only what is small and not what is great. *"Wild beans"* are large beans; *"sweet hemp"* is fine hempseed; *"water celery"* is a soup made from silk celery; *"duckweed seeds"* are another kind of vegetable. *"Stung"* means poisoned—they were acrid in the mouth, speaking of their fierce bitterness.

Yang Zhu said: *"Grand houses, fine clothes, rich flavors, and beautiful women—with these four, what more could one seek? Yet those who possess them and still seek beyond are insatiable by nature. Such insatiability gnaws at the balance of yin and yang."* Once these four are attained, a person’s life should be self-sufficient, yet those who still pursue fame and achievement are insatiable. *"Gnaws at yin and yang"* means that insatiability harms the body’s vital qi.

Loyalty is insufficient to secure a ruler’s peace and only endangers oneself; righteousness is insufficient to benefit others and only harms one’s life. Peace for superiors does not arise from loyalty, and so the name of loyalty perishes; benefiting others does not arise from righteousness, and so the name of righteousness vanishes. The ancient Way was for ruler and subject to be at peace together, for self and others to benefit alike. This passage also satirizes those who establish names through loyalty and righteousness, saying that loyalty must endanger the self and righteousness must harm one’s life—this is called attending to externals rather than internals. The reality of securing peace for superiors arises naturally; how could the loyalty of one person alone bring it about? The way of benefiting others also arises naturally; how could the righteousness of one person alone achieve it? To seek the name of loyalty and righteousness through personal ambition is to see the name vanish and only burden the self. Better to follow nature, so that ruler and subject are both at peace and self and others both benefit—this is the so-called ancient Way.

The *Yuzhi* says: *"Abandon names, and there is no sorrow."* Laozi said: *"Names are but guests of reality."* Yet the careless world pursues names without end. If names truly cannot be abandoned, can they truly be treated as mere guests? Now, to have a name is to be honored and exalted; to lack a name is to be lowly and disgraced. Honor brings ease and joy; disgrace brings sorrow and suffering. Sorrow and suffering violate one’s nature; ease and joy accord with it. These are the realities to which names are tied. How can names be abandoned? How can they be treated as mere guests? The danger lies in clinging to names and thereby burdening reality. Clinging to names and burdening reality will lead to irreparable peril—how can one then distinguish between joy and sorrow?

*"Abandon names, and there is no sorrow."* *"Names are but guests of reality."* Though these words come from the *Yuzhi* and Laozi, the world knows them well. Yet the careless world pursues names without cease—how can the words of these two masters serve as a warning? *"Guest"* means external; thus, names cannot be abandoned, nor can they be treated as external. People of this world take having a name as honor and delight, and lacking a name as disgrace and sorrow. They see sorrow as violating their nature and delight as according with it, and so they pursue names without end. *"These are the realities to which names are tied"* means that the violation or accord of one’s nature is tied to tangible gains and losses—one cannot help but seek them. Thus, though the two masters wished to abandon names, how could they be abandoned? Though they wished to treat names as external, how could they be made external?

Having fully expounded this, the text concludes by observing the ways of the world: though names cannot be abandoned or set aside, clinging to them too fiercely will lead to self-harm. In matters of nurturing life, this brings the peril of irreparable ruin—how then can one distinguish between suffering and joy? *"Pity"* means sorrow. This meaning is that those in the world who ceaselessly seek fame will ultimately destroy themselves—this is the consequence of loving joy and fearing sorrow, leading to self-destruction.

The seventh scroll of *The True Classic of Perfect Virtue and Empty Tranquility, Oral Teachings from the Yan Studio*, is now complete.

**Volume Eight of *The True Classic of Perfect Virtue and Empty Tranquility, Oral Teachings from the Yan Studio* by Lin Xiyi of the Yan Studio**

**Explaining the Talisman (Shuofu) Eighth**

*The Zhuangzi* speaks of *"Virtue’s Full Manifestation"* (Dechongfu), while this is called *"Explaining the Talisman"* (Shuofu). Though the character *fu* (符) is the same, its meaning differs.

*Fu* means "to accord." It signifies that the most profound words naturally align with the harmony of Heaven and humanity—hence the title *Shuofu*.

*The Liezi* comprises eight scrolls, with only the first and last bearing this title; the six in between are named by their opening two characters. It is likely the original text was not structured otherwise.

Master Liezi studied under Master Huqiu Zilin.
Master Huqiu Zilin said: "If you understand *holding to what comes after*, then we may speak of *holding the self*."
Liezi replied: "I wish to hear of *holding to what comes after*."
The master said: "Observe your shadow, and you will understand."

Liezi turned to examine his shadow: when the body was bent, the shadow appeared crooked; when the body was straight, the shadow was upright. Thus, crookedness and straightness follow the body and do not reside in the shadow. Flexion and extension depend on the object, not on the self—this is what it means to *hold to what comes after* and yet *dwell before all things*.

Guan Yin said to Master Liezi: "Beautiful words produce a beautiful echo; foul words produce a foul echo. A tall body casts a long shadow; a short body casts a short shadow. *Name* is the echo; *self* is the shadow. Thus it is said: Be cautious in your speech, for harmony will follow; be cautious in your conduct, for consequences will follow. Therefore, the sage observes outcomes to know others, examines the past to foresee the future—this is the principle by which they attain foreknowledge. Measurement lies in the self; verification lies in others. If others love me, I must love them in return; if others despise me, I must despise them in return. Tang and Wu loved the world, and so they ruled; Jie and Zhou despised the world, and so they perished—this is what is verified. When both measurement and verification are clear yet the Way is not followed, it is like leaving without using the door or walking without taking the path. How can one seek profit without difficulty?

I have observed the virtue of Shennong and the Flame Emperor, examined the records of Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou, and weighed the words of refined scholars and worthies. In all matters of preservation and ruin, rise and fall, there has never been a case that did not proceed from this Way.

*Holding to what comes after* means not preceding things. One who can *hold to what comes after* can thereby *hold the self*—for only by humbly placing oneself below others can one preserve the self. As for the shadow, it is your shadow. The shadow bends or straightens with the body; I flex or extend with things. The shadow does not precede the body; I do not precede things. One who can grasp this principle will always dwell before all things—this is also the meaning of *not contending for excellence yet surpassing others*.

Speech is sound. The echo responds to sound just as the shadow mirrors the body. Without seeking fame, fame naturally arrives; without prizing the self, the self naturally precedes. By following the pattern of echo and shadow rather than form and sound, one attains the Way. The sage’s Way is precisely this: through *non-speech*, others naturally harmonize; through *non-action*, others naturally follow—this is the inevitable principle. As going out must lead to returning, and departing must lead to coming, others may not know, but the sage knows—this is the sage’s foreknowledge, as if saying, *I have already grasped what all hearts must affirm*.

*Measurement* is the ruler. To measure an object is to *verify*. *Measurement lies in the self* means using the self as the standard to verify others. What others love in me, I must also love; what others despise in me, I must also despise—this speaks of the shared human sentiments of love and aversion. Tang and Wu were thus loved by the world and so ruled it; Jie and Zhou did not follow this Way and were despised by the world, and so lost their states. These are verifiable, historical events—one may examine Tang, Wu, Jie, and Zhou to see this. When what can be measured and verified is so clear, yet people do not follow the Way, it is like leaving without using the door or walking without taking the path. To seek profit without harm is difficult indeed.

The examples of Shennong, the Flame Emperor, Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou are all proven cases. Since ancient times, the words of refined scholars and worthies have all been thus. To seek the causes of rise and fall, preservation and ruin, yet not follow this Way—such a thing has never existed.

This passage is pure in its phrasing and upright in its argument, yet it differs markedly from the rest of this book. Could it be that the earlier sections were *deceptive speech* while this is *direct discourse*? Or is the text a patchwork, not the work of a single author?

Yan Hui said: "Those who inquire about the Way do so for the sake of wealth. Now that I have obtained a pearl, I am wealthy—why would I need the Way?"
Master Liezi replied: "Jie and Zhou prized profit and slighted the Way, and so they perished. How fortunate I did not speak to you earlier! A person without righteousness, who seeks only to eat, is no different from a chicken or a dog. Those who fiercely compete for food, locking horns, with the stronger dominating the weaker—these are the ways of beasts. To act like chickens, dogs, or beasts, yet desire others’ respect, is impossible. If others do not respect you, then danger and disgrace will befall you."

*Competing for food* means fighting over it. *Locking horns* refers to clashing like animals with antlers. The stronger overpower the weaker—this is the way of beasts. If a person knows nothing but seeks food, they act like beasts and will surely bring danger and disgrace upon themselves.

This passage also seems not to belong to this book, though its principles are quite sound.

Liezi had mastered archery and reported this to Master Guan Yin.
Master Guan Yin asked: "Do you know *why* you hit the mark?"
Liezi replied: "I do not."
Master Guan Yin said: "Then it is not yet time."
He withdrew and practiced further. After three years, he returned to report to Master Guan Yin.

Master Guan Yin said: "Do you now know *why* you hit the mark?"
Liezi replied: "I know."
Master Guan Yin said: "Then you may hold to this and not lose it. This is not only true of archery—governing a state and cultivating the self are likewise.

Thus the sage does not examine whether things exist or perish, but examines *why* they are so. At first, when asked about hitting the mark, you said you did not know—you had not yet grasped the Dao of hitting the mark. Now, when asked again, you say you know—you have grasped the Dao of hitting the mark. I tell you to hold to this and not lose it, so that you may preserve this Dao without forgetting it.

Yet if you *know* that you hit the mark, this is no longer the ‘knowing without knowing’; if you hold to it without losing it, this is no longer the discourse on the transforming Dao.

Existence and perishing are what can be seen. *Why* things are so is the underlying principle. Judging by such arguments, these passages do not belong to the teachings of Zhuangzi or Liezi, but rather approach those of our Confucians. This is why I suspect they may not be part of the original book.

Liezi said: When one’s appearance is flourishing, one becomes arrogant; when one’s strength is flourishing, one becomes reckless—such people cannot be spoken to of the Dao. Thus, even those whose hair is not yet streaked with white, if they speak of the Dao, will err—how much more so if they try to practice it?

Therefore, if one acts recklessly, no one will instruct them. If no one instructs them, they will be isolated and without support. The worthy delegate to others, and so even in old age they do not decline; even when their wisdom is exhausted, they do not fall into chaos.

Thus the difficulty in governing a state lies in knowing the worthy, not in considering oneself worthy. When one’s appearance is flourishing, arrogance shows in their face; when one’s strength is flourishing, they rely on brute force to prevail. Those whose hair is not yet streaked with white have little experience in the world and have not aged in worldly matters. With so little experience, how can they know the right and wrong, the gains and losses of the Dao? If even speaking of it is not yet possible, how much less so practicing it?

To act recklessly is to act on one’s own. If one has the mindset of self-reliance, who would be willing to teach them the good Dao? If no one instructs me, then I stand alone without support. To remain undiminished in old age means that, though my strength may be exhausted, I delegate to others to take my place; though my wisdom may be spent, I rely on others to plan for me—thus affairs are managed without disorder.

What is valued is not one’s own worthiness, but the ability to recognize worthiness. As the *Gongyang Commentary* says: ‘To be able to honor the worthy is to make the worthy also worthy.’ This aligns with the same idea. These arguments are quite sound, though it remains uncertain whether they truly originate from the *Liezi*."

An artisan of Song crafted for his lord a mulberry leaf from jade, taking three years to complete it. Its edges and veins, its finest striations and lustrous sheen, were so lifelike that when mixed among real mulberry leaves, none could distinguish it. Thereupon, this man lived by his skill throughout the state of Song.

Master Liezi heard of this and said: "Were heaven and earth to take three years to produce a single leaf, few things would bear leaves at all." Thus the sage relies on the transformative power of the Dao and not on clever skill.

*Feng* refers to the sharp edges of the leaf; *sha*, to the trimmed and tapered parts; *haomang*, to the veins and textures on the leaf; *fan*, to the abundance of such textures; and *ze*, to its lustrous color. *Dao-transformation* is non-action; *clever skill* is human effort. This analogy is most apt.

Master Liezi was impoverished, his countenance bearing the pallor of hunger. A visitor spoke of this to Ziyang of Zheng, saying: "Lie Yukou is surely a man of the Dao. That he dwells in your lord’s state yet suffers want—does this not suggest the lord fails to esteem scholars?" Ziyang of Zheng immediately ordered an official to send him grain. Master Liezi went out to meet the messenger, bowed twice, and declined. The messenger departed.

When Master Liezi returned home, his wife gazed at him and, striking her breast, said: "I have heard that the wives of those who possess the Dao all live in ease and joy. Yet you bear the look of hunger. The lord, recognizing his fault, sent you grain, and you refused it—is this not fate?" Master Liezi laughed and replied: "The lord does not truly know me. He sent me grain on another’s word, and should he condemn me, it would likewise be on another’s word. This is why I did not accept." In the end, the people indeed rose in revolt and killed Ziyang. If one knows me by another’s words, one will surely condemn me by another’s words—this shows he fundamentally does not understand me. To trust only the words of others, how can one be secure? As Wei Yang said: 'If a lord cannot employ me on your word, he will not kill me on your word either.' This is of the same kind. These seem like words from the Warring States period, yet they make for a fine tale.

The phrase "the lord, recognizing his fault, sent you grain" means the lord, considering it a failing not to esteem scholars, sent grain as a gift.

The Lu family of the Shi clan had two sons: one devoted to learning, the other to martial arts. The one fond of learning offered his arts to the Marquis of Qi, who accepted him and made him tutor to the noble scions. The one fond of martial arts went to Chu and presented his methods to the King of Chu, who was pleased and appointed him military commander. Their salaries enriched the household, and their ranks brought honor to their kin.

The Shi clan’s neighbor, the Meng family, also had two sons with the same pursuits, yet they were reduced to poverty. Envying the Shi clan’s fortune, they earnestly sought the means to advance. The two sons truthfully told the Meng family. One of the Meng sons went to Qin and offered his arts to the King of Qin. The King of Qin said: "Now the feudal lords contend with force, and all they attend to is arms and provisions. If I were to govern my state with benevolence and righteousness, it would be the path to ruin." He then castrated and banished him. The other son went to Wei and presented his methods to the Marquis of Wei. The Marquis of Wei said: "I am a weak state, hemmed in among great powers. I serve the great states and soothe the small—this is the way to seek security. If I were to rely on military power, destruction would soon follow. If I let you return intact to another state, the harm you would bring me would be no small matter." He then had his feet cut off and sent him back to Lu.

Upon their return, the father and sons of the Meng family beat their breasts and reproached the Shi clan. The Shi clan said: "Those who meet the moment prosper; those who miss it perish. Your path was the same as ours, yet your success differed—you missed the moment, not that your conduct was flawed. Moreover, in the world, principles have no constant right, and affairs no constant wrong. What was once employed may now be discarded; what is now discarded may later be used. This use or disuse has no fixed right or wrong. Seizing opportunities and adapting to the times, responding to affairs without fixed method—this belongs to wisdom. If wisdom is insufficient, even if one’s learning were as vast as Confucius’s or one’s strategies as those of Lü Shang, where could one go and not meet with failure?" The father and sons of the Meng family relaxed their expressions, free of resentment, and said: "We understand. Say no more."

Though their learning was the same, their circumstances differed. The moment brings success or failure—this is fate.

"Formerly" refers to the past. "Seizing opportunities and adapting to the times" means observing the gaps in the times and riding the moment to respond, without fixed place—this is the work of clever skill. Thus it is said: "Responding to affairs without fixed method belongs to wisdom." The meaning is that though you knew that devotion to learning or martial arts could be used to present your case, you could not adapt to the times and thus suffered mutilation and punishment—this shows a lack of clever skill. This again slightly contradicts the earlier idea of relying on Dao-transformation and not on clever skill. "Say no more" means there is no need to bring it up again.

Duke Wen of Jin went out to a meeting, intending to attack Wei. Gongzi Chu looked up to the heavens and laughed. The Duke asked why he laughed. He replied: "I laugh at a neighbor who was seeing his wife off to her parents' home. On the road, he saw a woman gathering mulberry leaves and, pleased, spoke with her. Yet when he turned to look at his wife, there was someone beckoning to her as well. I secretly laughed at this."

The Duke understood his meaning and halted the campaign. He led his army back, and before they arrived, someone had already raided the northern border of Jin.

This passage resembles the *Records of the Grand Historian: Biographies of Jesters*. Its meaning is that what one dislikes should not be imposed on others. If I can inflict something upon others, then others can inflict it upon me as well.

The state of Jin suffered from banditry. There was a man named Xi Yong who was skilled at discerning thieves by their appearance, examining their brows and lashes to uncover their true nature. The Marquis of Jin sent him to identify thieves, and not one in a thousand or a hundred escaped him. The Marquis was delighted and told Zhao Wenzi: "I have found one man, and the bandits of the entire state will be eradicated. Why would I need more?"

Wenzi replied: "If our lord relies on spies and surveillance to catch thieves, the thieves will never be fully eliminated. Moreover, Xi Yong will certainly not meet a good end."

Soon after, the bandits plotted among themselves: "The one who has cornered us is Xi Yong." They then joined forces to ambush and kill him.

When the Marquis of Jin heard this, he was greatly alarmed. He immediately summoned Wenzi and told him: "Just as you said, Xi Yong is dead. But what method should we use to capture thieves?"

Wenzi replied: "There is a Zhou proverb: 'He who sees the fish in the depths brings misfortune; he who discerns hidden things invites calamity.' If you wish to eliminate banditry, there is no better way than to appoint worthy men and entrust them with governance, so that instruction is clear above and transformation flows below. When the people have a sense of shame, what need is there for bandits?"

Thereupon, the state employed Sui Hui to oversee governance, and the bandits fled to Qin.

This passage illustrates how exposing hidden wrongs and uncovering secrets instead provokes contention among the people. Confucius said: "In hearing litigation, I am no different from others. What is essential is to make litigation unnecessary." He also said: "If you yourself do not desire it, though you reward them, they will not steal." This is the same idea.

Confucius, returning from Wei to Lu, rested his carriage by a bridge over the river and observed the waters. There was a cataract thirty *ren* high, its currents swirling for ninety *li*, where fish and turtles could not swim, nor could alligators and giant salamanders dwell. Yet a man was about to ford it.

Confucius sent someone along the bank to stop him, saying: "This cataract is thirty *ren* high, its currents swirl for ninety *li*, where fish and turtles cannot swim, nor alligators and giant salamanders dwell. Surely it is too perilous to cross?"

The man paid no heed, waded in, and emerged safely. Confucius asked him: "Is this skill? Or do you have some Daoist technique? How were you able to enter and exit?"

The man replied: "When I first entered, I relied on loyalty and trust. When I exited, I again followed loyalty and trust. Loyalty and trust placed my body amidst the waves, and I dared not act on private motives. That is how I was able to enter and exit."

Confucius said to his disciples: "Take note, all of you. If even water can be crossed by relying on loyalty and trust to align oneself with it, how much more so with people?"

*Li* (厉) means to ford water. As the *Book of Songs* says: "Where deep, wade through; where shallow, lift your robes." "Surely it is too perilous to cross" means it seemed impossible to ford. "Paid no heed" means he did not regard the peril of the waves. *Loyalty and trust* means sincerity. Placing oneself in the currents with loyalty and trust, without a trace of private motive, is how one can enter and exit the water.

The meaning of *loyalty and trust* here should not be sought in the conventional sense of the words in my writings. It broadly refers to sincerity alone.

The first half of this passage is identical to the Lü Liang episode in the *Yellow Emperor* chapter. Such repetition should not occur in the *Liezi*, which further confirms its杂乱. Moreover, the phrasing "first with loyalty and trust, then following with loyalty and trust" (先以忠信，又从以忠信) differs entirely from the style of the *Zhuangzi* and *Liezi*. If one remains in the realm of *with* (以), one has not yet undergone transformation. If one still clings to it without transformation, how could one traverse such a境界?

Duke Bai asked Confucius: "Can one speak in subtle terms with others?"

Confucius did not respond.

Duke Bai asked again: "If you throw a stone into water, what then?"

Confucius replied: "A skilled diver from Wu could retrieve it."

Duke Bai said: "If you pour water into water, what then?"

Confucius replied: "When the waters of the Zi and Meng rivers merge, Yi Ya could taste and distinguish them."

Duke Bai said: "Is it truly impossible to speak in subtle terms with others?"

Confucius replied: "Why would it be impossible? Only one who knows the meaning of words can do so. One who knows the meaning of words does not rely on words to convey meaning. Those who fight over fish get wet; those who chase beasts run—yet they do not do so for pleasure. Thus, the highest speech transcends words, and the highest action is non-action. What shallow minds contend over is trivial."

Duke Bai, unable to proceed further, later died in his bathhouse.

*Subtle speech* refers to hidden language. Duke Bai wished to rebel but dared not speak openly to seek Confucius' approval. Confucius understood his intent and thus did not answer.

"Throwing a stone into water—divers can retrieve it" means it is easily obtained. "Pouring water into water seems difficult, yet Yi Ya could distinguish them." The implication is that there is no principle by which words can be hidden; nothing spoken remains concealed from others.

Duke Bai, failing to grasp this, asked again whether subtle speech was truly impossible. "Why would it be impossible?" means: Is there truly anything in subtle speech that cannot be understood? Those who know the principle of words understand them. The principle of words lies not in the words themselves but beyond them, hence: "does not rely on words to convey meaning."

Those who fight over fish must enter the water—how could they not get wet? Those who chase beasts must enter the mountains—how could they not run and exhaust themselves? Pursuing things at the cost of harming oneself is no cause for joy.

Here the implication subtly rebukes his ill-conceived schemes.

Ultimate speech is the Dao; words cannot exhaust the Dao, and to depart from words is to attain the Dao. Ultimate action is the Dao; deliberate action cannot exhaust the Dao, and only non-action fully embodies the Dao. To employ shallow and clumsy wisdom in contending with the world is to fail to grasp the root. The overarching meaning is that the impulse to compete must not arise. Though Duke Bai understood these words, he could not restrain himself, and so ultimately rebelled and perished. "Could not restrain himself" means he was unable to cease. This entire chapter is identical to the "Dao and Its Applications" section of the *Huainanzi*. If the *Liezi* had already appeared by the time of Emperor Jing, the *Huainanzi* would not have copied it wholesale—thus we may be certain this is not the original text of the *Liezi*.

Viscount Xiang of Zhao sent Xin Zhi Muzi to attack the Di; he prevailed and captured the towns of Zuoren and Zhongren, then dispatched a courier to announce the victory. Viscount Xiang was eating when the news arrived, and his expression turned troubled. His attendants said, "In a single morning, two cities have fallen—this is cause for joy. Why does my lord appear distressed?" Viscount Xiang replied, "The greatness of the Yangtze and Yellow River lasts no more than three days; violent winds and torrential rains do not endure past morning; the sun at its zenith does not linger. Now the virtue and conduct of the Zhao house have not been cultivated over time, yet in a single morning two cities have fallen—ruin will surely overtake us." Confucius heard of this and said, "The Zhao house will flourish. For it is through concern that one flourishes, and through rejoicing that one perishes. Victory is not the difficult part; maintaining it is. A worthy ruler sustains victory in this way, and so his blessings extend to later generations. Qi, Chu, Wu, and Yue all frequently prevailed, yet ultimately met their downfall, for they did not understand how to sustain victory. Only a ruler who embodies the Dao can maintain victory."

Xin Zhi Muzi was a retainer of Viscount Xiang of Zhao. The Di were the same as the northern tribes. Zuoren and Zhongren were the names of two towns. The courier was a postal relay runner. "Violent winds and torrential rains do not endure past morning" is a saying from the *Laozi*. "The sun at its zenith does not linger" refers to the sun’s inevitable decline. The accumulation of virtue and conduct had not yet been extended to others, hence: "virtue and conduct have not been cultivated over time." Zichan said, "Without civil virtue but with military achievement"—this expresses the same idea. "Ruin will surely overtake us" means he feared arrogance would lead to defeat. Those who are able to be concerned will surely be secure; those who rejoice in themselves will surely invite disaster. Thus, achieving victory is not difficult—sustaining it is. This argument is entirely sound.

Confucius was so strong he could lift the crossbar of a city gate, yet he refused to be known for his strength. Mozi devised defensive strategies, and Gongshu Ban conceded defeat, yet Mozi refused to be known for his military skill. Thus, those who are adept at sustaining victory turn strength into weakness. "Lift" means to raise. "Refused to be known for his strength" means he was praised for his virtue, not his physical power. Gongshu Ban’s siege engines were the most refined of his time, yet he could not breach Mozi’s defenses, and was ultimately forced to submit—yet Mozi was not celebrated for his military knowledge. These two examples serve as metaphors for concealing courage in timidity and sustaining victory as if bearing a burden.

There was a man of Song who diligently practiced benevolence and righteousness for three generations without faltering. His family’s black ox unexpectedly bore a white calf, and he asked Confucius about it. Confucius said, "This is an auspicious omen; offer it in sacrifice to the Supreme Deity." A year later, the man’s father inexplicably went blind, and the ox again bore a white calf. The father once more instructed his son to ask Confucius. The son said, "Last time we asked, you lost your sight—why ask again?" The father replied, "The words of a sage may initially seem contrary but later prove fitting. The matter is not yet resolved—let us ask again." The son once more consulted Confucius, who again said, "It is auspicious," and instructed him to perform the sacrifice. The son returned and relayed the message. His father said, "Follow Confucius’ words." A year later, the son also inexplicably went blind. Later, Chu attacked Song and besieged its capital. The people resorted to exchanging children to eat and breaking bones for fuel. The able-bodied all mounted the walls to fight, and more than half perished—yet this man and his father, being afflicted, were spared. When the siege was lifted, their sight was restored. This chapter shares the same meaning as the parable of the old frontier man who gained and lost horses: fortune does not necessarily preclude misfortune, nor misfortune fortune. "Initially seem contrary but later prove fitting" means that what does not appear true at first will prove true later. "Not yet resolved" means the final outcome remains unknown.

There was a man of Song called Lanzi who sought an audience with the Lord of Yuan through his skills. The Lord of Yuan summoned him and had him demonstrate his art. Lanzi bound two long poles twice his height to his shins and ran swiftly back and forth, juggling seven swords in rapid succession, with five constantly in the air. The Lord of Yuan was astonished and immediately bestowed gold and silk upon him. Another Lanzi, skilled in acrobatics, heard of this and also sought an audience with the Lord of Yuan. The Lord of Yuan grew furious, saying, "Previously, someone with extraordinary skills sought my favor. His skills were of no use, but it so happened I was in high spirits, so I rewarded him with gold and silk. He must have heard of this and come seeking my favor again—he expects another reward." He had the man arrested and nearly executed, only releasing him after a month.

The binding of two poles to the shins is what people today call the "stilt-walking" performance. The "two poles" are two pieces of wood. Juggling seven swords with five constantly in the air—this performance also exists today. The "Yan acrobatics" refers to the skills performed amid feasting and revelry. "Skills of no use" means they were inherently without practical purpose, and he happened to reward them out of delight. "Arrested and nearly executed" means he detained and intended to punish him. Though their skills were the same, their fates differed—such is the unpredictability of circumstance.

Duke Mu of Qin said to Bo Le, "You are advanced in years. Among your descendants, is there anyone I could send to seek horses?" Bo Le replied, "A fine horse can be described by its form, appearance, sinews, and bones. But the horse of the world—it is as if vanished, as if lost, as if gone without a trace. Such a horse leaves no dust in its wake, outstrips the arrow’s flight. My sons are all of mediocre talent; they may be taught how to recognize a fine horse, but not how to recognize the horse of the world. I have a man with whom I once gathered firewood and shared greens—his name is Jiu Fang Gao. In matters of horses, he is no inferior to me. I beg you to meet him." Duke Mu received him and sent him forth to seek a horse.

After three months, he returned and reported, "I have found it—it is in Shaqiu." Duke Mu asked, "What kind of horse is it?" He replied, "A yellow mare." The duke sent men to fetch it, only to find it was a black stallion. Displeased, Duke Mu summoned Bo Le and said, "It is ruined. The man you sent to seek a horse cannot even discern its color or sex—how could he know anything of horses?"

Bo Le sighed deeply and said, "Has it truly come to this? This is why he surpasses me ten thousandfold without measure. What Jiu Fang Gao observes is the celestial mechanism: he grasps its essence and forgets its coarse form, attends to its inner nature and disregards its outward appearance. He sees what ought to be seen and overlooks what need not; he regards what should be regarded and ignores what should not. Such is the judgment of one who values the horse itself above all. When the horse arrived, it proved indeed to be the horse of the world."

*[Note: "子姓" refers to one’s descendants. "姓" derives from "生," meaning birth. The horse of the world is one that surpasses all others. "Vanished, lost, gone without a trace" describes its elusive, intangible nature—beyond form or description. "Leaves no dust" means it outpaces even the dust it raises; "outstrips the arrow’s flight" means it leaves no tracks. "Gathered firewood" refers to carrying firewood with a rope. "Surpasses me ten thousandfold without measure" means his ability exceeds mine beyond all reckoning. The "celestial mechanism" is the innate nature grasped while form is forgotten. What is seen is what heaven reveals; what is unseen—coat, color, sex—lies in the external.]*

King Zhuang of Chu asked Zhan He, "How should one govern the state?" Zhan He replied, "I am enlightened in cultivating the self but not in governing the state." The king said, "I have been entrusted with the ancestral temples and the altars of soil and grain. I wish to learn how to preserve them." Zhan He answered, "I have never heard of a well-ordered self leading to a disordered state, nor of a disordered self leading to a well-ordered state. Thus, the root lies in the self—I dare not speak of mere branches."

The king said, "Well said." This is the principle that the root of state and world lies in the self—a most excellent discourse.

The Elder of Huqiu said to Sunshu Ao, "There are three sources of resentment—do you know them?" Sunshu Ao asked, "What are they?" The elder replied, "Those with high rank are envied; those with great office are resented by their lord; those with generous emolument are beset by grievances."

Sunshu Ao said, "As my rank rises higher, my aspirations grow humbler; as my office grows greater, my heart grows smaller; as my emolument grows richer, my generosity grows wider."

"By this means, may one avoid the three resentments?" When Sunshu Ao fell ill and was near death, he admonished his son, saying: "The king has repeatedly offered to enfeoff me, but I have not accepted. When I die, the king will surely enfeoff you. You must not accept fertile land. Between Chu and Yue, there is a place called Qinqiu—this land is inauspicious, and its name is most ill-omened. The people of Chu revere ghosts, and the people of Yue are superstitious; only this place can be held in perpetuity."

After Sunshu Ao died, the king indeed offered his son fine land. The son declined and requested Qinqiu instead. It was granted to him, and to this day, his family has not lost it. The name of the town of Qinqiu resembles that of a burial ground, hence it is called "most ill-omened." "Inauspicious" means disadvantageous to the land’s lord. The people of Chu believe in ghosts and spirits, while the people of Yue favor divination and omens, abounding in taboos. They would surely despise this land and desire it no more, so that none would contend for it—thus it could be held forever. The underlying meaning is that by taking what others discard and securing what none contend for, one may preserve one’s peace.

Niu Que was a great Confucian scholar from Shangdi. Traveling to Handan, he encountered bandits in the marsh of Ōusha, who took all his clothing and carriage. Niu walked away unhurried, his countenance free of distress or regret. The bandits pursued him and asked the reason. He replied, "A noble man does not harm what he nurtures for the sake of what nurtures him." The bandits exclaimed, "Ah! How worthy he is!" Then they conferred among themselves: "With his worthiness, if he goes to see the lord of Zhao and gains influence, he will surely regard us as thorns in his side. Better to kill him." So they pursued and slew him.

When the people of Yan heard of this, they gathered their clans and warned one another: "If you meet bandits, do not be like Niu Que of Shangdi." They all heeded the lesson. Soon after, Niu Que’s younger brother traveled to Qin. At the pass, he too encountered bandits. Remembering his brother’s warning, he fought fiercely with them. When he could not prevail, he pursued them with humble pleas for his belongings. The bandits grew enraged: "We spared your life—how generous!—yet you persist in following us. Our tracks will be exposed. Since we are already bandits, where is our humanity?" They killed him and slaughtered four or five of his companions as well.

"Traveling to Handan" refers to descending from Shangdi, which is high ground, to Handan, which lies low. Ōusha is the name of a place. "Gains influence" means that if he were employed in his time, he would surely regard the bandits as obstacles. This passage suggests that misfortune does not depend on whether one is worthy or foolish—some escape it, others do not—all follows the natural order, beyond human comprehension.

The Yu family was a wealthy household of Liang. Their wealth overflowed, their money and silk were measureless, their treasures beyond reckoning. They ascended a high tower overlooking the main road, where they set out music and wine and played dice. A group of swordsmen followed them as they walked, and one of the gamblers on the tower shot an arrow, piercing a bright jade target. The dice were cast, the wooden pieces clattered, and laughter rose like fish breaking the water’s surface. Just then, a kite soaring overhead dropped a rotten rat, which struck the target. The swordsmen conferred among themselves: "The Yu family has long enjoyed wealth and pleasure, yet they have always held others in contempt. They have not wronged us, yet they have humiliated us with a rotten rat. If we do not avenge this, how can we stand among the bold knights of the world?"

"Let us join forces with one mind, lead our followers, and surely exterminate his household to set an example for our peers." All assented. On the appointed night, they gathered their forces and weapons, attacked the Yu family, and utterly destroyed their household.

*Ming qiong* refers to dice of the present kind. *Zhang zhong* means to set up the target and determine victory or defeat by whether one’s shot hits it. *Mu xi yu* is the name of a dice combination: among the five white faces, flipping two of them constitutes the *mu xi yu* throw. Liu Yi’s contest over the *xiao lu* throw belongs to this category.

Just as laughter rose on the tower, a kite in the sky happened to drop a rotten rat, striking a swordsman walking below outside the tower. Though unrelated to them, the swordsmen grew enraged and took vengeance on the Yu household—akin to the tale of weak Lu wine bringing siege to Handan, or the city gate’s fire bringing disaster to the pond’s fish: fortune and misfortune arise beyond all expectation.

*Li qin* means to establish a reputation for valor. *Deng lun* refers to the swordsmen’s peers.

In the east there was a man named Yuan Jingmu. As he traveled, he collapsed from hunger on the road. A thief from Hufu named Qiu saw him and offered a gourd of food to feed him. After three mouthfuls, Yuan Jingmu could finally see. He asked, "Who are you?" The man replied, "I am Qiu of Hufu." Yuan Jingmu said, "Bah! Are you not a thief? Why do you feed me? I will not eat your food on principle." Pressing his hands to the ground, he retched violently but could bring nothing up—*ke ke*. Then, still retching, he collapsed and died.

The man of Hufu was indeed a thief, yet the food he offered was not stolen. To judge the food as tainted merely because the giver was a thief, and thus refuse to eat—this is to confuse name and reality.

Yuan Jingmu was a man’s name. This passage conveys his scorn for the gift and his resolve to reject it, even to the point of death. When Master Zhong of Wuling retched at the sight of his brother’s goose, Mencius likewise mocked him for this same misplaced principle.

Zhu Lishu served Duke Ao of Ju, but believing the duke failed to recognize his worth, he left and dwelt by the sea. In summer he ate water caltrop and water chestnut; in winter he ate acorns. When Duke Ao faced peril, Zhu Lishu bade his friends farewell and went to die for him. His friends said, "You left because you believed he did not know you. Now you go to die for him—how is this different from being known or unknown?" Zhu Lishu replied, "Not so. I left because I believed he did not know me. Now, by dying for him, I prove he truly did not know me. I will die to shame future rulers who fail to recognize their ministers. To die for one who knows you, and not for one who does not—this is the straightforward way of the world." Zhu Lishu may be called one who, in his resentment, forgot his own life.

The case of Lang Shen in the *Zuo Tradition* follows this same principle. To resent one’s lord for not knowing one, and thus go so far as to die—this is not the straightforward way. Yet to die in order to shame future rulers who do not know their ministers—this intention is admirable.

Yang Zhu said: "Benefit given brings real reward; resentment sent invites harm in return. What is initiated here finds response abroad—this is the nature of requests. Thus the worthy are cautious in what they put forth. If I extend benefit to others, then the reality of that benefit will return to me. If I act unjustly toward others, causing them to harbor resentment against me, then harm will come to me in turn." This speaks to the principle of reciprocity.

*Wei* means "yes." When someone makes a request of me and I assent, then when I make a request of others, they too will assent. What is initiated here is the act of giving; what responds abroad is the act of repayment. To be cautious in what one puts forth means to refrain from imposing on others. This is the meaning of "what goes around comes around."

Yangzi’s neighbor lost a sheep. The man not only led his own household in pursuit but also asked Yangzi’s servant to join the chase. Yangzi said, "Hmph! Why so many chasing one sheep?" The neighbor replied, "There are many branching paths." When they returned, Yangzi asked, "Did you catch the sheep?" The man said, "It was lost." Yangzi asked, "How was it lost?"

He replied, "Among the branching paths there are yet more branches. I knew not where to go, and so I returned." Yangzi’s countenance fell into sorrow, and for a long while he did not speak; for the rest of the day he did not smile.

His disciples, perplexed, asked, "The sheep is but a lowly beast, and not even yours to lose. Why then this loss of words and mirth?"

Yangzi gave no answer, and his disciples could not fathom his intent. His student Meng Sunyang went out and told this to Xin Duzi.

Another day, Xin Duzi came with Meng Sunyang and asked, "Once there were three brothers who traveled between Qi and Lu, studying under the same master and returning home to practice the Way of benevolence and righteousness. Their father asked, ‘What is this Way of benevolence and righteousness?’

The eldest said, ‘Benevolence and righteousness make me cherish my life first, then my reputation.’
The second said, ‘Benevolence and righteousness make me sacrifice my life to achieve reputation.’
The youngest said, ‘Benevolence and righteousness make me preserve both life and reputation.’

These three methods are at odds, yet all stem from the Ru. Which is right, and which is wrong?"

Yangzi said, "There was a man who lived by the river. Skilled in water, bold in swimming, he plied a boat to ferry passengers, earning enough to feed a hundred mouths. Those who came with provisions to learn from him gathered in numbers, yet nearly half drowned. They came to learn swimming, not drowning, yet the outcome was thus. How then would you judge right and wrong?"

Xin Duzi fell silent and departed. Meng Sunyang reproached him, "Why did you ask such a roundabout question, only for the Master to answer so obliquely? Now my confusion is greater still."

Xin Duzi said, "The great Way, when riddled with branching paths, loses the sheep. The scholar, when led astray by many directions, forfeits his life. The root of learning is not different, nor is it not one, yet the branches diverge so. Only by returning to unity and reverting to oneness can one avoid loss and gain, ruin and preservation.

You have long stood at the Master’s gate, studied the Master’s Way, yet fail to grasp the Master’s sentiment—how lamentable!

Xin Duzi’s question shares the same vein as Zigong’s inquiry about Boyi and Shuqi.

*Qi* means a fork in the road. ‘Among the branching paths there are yet more branches’ describes divisions upon divisions, likening them to the disunity of learning. Yangzi’s sorrow and silence were born of feeling.

The Ru is one, yet it has three methods—this is the many branches. *Cheng tu* means a multitude; it is like saying a gathered throng. That those who came to learn swimming drowned instead illustrates how the later currents of learning often betray their origins, losing their true essence.

Xin Duzi’s silent departure showed he grasped the meaning beyond the words.

The great Way is a broad path. When it is one, the sheep is not lost; when it branches, the sheep is lost. Learning is originally the same, but when it diverges into many directions, lives are lost—this is the root being one while the branches differ.

‘Returning to unity’ means converging upon the ultimate Way and reverting to the principle of ultimate oneness, where there is neither gain nor loss.

*Kuang* means sentiment. If one does not understand the Master’s sentiment, how can one practice the Master’s Way?

This chapter unfolds through layered analogies in question and answer, a style also found in later Chan responses.

Yang Zhu’s younger brother, Yang Bu, went out wearing white clothes. When it rained, he removed his white garments and returned in black. His dog, not recognizing him, barked and rushed at him. Yang Bu grew angry and was about to strike the dog.

Yang Zhu said, "Do not strike it. You would do the same. If your dog had gone out white and returned black, how could you not be startled?"

This chapter suggests that those who do not understand the principle of ultimate oneness are seldom unaltered by external things. The dog, seeing white clothes turn black, how could it not bark? If a man saw a white dog turn black, how could he not be astonished? Seeing the outer without perceiving the inner—this is common to all.

Yang Zhu said, "To practice goodness without seeking fame, yet fame follows; fame does not seek profit, yet profit comes; profit does not seek contention, yet contention arises. Thus the noble person must be cautious in doing good."

This reflects Zhuangzi’s idea of "doing good without drawing near to fame." When fame arises, profit inevitably follows; when profit comes, contention is sure to follow. Thus, one who does good must forget the self and relinquish fame to succeed.

Once there was a man who claimed to know the Way to immortality. The Lord of Yan sent someone to learn it, but the attempt failed, and the man died. The Lord of Yan was furious and was about to punish his envoy.

A favored courtier advised, "What people fear most is death; what they value most is life. He has already lost his own life—how could he secure yours? Do not punish him."

Another man from Qi also wished to learn the Way. Hearing that the first man had died, he struck his chest in regret. Fuzi heard of this and laughed, saying, "He wished to learn immortality, yet the man is dead, and he still regrets it—he does not understand the purpose of learning."

Huzi said, "Fuzi’s words are wrong. There are those who possess a method but cannot practice it, and those who can practice it but lack the method."

A man of Wei skilled in calculation, on his deathbed, imparted his secrets to his son. The son memorized his father’s words but could not put them into practice. When others asked him, he repeated what his father had told him.

The inquirer applied his father’s words and practiced his method, matching his father’s skill without discrepancy. If so, why could the dead not transmit the art of life? Those who receive it yet fail to master it—"mastery" means swiftness; if one’s practice is not swift, they will not live to see their teacher again.

The skilled calculator excelled in mathematics. The meaning of this passage is that learning is not difficult, but practice is; knowing is not as good as doing. The analogy of the immortality teaching is most apt.

Why could the dead not transmit the art of life? It means that though the man is dead, the method of longevity he taught remains valid—only that people cannot practice it.

On the first day of the first month, the people of Handan presented doves to Jianzi, who was greatly pleased and rewarded them generously. A guest asked the reason. Jianzi said, "On New Year’s Day, I release living creatures to show my benevolence." The guest replied, "The people know you wish to release them, so they compete to capture them, and many die. If you truly wish to preserve life, it would be better to forbid them from capturing creatures in the first place. To capture and then release them—your kindness cannot compensate for the harm." Jianzi said, "You are right."
