This analogy closely reflects human nature. Many in the world today commit the same error, such as releasing fish and turtles in Gushan Lake, only for them to be sold multiple times in a single day.

The Qi nobleman Tian held a sacrificial feast in his courtyard, hosting a thousand guests. At the feast, someone presented fish and wild geese. Tian looked at them and sighed, saying, "Heaven is generous to the people. It cultivates the five grains and gives life to fish and birds for their use." The assembled guests echoed his words like an echo.

The son of the Bao family, aged twelve, was present among the guests. He stepped forward and said, "Your words are not so. Heaven and earth, the myriad things, and I are born together—we are of the same kind. Among kinds, there is no nobility or baseness; they merely dominate one another by size, wisdom, or strength, devouring each other in turn. They are not born for one another’s sake. Humans take what is edible and eat it—how could Heaven have originally created them for human use? Moreover, mosquitoes and gnats bite the skin, and tigers and wolves eat flesh—was Heaven originally creating humans for mosquitoes and gnats, or flesh for tigers and wolves?"

This passage expounds the Buddhist notion of a world where great creatures devour small ones. Its reasoning holds merit, as when humans eat chickens and chickens eat ants—things are not born for one another’s sake. Heaven does not create the hundred creatures for humanity. The analogy of mosquitoes, gnats, tigers, and wolves is apt. The phrase "非天本为蚊蚋生人" should read "岂天本为蚊蚋生人."

In Qi, there was a poor man who often begged in the marketplace. The townspeople, weary of his frequent appeals, refused to give him anything. He then went to the stables of the Tian family, where he worked under the horse-doctors and received food in return. Someone in the city mocked him, saying, "Working for the horse-doctors for food—is that not shameful?" The beggar replied, "Of all shames in the world, none surpasses begging. If begging is not shameful, how could working for the horse-doctors be?" This sentiment suggests that people occupy different stations, yet all are mired in shame without realizing it—echoing Zhuangzi’s idea of mutual deference among servants, and subtly reflecting Mencius’ reference to the beggar at the grave-mound, though less overtly.

A man from Song was traveling along a road when he found a lost contract. He took it home and hid it, secretly counting its notches. He told his neighbor, "I shall soon be rich!" The notches represented the quantities of goods listed on the contract. Rejoicing over an empty contract is an analogy for valuing hollow names without substance. Su Shi’s poem employing "urn calculations" carries the same meaning.

A man had a withered parasol tree. His elderly neighbor said, "A withered parasol tree is inauspicious," so the man promptly cut it down. The neighbor then asked to take it as firewood, at which the man grew displeased. He said, "That old neighbor only wanted firewood and urged me to cut it down. To live beside such a treacherous man—how can this be?" The warning about inauspiciousness was well-intentioned, but the request for firewood aroused suspicion of self-interest. This illustrates the difficulty of discerning worldly motives and the ambiguity between public and private interests. The analogy is most apt. The phrase "若此其险" stands alone, followed by "岂可哉" as a three-character clause.

A man lost his iron and suspected his neighbor’s son. He observed the boy’s gait, demeanor, expressions, speech, and every movement—all seemed to confirm the theft. Later, he dug in his grain cellar and found the iron. The next time he saw the neighbor’s son, none of his movements resembled a thief. This passage illustrates how suspicion breeds illusions. When the mind is doubtful, even an innocent person appears guilty in every detail. The analogy keenly captures the subtleties of human nature.

Bai Gong Sheng was plotting rebellion. After dismissing court, he stood lost in thought, leaning on his staff upside down. The iron tip of the staff pierced his chin, and blood flowed to the ground, yet he remained unaware. A man from Zheng heard of this and said, "If one can forget one’s chin, what else might one forget?" When the mind is fixated, one may stumble over roots or strike one’s head against rough wood without realizing it. Such is human nature. "倒杖策" means using the staff upside down to prod oneself. "錣" refers to the sharp end of the staff. "株" means wood, and "埳" means a pitfall. When the mind is preoccupied, even obstacles in one’s path go unnoticed—echoing *The Great Learning*: "When the mind is not present, one sees without seeing, hears without hearing."

Once, a man from Qi coveted gold. At dawn, he dressed and went to the market, where he approached a gold-seller’s stall, seized the gold, and fled. The authorities caught him and asked, "With so many people around, why did you seize another’s gold?" He replied, "When I took the gold, I saw nothing but the gold." Fixated on seizing gold, he saw no one—like one chasing beasts who fails to see Mount Tai. This shows how obsession clouds the mind.

The arguments in this chapter are sound and align with Confucian texts. The latter analogies are particularly well-crafted, and the prose differs from other sections. Among the eight chapters of this book, its authenticity is debatable. Several passages closely resemble the style of *Robber Zhi’s Discourse on Swords*, making it clear they were not written by Liezi. Though the arguments here are sound, they do not reflect Liezi’s school of thought. Only those well-versed in the various traditions can discern this.

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Oral Teachings of Yan Zhai, Volume Eight* concludes.
